Temporal Multiplicity Does Not Change God's Knowledge
Ghazali argues that temporal divisions do not imply multiplicity or change in God's eternal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the multiplicity of temporal events does not entail any change or multiplicity in God's eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Temporal diversity does not necessitate change in the eternal Knower.

Ghazali confronts a key objection: if God knows all changing events, doesn't His knowledge change? In this passage, he dismantles this objection by drawing a distinction between temporal multiplicity and genuine difference. He argues that time-based divisions alone do not imply change in the knower, preserving the simplicity and eternity of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow: he starts with a premise about a single thing's states divided by time, then asks a rhetorical question, then cites a proof about times vs species, and concludes.
Pay attention to the logical connectives like 'إذا لم يوجب' and 'فكيف يوجب'.
2
Identify the key premise
Ghazali assumes it is proven that difference of times does not necessarily cause multiplicity or difference in a thing. Find that statement.
This premise is crucial.
3
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that one eternal knowledge can encompass all temporal diversity without change in God's essence. See how he connects the premise to the conclusion.
The phrase 'جازت الإحاطة بالكل' indicates permissibility.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that God's knowledge must change with events. How does Ghazali's argument refute that?
This ties to the lesson objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك تعدداً أحوال الشيء الواحد، المنقسم بانقسام الزمان؛ وإذا لم يوجب اختلافاً، فكيف يوجب هذا تعدداً واختلافاً؟! ومهما ثبت بالبرهان، أن اختلاف الأزمان، دون اختلاف الأجناس والأنواع، وأن ذلك لم يوجب التعدد والاختلاف، فهذا أيضاً لا يوجب الاختلاف، وإذا لم يوجب الاختلاف، جازت الإحاطة بالكل، بعلم واحد، دائم في الأزل والأبد، ولا يوجب ذلك تغيراً في ذات العالم.
That is multiplicity of states of a single thing, divided by the division of time; and if it does not necessitate difference, then how does this necessitate multiplicity and difference? And whenever it is established by proof that difference of times, without difference of genera and species, and that did not necessitate multiplicity and difference, then this also does not necessitate difference. And if it does not necessitate difference, then encompassing all things by a single knowledge, eternal in pre-eternity and perpetuity, is permissible, and that does not necessitate change in the essence of the world.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about divine knowledge and temporal multiplicity. Ghazali begins by noting that the multiplicity of states of a single thing that are divided by time does not entail difference or diversity in the thing itself. He then poses a rhetorical question: if temporal difference alone does not cause multiplicity and difference, then how could it cause it here? Since it has been proven that difference of times without difference of species does not necessitate multiplicity, then the same applies. Therefore, it is permissible for one eternal knowledge to encompass all things, and that does not cause change in the essence of the Knower (or the world). The argument is that God's knowledge is timeless and unchanging despite the diversity of temporal events.

Key Terms
تعدد
اختلاف
أزمان
علم قديم
إحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because events change over time, God's knowledge of them must also change.
Correction: Ghazali argues that temporal multiplicity alone does not necessitate change in the knower; a single eternal knowledge can encompass all times without alteration.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the division of times not necessitate change in God's knowledge?
A) Because time is an illusion.
B) Because difference of times alone does not necessitate multiplicity or change in the knower.
C) Because God's knowledge is created with each event.
D) Because God does not know particulars.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that temporal multiplicity does not compel change in God's eternal knowledge, preserving divine immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face unexpected changes in your life, do you genuinely believe that God's knowledge of your situation has always been the same, or do you sometimes feel that God is 'learning' something new about you? How does Ghazali's argument challenge that feeling, and what can you do today to trust that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging?
?
If God's knowledge encompasses all times in a single eternal act, then your past sins and future struggles are already present to Him. How does this truth affect your fear of the future or regret over the past? Will you allow this reality to free you from anxiety, or will you continue to live as if God's knowledge is limited by time?
?
Ghazali says that temporal multiplicity does not necessitate change in God. But do you live as if your own actions 'inform' God or 'add' to His knowledge? For example, when you repent, do you think God's knowledge updates? How can you align your worship and reliance with the fact that God's knowledge is one, eternal, and unaffected by time?

The Eternal as Source of Originated Things
Ghazali presents objection and refutes the first horn using the example of celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the claim that an eternal being cannot be the source of originated knowledge, using the philosophers' own cosmology of circular motion.

Core Idea
The eternal can produce originated things if its state is uniform.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles the second objection: that God's knowledge of changing particulars would require change in Him. He first presents the objector's dilemma: originated knowledge must come either from God or from another. Then he dismantles the first horn by appealing to the philosophers' own acceptance that the eternal celestial soul produces originated circular motion. This lesson focuses on that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read from the beginning to 'فلامانع لكم من هذا الاعتقاد' to understand the challenge: why can't God know changing things?
Notice the references to Jahm and Karramiyya as examples of those who accept change in God.
2
Follow the philosopher's response
Read the philosopher's argument that originated knowledge must come from God or another, and both are impossible. Identify why each is deemed impossible.
The philosopher claims the eternal cannot produce an originated thing as an agent after not being an agent.
3
Examine Ghazali's counterexample
Read Ghazali's refutation: he points to the celestial soul and circular motion. Understand how this shows that an eternal can produce originated things when its state is uniform.
The key is the phrase 'إذا تشابهت أحوال القديم تشابه فيضان الحوادث منه على الدوام'.
4
Conclude the first part
Read to 'فلتكن العلوم الحادثة من هذا القبيل' and see how Ghazali applies the same logic to originated knowledges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثانى هو أن يقال: وما المانع على أصلكم من أن يعلم هذه الأمور الجزئية، وإن كان يتغير؟! وهلا اعتقدتم: أن هذا النوع من التغير، لا يستحيل عليه، كما ذهب «جهم» من «المعتزلة» إلى أن علومه بالحوادث حادثة، وكما اعتقد «الكرامية» من عند آخرهم، أنه محل للحوادث، ولم ينكر جماهير أهل الحق عليهم، إلا من حيث إن المتغير، لا يخلو عن التغير، وما لا يخلو عن التغير والحوادث، فهو حادث، وليس بقديم. وأما أنتم فمذهبكم أن العالم قديم، وأنه لا يخلو عن التغير، فإذا عقلتم قديماً متغيراً، فلامانع لكم من هذا الاعتقاد. فإن قيل: إنما أحلنا ذلك، لأن العلم الحادث في ذاته، لا يخلو: إما أن يحدث من جهته، أو من جهة غيره. وإن باطل أن يحدث منه، فإنا بينا أن القديم لا يصدر منه حادث فاعلاً بعد أن لم يكن فاعلاً، فإنه يوجب تغيراً، وقد قررناه في مسألة حدوث العالم. وإن حصل ذلك في ذاته من جهة غيره، فكيف يكون غيره مؤثراً فيه ومغيراً له، حتى تتغير أحواله، على سبيل التسخير والاضطرار من جهة غيره؟! قلنا: كل واحد من القسمين غير محال على أصلكم. أما قولكم: إنه يستحيل أن يصدر من القديم حادث هو أول الحوادث، فقد أبطلناه في تلك المسألة، كيف؟! وعندكم يستحيل أن يصدر من القديم حادث هو أول الحوادث، فشرط استحالته كونه أولاً، وإلا فهذه الحوادث ليست لها أسباب حادثة إلى غير نهاية، بل تنتهي بوساطة الحركة الدورية إلى شيء قديم هو نفس الفلك وحياته، فالنفس الفلكية قديمة، والحركة الدورية تحدث منها، وكل جزء من أجزاء الحركة يحدث وينقضي، وما بعده متجدد لا محالة، فإذن الحوادث صادرة من القديم عندكم، ولكن إذا تشابهت أحوال القديم تشابه فيضان الحوادث منه على الدوام، كما تشابهت أحوال الحركة لما أن كانت تصدر من قديم متشابه الأحوال. فاستبان أن كل فريق مبهم معترف بأنه يجوز صدور الحوادث من قديم إذا كانت تصدر على التناسب والدوام، فلتكن العلوم الحادثة من هذا القبيل. وأما القسم الثاني، وهو صدور هذا العلم فيه من غيره، فنقول: ولم يستحيل ذلك عندكم؟! وليس فيه إلا ثلاثة أمور: أحدها: التغير، قد بينا لزومه على أصلكم. والثاني: كون الغير سبباً لتغير الغير، وهو ليس بمحال عندكم، فليكن حدوث الشيء سبباً لحدوث العلم به؛ كما أنكم تقولون: تمثل الشخص المتلون بإزاء الحدقة الباصرة سبب لانطباع مثال الشخص في الطبقة الجليدية من الحدقة عند توسط الهواء المشف بين الحدقة والمبصر. فإذا جاز أن يكون جماد سبباً لانطباع الصورة في الحدقة وهو معنى الإبصار، فلم يستحيل أن يكون حدوث الحوادث سبباً لحصول علم الأول بها، فإن القوة الباصرة كما أنها مستعدة للإدراك، ويكون حصول الشخص
The second objection is that it is said: And what prevents you, according to your principle, from knowing these particular things, even if it changes? And why did you not believe that this type of change is not impossible for Him, as Jahm of the Mu`tazila went to the view that His knowledges of events are originated, and as the Karramiyya believed from their last, that He is a locus for events, and the masses of the people of truth did not deny them, except insofar as that which changes is not free from change, and that which is not free from change and events is originated, not eternal. But as for you, your doctrine is that the world is eternal, and that it is not free from change, so if you have understood an eternal that changes, there is no obstacle for you from this belief. So if it is said: We deemed that impossible only because the originated knowledge in His essence is either that it arises from Him or from another. And it is false that it arises from Him, for we have shown that the eternal does not produce an originated thing as an agent after not being an agent, for that entails change, and we have established this in the problem of the origination of the world. And if it occurs in His essence from another, then how can another be effective in Him and changing Him, so that His states change, by way of subjugation and compulsion from another? We say: Each of the two divisions is not impossible according to your principles. As for your saying: It is impossible for an originated thing that is the first of originated things to issue from the eternal, we have refuted this in that problem. How? And according to you it is impossible for an originated thing that is the first of originated things to issue from the eternal, so the condition of its impossibility is its being first. Otherwise, these events have no originated causes ad infinitum, but they end by means of circular motion to an eternal thing, which is the celestial soul and its life. Thus the celestial soul is eternal, and circular motion arises from it, and every part of motion arises and passes away, and what comes after it is renewed inevitably. So then events proceed from the eternal according to you, but when the states of the eternal are uniform, the outpouring of events from it is uniform perpetually, just as the states of motion were uniform when they proceeded from an eternal of uniform states. So it became clear that every obscure party admits that it is permissible for events to proceed from an eternal if they proceed proportionally and perpetually. So let the originated knowledges be of this kind. As for the second division, which is the occurrence of this knowledge in Him from another, we say: And why is that impossible according to you? And there are only three matters: One of them: change, we have shown its necessity according to your principle. The second: that another is a cause for the change of another, and that is not impossible according to you. So let the occurrence of a thing be a cause for the occurrence of knowledge of it; just as you say: the representation of a colored person before the seeing pupil is a cause for the impression of the person's image in the icy layer of the pupil, upon the mediation of the transparent air between the pupil and the seen object. So if it is permissible that an inanimate object be a cause for the impression of the image in the pupil, which is the meaning of sight, then why is it impossible that the occurrence of events be a cause for the acquisition of knowledge of them by the First? For the power of sight, as it is prepared for perception, and the acquisition of the person
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and Ghazali's refutation. First, the objector asks: Why cannot God know changing particulars? You (philosophers) claim the world is eternal and subject to change, so you should accept that God knows changing things. The philosophers respond that originated knowledge in God would require change, which is impossible because either it arises from God Himself (but that would imply change in the eternal), or from another (which would mean another influences God). Ghazali refutes both: regarding the first, he says the philosophers themselves accept that originated things arise from the eternal (e.g., circular motion from the celestial soul). So if an originated thing can arise from an eternal with uniform state, then originated knowledge can too. Regarding the second, he argues that change due to another is not impossible for them; they accept that an inanimate object causes perception. So similarly, the occurrence of events can be a cause for God's knowledge of them. He lists three matters, but only two are given in the text.

اعتراض
علم حادث
قديم
تغير
الحركة الدورية
نفس فلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies divine knowledge of particulars.
Correction: Ghazali affirms it; he is defending it against philosophical objections.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali use to show that an eternal can produce originated things?
A) The celestial soul and circular motion
B) The creation of the world in six days
C) The burning of fire
D) The human soul's knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the first horn by showing that the philosophers' own cosmology accepts originated things from an eternal, so divine originated knowledge is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because God is unchanging, He cannot be intimately involved in the details of your changing life? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what would change if you truly believed God knows every fleeting thought and situation?
?
Ghazali argues that the philosophers' denial of divine knowledge was based on an incomplete logic—they accepted the celestial soul producing continuous change but denied it for God. What assumptions about God have you held that might be based on incomplete reasoning? Are you willing to re-examine them with this passage in mind?
?
If God's knowledge includes every originated event—including your daily struggles and sins—and He remains unchanged, does that give you comfort or discomfort? How does this affect your relationship with Him, especially in moments when you feel distant or forgotten?

Knowledge from Another: The Analogy of Sight
Ghazali defends the possibility of God's knowledge arising from events, using the analogy of visual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the possibility of God's knowledge arising from external events, using the analogy of the eye perceiving an object.

Core Idea
Events can cause knowledge in God just as objects cause perception in the eye.

Having dealt with the first division, Ghazali now tackles the second: that originated knowledge in God could come from another (i.e., from events themselves). The philosophers objected that this would mean another being influences God. Ghazali argues that this is not impossible on their own principles, and he illustrates with the example of sight: an inanimate object causes an image in the eye. This lesson unpacks that analogy and Ghazali's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division
Read from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to the end of the passage. Identify the three matters Ghazali mentions (though only two are given in the text).
The text lists only two of the three matters explicitly.
2
Understand the objection
The philosophers claim that if knowledge comes from another, that other would be changing God, which seems problematic. Ghazali asks why that is impossible on their principles.
He notes that change and causality are accepted in their system.
3
Study the analogy
Ghazali compares the occurrence of events causing knowledge to the representation of a colored object causing an image in the eye. Notice how he describes the process.
He uses their own theory of vision to make his point.
4
Apply the analogy
Ghazali concludes that if an inanimate object can be a cause for perception, then events can be a cause for God's knowledge. Understand the parallel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Reply to the Objection that Subjugation Implies Imperfection
Ghazali responds to the claim that subjugation would be imperfection in the First.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God's knowledge of events is a perfection, not a sign of subjugation.

Core Idea
Knowing all events is a perfection, not a deficiency.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses an objection from the philosophers who claim that if God's knowledge depends on events, that would imply subjugation and imperfection. The objector argues that God's perfection lies in being the source of all things, not in being affected. Ghazali responds by redefining perfection: It is God's perfection to know all things, just as it would be a perfection for us to know every event. He thus turns the objection on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of objection and reply.
2
Identify the objection
What is the opponent's claim? They say that being a source of all is perfection, not subjugation.
The objector uses the notion of 'source' to argue against compulsion.
3
Identify Ghazali's reply
Ghazali argues that knowledge of all things is perfection, using an analogy to human knowledge.
He shifts the definition of perfection from being a source to knowing all.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Ask yourself how the analogy strengthens Ghazali's case and why it refutes the objection.
Consider: if knowing all events is perfection for us, why not for God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إن ذلك ليس باضطرار ، لأن كماله في أن يكون مصدراً الجميع الأشياء قلنا : فهذا ليس بتسخير ، فإن كماله في أن يعلم جميع الأشياء ، ولو حصل لنا علم مقارن لكل حادث ، لكان ذلك كمالا لنا ، لا نقصاناً وتسخيراً . فليكن كذلك في حقه
If it is said: That is not compulsion, because His perfection is that He be source of all things. We say: This is not subjugation, for His perfection is that He know all things. Even if we had a knowledge concomitant with every event, that would be a perfection for us, not a deficiency and subjugation. So let it be likewise in His case.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair. The objector claims that the previous characterization of God as subjugated is false because God's perfection lies in being the source of all things, not in being affected by them. Ghazali replies that if God's perfection is to be the source of all things, it is also His perfection to know all things, and that does not imply subjugation. He draws an analogy: if we humans had knowledge concomitant with every event, that would be a perfection for us, not a deficiency. Similarly for God, knowing all events is a perfection, not subjugation.

Key Terms
كمال
تسخير
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is affirming that God gains new knowledge of contingent events, implying change in God.
Correction: Ghazali is not asserting that God acquires new knowledge; he is arguing that the objection's characterization of such knowledge as deficiency is mistaken. The discussion is about the logical implication of the philosophers' own premises.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how should we view the acquisition of knowledge of events by the First?
A) As a sign of subjugation.
B) As a perfection.
C) As a necessity.
D) As an impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses an analogy to human knowledge to argue that God's knowledge of all events is a perfection, not a deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God knows every detail of your life—including your secret sins and your most private thoughts. Does that knowledge feel like a burden that exposes your flaws, or does it make you feel completely known and perfectly loved? What would change in the way you live today if you truly internalized that this knowledge is God's perfection, not your condemnation?
?
If you had a friend who knew absolutely everything about you—every success, every failure, every hidden intention—would you feel subjugated by that friend, or would you feel safe and deeply cared for? What does your honest answer reveal about how you actually view God's omniscience?
?
Tonight, when you lay your head on your pillow and review the day's events, consider that God knew every moment of it before it happened and that His knowledge of it is a perfection for Him. How does that change your perspective on the mistakes you made? Are you willing to see your own story as part of a perfect divine knowledge that redeems, not shames?

The Eternal Can Change: Refuting the Philosophers' Objection
Ghazali argues that change in the eternal is not impossible on the philosophers' own terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' own principles allow for change in the eternal First Cause, undermining their objection.

Core Idea
Change in God does not undermine His eternality.

In this passage, Ghazali turns the philosophers' own logic against them. They claim that the First Cause cannot acquire new knowledge because that would imply change. But Ghazali points out that if the First Cause's essence can become receptive to knowledge (as they might accept), then change is not impossible. Moreover, their very proof for the Necessary Existence relies on cutting the chain of causes, which could allow a changing eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the first part of the passage from 'المتلون' to 'بقديم متغير'. Notice how Ghazali starts by referencing the philosophers' own concept of removing barriers.
2
Identify the core claim and counterclaim
The philosophers claim that knowledge of events would change the eternal. Ghazali responds that if the essence becomes receptive, change is not impossible. Also, their proof for the Necessary Existence does not preclude a changing eternal.
Ghazali is not affirming change in God; he is showing that the philosophers have no grounds to deny it.
3
See how Ghazali uses their own premises
Ghazali uses the philosophers' principle that cutting the chain of causes can yield a changing eternal, which he claims to have demonstrated earlier.
This is a dialectical move—he adopts their framework to defeat them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتلون ، مع ارتفاع الحواجز ، سبباً لحصول الإدراك ، فلتكن ذات المبدأ الأول ، عندكم مستعدة لقبول العلم ، ويخرج من القوة إلى الفعل ، بوجود ذلك الحادث ، فإن كان فيه تغير القديم، فالقديم المتغير عندكم غير مستحيل . وإن زعمتم أن ذلك يستحيل في واجب الوجود ، فليس لكم على إثبات واجب الوجود ، دليل ، إلا قطع سلسلة العلل والمعلولات ، كما سبق ، وقد بينا أن قطع التسلسل ممكن ، بقديم متغير. والأمر الثالث : الذي يتضمنه هذا ، هو كون القديم متغيراً بغيره وأن ذلك يشبه التسخير ، واستيلاء الغير عليه ، فيقال : ولم يستحيل عندكم هذا ؟ وهو أن يكون هو سبباً لحدوث الحوادث بوسائط ، ثم يكون حدوث الحوادث ، سبباً لحصول العلم له بها ، فكأنه هو السبب في تحصيل العلم لنفسه ، ولكن بالوسائط. وقولكم : إن ذلك يشبه التسخير ، فليكن كذلك ، فإنه لائق بأصلكم أن ما إذ زعمتم يصدر من الله تعالى إنما يصدر على سبيل اللزوم والطبع ولا قدرة له على أن لا يفعل ، وهذا أيضاً يشبه نوعاً من التسخير ، ويشير إلى أنه كالمضطر ، في ما يصدر منه. فإن قيل : إن ذلك ليس باضطرار، لأن كماله في أن يكون مصدراً الجميع الأشياء قلنا : فهذا ليس بتسخير ، فإن كماله في أن يعلم جميع الأشياء ، ولو حصل لنا علم مقارن لكل حادث ، لكان ذلك كمالا لنا ، لا نقصاناً وتسخيراً . فليكن كذلك في حقه.
The changing (al-mutalawwin), with the removal of barriers, is a cause for obtaining perception. So let the essence of the First Principle (dhāt al-mabda' al-awwal), according to you, be receptive to acquiring knowledge, and it emerges from potentiality to actuality by the existence of that accident. If that involves change in the eternal (al-qadīm), then the changing eternal is not impossible according to you. And if you claim that this is impossible for the Necessary Existence (wājib al-wujūd), then you have no proof for establishing the Necessary Existence except by cutting the chain of causes and effects (silsilat al-'ilal wa al-ma'lūlāt), as previously, and we have shown that cutting the chain is possible with a changing eternal. And the third matter: which this contains is that the eternal is changed by another (al-qadīm mutaghayyiran bi-ghayrihī), and that this resembles subjugation (tashkheer) and domination by another over it. So it is said: why is this impossible according to you? That is, that He Himself is the cause of the occurrence of events through intermediaries, then the occurrence of events becomes the cause for the attainment of knowledge of them for Him, as if He is the cause of acquiring knowledge for Himself, but through intermediaries. And your saying: that this resembles subjugation—let it be so, for it is befitting of your principle that you claim that what emanates from God Almighty only emanates by way of necessity and nature (al-luzūm wa al-tab'), and He has no power not to do it. This also resembles a kind of subjugation and indicates that He is like one compelled in what emanates from Him. If it is said: that is not compulsion (idṭirār), because His perfection consists in being the source of all things, we say: then this is not subjugation, because His perfection consists in knowing all things. If we attained knowledge concomitant with every event, that would be a perfection for us, not a deficiency or subjugation. So let it be so in His case.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is part of a dialectical argument where Ghazali responds to the philosophers' objection that if the First Cause acquires knowledge of events after they occur, that would imply change and subjugation in the eternal. Ghazali first argues that if the philosophers accept that the First Cause can be receptive to knowledge through the removal of barriers (a concept they might accept), then the eternal can change, and they have no proof against that. He then addresses the claim that such dependence resembles subjugation: he turns the tables by noting that the philosophers' own view—that God acts by necessity and cannot refrain—itself resembles subjugation and compulsion. Finally, he counters the objection that compulsion is not at issue because God's perfection lies in being the source of all things; Ghazali retorts that knowledge of all events is also a perfection, so it is not subjugation.

Key Terms
المتلون
المبدأ الأول
القديم
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali believes that God actually changes.
Correction: Ghazali is using a hypothetical argument: he is not affirming change in God, but showing that the philosophers have no rational basis to deny it given their own principles.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the philosophers' only proof for the Necessary Existence?
A) The argument from design
B) The ontological argument
C) Cutting the chain of causes and effects
D) The argument from contingency

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' objection that divine knowledge implies change fails because they have no proof against a changing eternal, and their own proof for God does not preclude it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided examining a contradiction in your own beliefs because it might be uncomfortable? What would it cost you to face it honestly today? Commit to writing down one inconsistency between your actions and your claim to believe in God's attributes, and take one step to resolve it this week.
?
When you hear an objection against a divine attribute, do you accept it without considering whether the objector's own principles lead to a contradiction? This lesson shows that even philosophical objections can be turned on their head. Think of one doubt you have had about God's knowledge or power. Can you identify a hidden assumption in that doubt that also undermines the objector's position? If not, does that mean the doubt is valid?
?
Ghazali uses the opponent's tools against them. Are you willing to learn the arguments of those who disagree with you in order to refute them? What prevents you from doing so? Set a goal to study one argument against a core Islamic belief with the intent to understand it and find its weakness.

Knowledge as Perfection: Subjugation Refuted
Ghazali refutes the subjugation objection by reversing it and arguing knowledge is perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's twofold argument that God's knowledge of events through intermediaries is not subjugation but a perfection.

Core Idea
Knowledge of all events is a perfection, not a deficiency.

The philosophers object that if the First Cause acquires knowledge from events, He is subjugated by them. Ghazali counters with a powerful twofold response. First, he turns the tables: the philosophers' own view that God acts by necessity and cannot refrain is itself a form of subjugation and compulsion. Second, he argues that knowledge of all things is a perfection; if it were a perfection for us, it must be even more so for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'والأمر الثالث' to the end. Notice how Ghazali first states the philosophers' objection and then responds.
2
Analyze Ghazali's tu quoque argument
The philosophers claim that God's dependence on events for knowledge is subjugation. Ghazali retorts that their own view of necessary emanation implies that God is compelled, which is itself a kind of subjugation.
This is a classic dialectical move: if subjugation is problematic, your view has it too.
3
Understand the perfection argument
Ghazali then counters the reply that compulsion is not subjugation because God's perfection is to be the source of all things. He says: similarly, knowledge of all things is a perfection, not a deficiency. Thus, it is not subjugation.
This argument appeals to the intrinsic value of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Motion by Divine Coercion: Ghazali's Defense
Ghazali defends the second alternative and refutes the specification objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the second alternative—that motion occurs by divine coercion—and how he turns the philosophers' specification objection back on them through infinite regress.

Core Idea
Divine will specifies without cause; any attempt to explain specification leads to infinite regress.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' theory of celestial motion by presenting a second possibility: that the motion of the heavens is coerced, directly created by God. He anticipates the philosophers' objection—why would God's will attach specifically to the heaven and not to other bodies?—and delivers a powerful reply. By insisting that the eternal will has the inherent power to specify, and by showing that the philosophers' own explanation leads to an infinite regress, Ghazali forces them to admit a principle of specification that ultimately aligns with divine will. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: a claim, an objection, and a two-part response. Let the argument flow before analyzing.
The passage is dense; allow yourself to get the gist first.
2
Identify the philosophers' objection
Find where Ghazali states the philosophers' objection: they question why the will would be specific to the heaven when all bodies share corporeality. Understand that the burden of proof is on Ghazali.
3
Analyze Ghazali's first response
Ghazali first reiterates that the eternal will has the property of specifying without cause, and that the philosophers themselves accept this kind of specification in other contexts (e.g., direction of motion, position of poles).
This is a reminder of earlier arguments.
4
Analyze Ghazali's second response (the reversal)
Ghazali turns the objection back: if the philosophers claim the heaven has a special quality, ask why it has that quality. Any explanation leads to an infinite regress, forcing them to admit a principle of specification—which is divine will.
This is the core of the argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice how Ghazali concludes that the philosophers are compelled to affirm will as the ultimate specifier. See how this defense strengthens the second alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : هو نقول : حركة الحجر إلى أسفل أيضاً قسرية تحدث بخلق الله تعالى فيه الحركة ، وكذا القول فى سائر حركات الأجسام ، التي ليست حيوانية فيبقى استبعادهم ، أن الإرادة لم اختصت به ؟ وسائر الأجسام تشاركها في الجسمية . فقد بينا ، أن الإرادة القديمة، من شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، وأنهم مضطرون إلى إثبات صفة هذا شأنها ، فى تعيين جهة الحركة الدورية ، وفى تعيين موضع القطب والنقطة ، فلا نعيده . والقول الوجيز ، أن ما استبعدوه في اختصاص الجسم ، بتعلق الإرادة به من غير تميز بصفة ، ينقلب عليهم ، في تميزه بتلك الصفة ، فإنا نقول : ولم تميز جسم السماء بتلك الصفة ، التي بها فارق غيره من الأجسام ، وسائر الأجسام أيضاً أجسام ، فلم حصل فيه ، ما لم يحصل في غيره ؟ فإن علل ذلك بصفة أخرى ، توجه السؤال فى الصفة الأخرى ، وهكذا ، يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية فيضطرون بالآخرة ، إلى الحكم بالإرادة ، وأن في المبادئ ما يميز الشيء عن مثله ، ويخصصه بصفة عن أمثالها .
The second: we say that the motion of the stone downward is also coerced, occurring by God's creation of motion in it, and likewise for all motions of bodies that are not animal. So their objection remains: why did the will become specific to it (the heaven) while other bodies share with it in corporeality? We have already explained that the eternal will has the property of specifying a thing from its like, and that they are compelled to affirm a quality with this property in specifying the direction of circular motion and in specifying the position of the pole and the point, so we will not repeat it. The concise statement is that what they deemed improbable regarding the specification of a body by the attachment of will to it without distinction by a quality turns back upon them regarding its distinction by that quality. For we say: Why was the body of the heaven distinguished by that quality through which it differs from other bodies, while other bodies are also bodies? So why did it attain what others did not? If they explain that by another quality, the question applies to that other quality, and so on in infinite regress, until they are finally compelled to affirm will and that in the principles there is something that distinguishes a thing from its like and specifies it with a quality from its peers.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents the second alternative and Ghazali's response. The second possibility is that the motion of the heavens, like the falling of a stone, is coerced—it occurs because God creates motion in the body. The philosophers object that if motion is directly from God's will, why would that will specifically attach to the heaven and not to other bodies? Since all bodies share corporeality, there is no basis for specification. Ghazali replies with a two-part argument. First, he reiterates the earlier proof that the eternal will has the property of specifying one thing from its like without a differentiating quality; the philosophers themselves are forced to affirm such a specifying attribute when they explain why the circular motion occurs in a specific direction or why the poles are in particular positions. Second, he turns the objection back: if the philosophers claim the heaven is distinguished by a specific quality (e.g., being the cause of its motion), then that quality itself needs an explanation. Why does the heaven have that quality and not other bodies? If they explain it by another quality, an infinite regress arises. Ultimately, they must admit a principle of specification, which is essentially divine will. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
قسرية
إرادة قديمة
تخصيص
صفة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will must be based on a reason or quality to attach to a particular thing.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the eternal will has the property of specifying without any cause or quality; otherwise, an infinite regress results, and the philosophers themselves accept such unconditional specification in other cases.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, what is the consequence of the philosophers' attempt to explain the heaven's specification by a quality?
A) It proves the heaven is unique.
B) It leads to an infinite regress.
C) It shows the will has a cause.
D) It confirms the philosophers' theory.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends divine coercion by showing that the philosophers' own logic forces them to accept an unconditional principle of specification, which is the divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something happens in your life that feels random or unfair, do you instinctively demand a reason from God? Ghazali insists that God's will needs no cause. Can you sincerely trust His will without needing to understand the reason? What would it take for you to submit to that kind of faith today?
?
You admit that God can specify without cause in the heavens, but do you live as if He also specifies without cause in your daily difficulties? Identify one area where you have been insisting on a 'reason' from God. Will you release that demand now and accept His will as final?

First Approach: Refutation Through Divine Will
Ghazali presents the first approach of his refutation, asserting divine will as the basis for miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that miracles are possible because God acts by will, not by necessity.

Core Idea
God's will is the direct cause of all events; miracles are just His will acting without the usual means.

Ghazali begins his refutation by going straight to the heart of the matter: the nature of divine action. The philosophers claimed that the celestial principles act by necessity, not choice, and that God does not act by will. Ghazali rejects this outright, reminding the reader that he has already proven—in the discussion on the world's creation—that God is a free agent. From this foundation, he builds his case: if God creates burning by His will when fire meets cotton, then it is perfectly within His power to withhold burning even when contact occurs. This short passage sets the stage for the entire debate on miracles and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the confident opening: 'we do not concede.' Ghazali is not arguing for probability; he is asserting a fundamental premise.
Observe the reference to a prior refutation—this shows the interconnectedness of his arguments.
2
Identify the key premise
Ghazali says he has already refuted the philosophers' claim that God does not act by will. What is that refutation based on? (Refer back to the earlier lesson on the world's creation if known.)
3
Understand the logical move
If God creates burning by will at the moment of contact, then He can also choose not to create burning. The possibility of a miracle follows directly from the fact that God is a free agent.
This is the core of the argument: possibility, not probability.
4
Appreciate the shift in burden of proof
Ghazali is not proving that miracles happen, only that they are not impossible. The philosophers must show that God's action is necessary, not free. By denying that, Ghazali opens the door.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب له مسلكان : الأول ، أن نقول : لا نسلم أن المبادئ ، ليست تفعل بالاختيار ، وأن الله تعالى لا يفعل بالإرادة ، وقد فرغنا من إبطال دعواهم ، في ذلك ، في مسألة حدوث العالم ، وإذا ثبت أن الفاعل يخلق الاحتراق بإرادته ، عند ملاقاة القطنة النار ، أمكن فى العقل ، ألا يخلق الاحتراق ، مع وجود الملاقاة .
And the answer has two approaches: The first: we say: we do not concede that the principles do not act by choice, and that God Almighty does not act by will. And we have already completed the refutation of their claim in that matter in the question of the origination of the world. And if it is established that the agent creates burning by His will when the cotton contacts the fire, it is rationally possible that He does not create burning despite the existence of the contact.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's first approach to answering the philosophers' denial of the miracle. He declares that he does not accept the premise that the principles act without choice or that God does not act by will. He reminds that this claim was already refuted in the earlier discussion on the creation of the world (i.e., his proof that the world is created and that God is a free agent). Given that the agent (God) creates burning by His will at the moment of contact, it is rationally possible that He does not create burning even though the cotton touches the fire. Thus the miracle is not impossible.

Key Terms
اختيار
إرادة
إحداث العالم
احتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali claims that fire does not burn at all, denying its normal effect.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that normally fire burns, but he denies that this is necessary. Burning is caused by God's will, so He can suspend it.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali claim he has already refuted in the question of the world's creation?
A) That the world is eternal
B) That God acts without will
C) That the philosophers' principles are generous
D) That fire is necessary for burning

Key Takeaway
Miracles are rationally possible because God acts by will, not by necessity; He can always choose to create or not create the usual effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every outcome in your life—even the most predictable—happens only because God wills it? If you were diagnosed with an incurable disease, would you accept that God could heal you without medicine just as easily as with it? What keeps you from treating God's will as the only real cause?
?
Think of a situation right now where you feel stuck or helpless because 'that's just how things work'. Are you assuming a necessity that God could break? What would change in your heart if you prayed expecting a miracle instead of just going through the motions?
?
If God's will is the direct cause of all events, then your failures, your habits, and even your sins are not forced by circumstances. How does this truth hold you accountable? Will you live today as if God's will alone determines your success, or will you hide behind 'it was inevitable'?

5 min
Defining the Impossible
Ghazali defines impossibility as logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of impossibility as a logical contradiction and why it does not limit divine power.

Core Idea
The impossible is not a limit on God's power but a logical contradiction.

An objection claims that if God can do everything, absurdities follow. Ghazali's response begins by defining what 'impossible' truly means. This definition becomes the key to resolving the entire objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first paragraph describing what the objector thinks God could do if power were unlimited. Notice the examples: creating will without knowledge, moving a dead body, etc.
The objector is using exaggeration to make a point.
2
Identify Ghazali's definition
In the second paragraph, find the sentence that defines 'impossible'. Ghazali gives three forms of logical contradiction. Write them down.
3
Understand the implication
Notice that Ghazali says whatever is not impossible is within divine power. This shows he is not limiting God but clarifying what can be attributed to God.
The distinction between necessary and impossible is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستدعى وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ؛ ويقولون : إن الله تعالى يقدر على خلق إرادة من غير علم بالمراد ، وخلق علم من غير حياة ، ويقدر على أن يحرك يد ميت ، ويقعده ، ويكتب بيده مجلدات ، ويتعاطى صناعات ، وهو مفتوح العين محدق بصره نحوه ، ولكنه لا يرى ولا حياة فيه ، ولا قدرة له عليه ، وإنما هذه الأفعال المنظومة يخلقها الله تعالى مع تحريك يده ، والحركة من جهة الله تعالى . وبتجويز هذا ، يبطل الفرق بين الحركة الاختيارية وبين الرعدة فلا يدل الفعل المحكم على العلم ولا على قدرة الفاعل . وينبغي أن يقدر على قلب الأجناس ، فيقلب الجوهر عرضاً ، ويقلب العلم قدرة ، والسواد بياضاً ، والصوت رائحة ، كما اقتدر على قلب الجماد حيواناً ، والحجر ذهباً ، ويلزم عليه أيضاً من المحالات ما لا له حصر .
والجواب : أن المحال غير مقدور عليه ، والمحال إثبات الشيء مع نفيه ، أو إثبات الأخص مع نفي الأعم ، أو إثبات الاثنين مع نفي الواحد ، وما لا يرجع إلى هذا فليس بمحال ، وما ليس بمحال فهو مقدور .
أما الجمع بين السواد والبياض فمحال ، لأنا نفهم من إثبات صورة السواد في المحل نفي ماهية البياض ووجود السواد ، فإذا صار نفي البياض مفهوماً من إثبات السواد ، كان إثبات البياض مع نفيه محالاً .
وإنما لا يجوز كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين ، لأنا نفهم من كونه في البيت عدم كونه في غير البيت ، فلا يمكن تقديره في غير البيت كونه في البيت ، المفهم لنفيه عن غير البيت .
وكذلك تفهم من الإرادة طلب معلوم ، فإن فرض طلب ولا علم لم يكن إرادة فكان فيه نفي ما فهمناه .
والجماد يستحيل أن يخلق فيه العلم ، لأنا نفهم من الجماد ما لا يدرك ، فإن خلق فيه إدراك فتسميته جماداً بالمعنى الذي فهمناه محال ، وإن لم يدرك فتسمية الحادث علماً ولا يدرك به محله شيئاً محال ، فهذا وجه استحالته .
وأما قلب الأجناس فقد قال بعض المتكلمين : إنه مقدور لله تعالى . فنقول : مصير الشيء شيئاً آخر غير معقول ، لأن السواد إذا انقلب كدرة مثلاً فالسواد باق أم لا ؟ ! فإن كان معدوماً فلم ينقلب ، بل عدم ذاك ووجد غيره ، وإن كان موجوداً مع الكدرة فلم ينقلب ولكن انضاف إليه غيره ، وإن بقي السواد والكدرة معدومة فلم ينقلب بل بقي على ما هو عليه .
وإذا قلنا : انقلب الدم منياً أردنا به أن تلك المادة بعينها خلعت صورة ولبست صورة أخرى ، فرجع الحاصل إلى أن صورة عدمت وصورة حدثت وثم مادة قائمة تعاقبت عليها الصورتان . وإذا قلنا : انقلب الماء هواء بالتسخين أردنا به أن المادة القابلة لصورة المائية خلعت هذه الصورة وقبلت صورة أخرى فالمادة مشتركة والصورة متغيرة .
وكذلك إذا قلنا : انقلبت العصا ثعباناً والتراب حيواناً . وليس بين العرض والجوهر مادة مشتركة ولا بين السواد والكدرة ولا بين سائر الأجناس مادة مشتركة فكان هذا محالاً من هذا الوجه .
It requires that the existence of one necessitates the existence of the other; and they say: Verily Allah Almighty is able to create will without knowledge of the willed object, and to create knowledge without life, and He is able to move the hand of a dead person, and to seat him, and to write volumes with his hand, and to engage in crafts, while his eye is open, his gaze fixed toward it, but he does not see, and there is no life in him, nor power for him over it, and rather these well-ordered actions, Allah Almighty creates them with the movement of his hand, and the movement is from Allah Almighty. And by allowing this, the distinction between voluntary movement and tremor is invalidated, so the well-ordered action does not indicate knowledge nor the power of the agent. And it should be possible to change the kinds, so that He turns substance into accident, and turns knowledge into power, and blackness into whiteness, and sound into smell, just as He had power to turn an inanimate object into an animal, and stone into gold, and also necessitated from it of impossibilities that which cannot be enumerated.
The answer: That the impossible is not within power, and the impossible is affirming a thing along with its negation, or affirming the more specific while negating the more general, or affirming two while negating one, and whatever does not reduce to this is not impossible, and whatever is not impossible is within power.
As for combining blackness and whiteness, it is impossible, because we understand from affirming the form of blackness in the substrate the negation of the quiddity of whiteness and the existence of blackness, so when the negation of whiteness is understood from the affirmation of blackness, affirming whiteness along with its negation becomes impossible.
And the reason a single person cannot be in two places is because we understand from his being in the house his not being outside the house, so it is impossible to suppose him outside the house while being in the house, the latter implying his negation from outside the house.
Similarly, from will we understand the seeking of something known, so if seeking is assumed without knowledge, it would not be will, and thus it would involve the negation of what we understood.
And it is impossible that knowledge be created in an inanimate object, because we understand from 'inanimate object' that which does not perceive, so if perception is created in it, calling it 'inanimate' in the sense we understand is impossible; and if it does not perceive, then calling the occuring thing 'knowledge' while the substrate perceives nothing by it is impossible. So this is the aspect of its impossibility.
As for the changing of kinds, some theologians have said that it is within the power of Allah Almighty. We say: The becoming of a thing into another thing is unintelligible, because if blackness changes into muddiness, for example, does the blackness remain or not? If it becomes non-existent, then it did not change, but rather that ceased to exist and something else came to be; if it exists along with muddiness, then it did not change, but something else was added to it; and if the blackness remains and the muddiness is non-existent, then it did not change, but remained as it is.
And when we say: blood changed into semen, we mean by it that that same matter cast off one form and put on another form, so the result returns to: a form ceased and a form came to be, and there was an enduring substrate upon which the two forms alternated. And when we say: water changed into air by heating, we mean by it that the matter receptive to the form of water cast off this form and accepted another form, so the matter is shared and the form changes.
Similarly, when we say: the staff changed into a serpent, and dust into an animal. But there is no shared matter between substance and accident, nor between blackness and muddiness, nor between the other kinds, so this is impossible from this aspect.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's defense of divine power against an objection that if God can do everything, then absurdities follow. The objection first lists extreme possibilities: God could create will without knowledge of the willed object, knowledge without life, move a dead body to perform complex actions while it sees nothing, etc. If such things are possible, then there is no distinction between voluntary movement and an involuntary tremor; an orderly act would not prove the agent's knowledge or power. Further, it would require the ability to change kinds (قلب الأجناس), e.g., turning a substance into an accident, knowledge into power, black into white, sound into smell, just as God turned an inanimate object into an animal or stone into gold. This would imply endless impossibilities.

Ghazali's answer begins by defining the impossible (المحال): it is that which is not within divine power (غير مقدور عليه). The impossible reduces to logical contradiction: affirming a thing with its negation, affirming the more specific while negating the more general, or affirming two while negating one. Whatever does not reduce to this is not impossible and therefore is within divine power.

He then applies this definition to specific examples:
- Combining blackness and whiteness in the same subject is impossible because affirming blackness inherently negates whiteness.
- A single person being in two places is impossible because being in one place entails not being elsewhere.
- Will without knowledge is impossible because will by definition is a seeking of something known; without knowledge, the seeking is not will.
- Creating knowledge in an inanimate object is impossible because 'inanimate' is defined as lacking perception. If perception is created, it is no longer inanimate in that sense; if perception is not created, the occuring event cannot be called knowledge.

Finally, he discusses the claim by some theologians that changing kinds (قلب الأجناس) is within God's power. Ghazali argues that such change is unintelligible. He analyzes what 'change' means: if blackness becomes muddiness, either blackness remains (then no change), or perishes (then it is not a change but replacement). True change involves a common substrate (مادة) that persists while forms alternate, as in blood turning to semen (same matter, new form) or water to air (common matter receptive to both forms). However, between substance and accident, blackness and muddiness, and other kinds, there is no common substrate; hence such a transformation is impossible by this reasoning. Thus, the objection's claim is answered: the alleged impossibilities are either contradictions or lack the basis for genuine change, and do not impugn divine power since they are intrinsically impossible.

Key Terms
محال (impossible)
قدرة (power)
الجمع بين النقيضين (combination of opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is limiting God's power by saying some things are impossible.
Correction: Ghazali is defining impossibility in terms of logical contradiction, not imposing arbitrary limits. The impossible is not a real thing but a conceptual absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's definition, which of the following is an example of the impossible?
A) Creating a rock too heavy for God to lift.
B) Affirming blackness while also affirming whiteness in the same subject.
C) Turning a staff into a serpent.
D) Creating a will without knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The impossible is not a limit on divine power but a logical contradiction; God's power encompasses everything that is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever entertained the thought that God could do something logically impossible, like creating a square circle? How does Ghazali's definition challenge or confirm your understanding of God's power? Commit to affirming that God's power is not arbitrary but rooted in rational order — how will this change how you speak about divine attributes?
?
When faced with a difficult theological issue, do you tend to retreat into 'God can do anything' as an easy answer? How does this lesson force you to think more precisely about what 'anything' means? Write down one area where you will now strive for logical consistency in your beliefs about God.
?
If impossibility is defined by logical contradiction, then God's creation reflects a rational, orderly foundation. Are you comfortable with the idea that God's own nature imposes a kind of internal consistency? Reflect on whether you have ever resisted this idea, and decide now to embrace it as a way to deepen your trust in God's wisdom.

Applying the Definition to Specific Cases
Ghazali applies his definition to specific cases from the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali applies his definition of impossibility to concrete cases like black/white, being in two places, will without knowledge, and knowledge in inanimate objects.

Core Idea
Impossibility is shown by analyzing the meanings of terms.

Now that Ghazali has defined the impossible, he applies it to specific examples from the objection. Each case is examined to see if it involves a logical contradiction. This shows that the alleged impossibilities are indeed impossible by definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the first example: blackness and whiteness
Read the paragraph starting with 'أما الجمع بين السواد والبياض'. Understand why combining them is impossible: affirming blackness in a substrate implies the negation of whiteness.
2
Read the second example: one person in two places
Read how Ghazali analyses spatial location: being in one place implies not being elsewhere. So affirming both is contradictory.
3
Read the third and fourth examples
Read the paragraphs on will without knowledge and knowledge in an inanimate object. Notice how each reduces to a contradiction in terms.
Knowledge in an inanimate is impossible because 'inanimate' means lacking perception.

If it becomes non-existent, then it did not change, but rather that ceased to exist and something else came to be; if it exists along with muddiness, then it did not change, but something else was added to it; and if the blackness remains and the muddiness is non-existent, then it did not change, but remained as it is.
And when we say: blood changed into semen, we mean by it that that same matter cast off one form and put on another form, so the result returns to: a form ceased and a form came to be, and there was an enduring substrate upon which the two forms alternated. And when we say: water changed into air by heating, we mean by it that the matter receptive to the form of water cast off this form and accepted another form, so the matter is shared and the form changes.
Similarly, when we say: the staff changed into a serpent, and dust into an animal. But there is no shared matter between substance and accident, nor between blackness and muddiness, nor between the other kinds, so this is impossible from this aspect.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's defense of divine power against an objection that if God can do everything, then absurdities follow. The objection first lists extreme possibilities: God could create will without knowledge of the willed object, knowledge without life, move a dead body to perform complex actions while it sees nothing, etc. If such things are possible, then there is no distinction between voluntary movement and an involuntary tremor; an orderly act would not prove the agent's knowledge or power. Further, it would require the ability to change kinds (قلب الأجناس), e.g., turning a substance into an accident, knowledge into power, black into white, sound into smell, just as God turned an inanimate object into an animal or stone into gold. This would imply endless impossibilities.

Ghazali's answer begins by defining the impossible (المحال): it is that which is not within divine power (غير مقدور عليه). The impossible reduces to logical contradiction: affirming a thing with its negation, affirming the more specific while negating the more general, or affirming two while negating one. Whatever does not reduce to this is not impossible and therefore is within divine power.

He then applies this definition to specific examples:
- Combining blackness and whiteness in the same subject is impossible because affirming blackness inherently negates whiteness.
- A single person being in two places is impossible because being in one place entails not being elsewhere.
- Will without knowledge is impossible because will by definition is a seeking of something known; without knowledge, the seeking is not will.
- Creating knowledge in an inanimate object is impossible because 'inanimate' is defined as lacking perception. If perception is created, it is no longer inanimate in that sense; if perception is not created, the occuring event cannot be called knowledge.

Finally, he discusses the claim by some theologians that changing kinds (قلب الأجناس) is within God's power. Ghazali argues that such change is unintelligible. He analyzes what 'change' means: if blackness becomes muddiness, either blackness remains (then no change), or perishes (then it is not a change but replacement). True change involves a common substrate (مادة) that persists while forms alternate, as in blood turning to semen (same matter, new form) or water to air (common matter receptive to both forms). However, between substance and accident, blackness and muddiness, and other kinds, there is no common substrate; hence such a transformation is impossible by this reasoning. Thus, the objection's claim is answered: the alleged impossibilities are either contradictions or lack the basis for genuine change, and do not impugn divine power since they are intrinsically impossible.

Key Terms
إثبات (affirmation)
نفي (negation)
محل (substrate)
إرادة (will)
علم (knowledge)
جماد (inanimate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It might be thought that Ghazali is just inventing arbitrary definitions to avoid the objection.
Correction: Ghazali is drawing on necessary conceptual relations that any rational person acknowledges. The definitions are not arbitrary but reflect how we understand these terms.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is will without knowledge impossible?
A) Because God has decreed that will requires knowledge.
B) Because will is defined as seeking something known, so without knowledge it would not be will.
C) Because the objector said so.
D) Because knowledge is a condition for will in human beings only.

Key Takeaway
Each alleged impossibility reduces to a logical contradiction when we analyze the meanings of the terms involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali shows that will without knowledge is impossible. In your own life, when have you pursued something without truly understanding it? What does that reveal about the sincerity or wisdom of your will? Will you commit to seeking knowledge before acting on your desires?
?
Consider a belief or conviction you hold that might involve a hidden contradiction. Are you willing to dissect its meaning as Ghazali does here, even if it challenges your comfort? What would it take for you to subject your most cherished ideas to this kind of analysis?
?
The definition of an inanimate object excludes perception. Do you ever treat other people as 'inanimate'—failing to perceive their feelings or dignity? How can you change that starting today, by truly seeing them as beings with perception?

The Changing of Kinds (قلب الأجناس)
Ghazali argues against the possibility of changing kinds due to lack of a common substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that changing a thing into another kind (e.g., substance into accident) is impossible because there is no common substrate to undergo the change.

Core Idea
Real change requires a shared matter; without it, talk of 'change' is incoherent.

The final part of the objection claims that if God can do anything, He should be able to change kinds (قلب الأجناس)—turning substances into accidents, black into white, etc. Ghazali now addresses this deeper issue, arguing that such change is unintelligible because it lacks a common substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the question about changing kinds
Read the opening: some theologians say changing kinds is possible. Ghazali first asks what 'change' really means. Consider the example of blackness changing into muddiness.
2
Understand the two possibilities
If blackness becomes muddiness, either blackness remains or not. If it remains, no change; if it vanishes, it's replacement, not change. See how Ghazali dismantles the concept.
3
Learn what real change requires
For genuine change, there must be a common substrate (مادة مشتركة) that persists while forms change. Examples: blood to semen, water to air.
4
Apply to the objection's examples
Between substance and accident, blackness and whiteness, etc., there is no common substrate. Hence such 'change' is impossible.
This shows that the objection's examples are not genuine changes but conceptual confusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستدعى وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ؛ ويقولون : إن الله تعالى يقدر على خلق إرادة من غير علم بالمراد ، وخلق علم من غير حياة ، ويقدر على أن يحرك يد ميت ، ويقعده ، ويكتب بيده مجلدات ، ويتعاطى صناعات ، وهو مفتوح العين محدق بصره نحوه ، ولكنه لا يرى ولا حياة فيه ، ولا قدرة له عليه ، وإنما هذه الأفعال المنظومة يخلقها الله تعالى مع تحريك يده ، والحركة من جهة الله تعالى . وبتجويز هذا ، يبطل الفرق بين الحركة الاختيارية وبين الرعدة فلا يدل الفعل المحكم على العلم ولا على قدرة الفاعل . وينبغي أن يقدر على قلب الأجناس ، فيقلب الجوهر عرضاً ، ويقلب العلم قدرة ، والسواد بياضاً ، والصوت رائحة ، كما اقتدر على قلب الجماد حيواناً ، والحجر ذهباً ، ويلزم عليه أيضاً من المحالات ما لا له حصر .
والجواب : أن المحال غير مقدور عليه ، والمحال إثبات الشيء مع نفيه ، أو إثبات الأخص مع نفي الأعم ، أو إثبات الاثنين مع نفي الواحد ، وما لا يرجع إلى هذا فليس بمحال ، وما ليس بمحال فهو مقدور .
أما الجمع بين السواد والبياض فمحال ، لأنا نفهم من إثبات صورة السواد في المحل نفي ماهية البياض ووجود السواد ، فإذا صار نفي البياض مفهوماً من إثبات السواد ، كان إثبات البياض مع نفيه محالاً .
وإنما لا يجوز كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين ، لأنا نفهم من كونه في البيت عدم كونه في غير البيت ، فلا يمكن تقديره في غير البيت كونه في البيت ، المفهم لنفيه عن غير البيت .
وكذلك تفهم من الإرادة طلب معلوم ، فإن فرض طلب ولا علم لم يكن إرادة فكان فيه نفي ما فهمناه .
والجماد يستحيل أن يخلق فيه العلم ، لأنا نفهم من الجماد ما لا يدرك ، فإن خلق فيه إدراك فتسميته جماداً بالمعنى الذي فهمناه محال ، وإن لم يدرك فتسمية الحادث علماً ولا يدرك به محله شيئاً محال ، فهذا وجه استحالته .
وأما قلب الأجناس فقد قال بعض المتكلمين : إنه مقدور لله تعالى . فنقول : مصير الشيء شيئاً آخر غير معقول ، لأن السواد إذا انقلب كدرة مثلاً فالسواد باق أم لا ؟ ! فإن كان معدوماً فلم ينقلب ، بل عدم ذاك ووجد غيره ، وإن كان موجوداً مع الكدرة فلم ينقلب ولكن انضاف إليه غيره ، وإن بقي السواد والكدرة معدومة فلم ينقلب بل بقي على ما هو عليه .
وإذا قلنا : انقلب الدم منياً أردنا به أن تلك المادة بعينها خلعت صورة ولبست صورة أخرى ، فرجع الحاصل إلى أن صورة عدمت وصورة حدثت وثم مادة قائمة تعاقبت عليها الصورتان . وإذا قلنا : انقلب الماء هواء بالتسخين أردنا به أن المادة القابلة لصورة المائية خلعت هذه الصورة وقبلت صورة أخرى فالمادة مشتركة والصورة متغيرة .
وكذلك إذا قلنا : انقلبت العصا ثعباناً والتراب حيواناً . وليس بين العرض والجوهر مادة مشتركة ولا بين السواد والكدرة ولا بين سائر الأجناس مادة مشتركة فكان هذا محالاً من هذا الوجه .
It requires that the existence of one necessitates the existence of the other; and they say: Verily Allah Almighty is able to create will without knowledge of the willed object, and to create knowledge without life, and He is able to move the hand of a dead person, and to seat him, and to write volumes with his hand, and to engage in crafts, while his eye is open, his gaze fixed toward it, but he does not see, and there is no life in him, nor power for him over it, and rather these well-ordered actions, Allah Almighty creates them with the movement of his hand, and the movement is from Allah Almighty. And by allowing this, the distinction between voluntary movement and tremor is invalidated, so the well-ordered action does not indicate knowledge nor the power of the agent. And it should be possible to change the kinds, so that He turns substance into accident, and turns knowledge into power, and blackness into whiteness, and sound into smell, just as He had power to turn an inanimate object into an animal, and stone into gold, and also necessitated from it of impossibilities that which cannot be enumerated.
The answer: That the impossible is not within power, and the impossible is affirming a thing along with its negation, or affirming the more specific while negating the more general, or affirming two while negating one, and whatever does not reduce to this is not impossible, and whatever is not impossible is within power.
As for combining blackness and whiteness, it is impossible, because we understand from affirming the form of blackness in the substrate the negation of the quiddity of whiteness and the existence of blackness, so when the negation of whiteness is understood from the affirmation of blackness, affirming whiteness along with its negation becomes impossible.
And the reason a single person cannot be in two places is because we understand from his being in the house his not being outside the house, so it is impossible to suppose him outside the house while being in the house, the latter implying his negation from outside the house.
Similarly, from will we understand the seeking of something known, so if seeking is assumed without knowledge, it would not be will, and thus it would involve the negation of what we understood.
And it is impossible that knowledge be created in an inanimate object, because we understand from 'inanimate object' that which does not perceive, so if perception is created in it, calling it 'inanimate' in the sense we understand is impossible; and if it does not perceive, then calling the occuring thing 'knowledge' while the substrate perceives nothing by it is impossible. So this is the aspect of its impossibility.
As for the changing of kinds, some theologians have said that it is within the power of Allah Almighty. We say: The becoming of a thing into another thing is unintelligible, because if blackness changes into muddiness, for example, does the blackness remain or not? If it becomes non-existent, then it did not change, but rather that ceased to exist and something else came to be; if it exists along with muddiness, then it did not change, but something else was added to it; and if the blackness remains and the muddiness is non-existent, then it did not change, but remained as it is.
And when we say: blood changed into semen, we mean by it that that same matter cast off one form and put on another form, so the result returns to: a form ceased and a form came to be, and there was an enduring substrate upon which the two forms alternated. And when we say: water changed into air by heating, we mean by it that the matter receptive to the form of water cast off this form and accepted another form, so the matter is shared and the form changes.
Similarly, when we say: the staff changed into a serpent, and dust into an animal. But there is no shared matter between substance and accident, nor between blackness and muddiness, nor between the other kinds, so this is impossible from this aspect.

Misconception: Some think that 'changing kinds' (like turning a substance into an accident) is a straightforward possibility for an all-powerful God.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is required for a genuine change (e.g., blood turning into semen)?
A) The original form must be annihilated.
B) There must be a common substrate that persists while forms change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you expected God to change a fundamental aspect of your reality—perhaps a character trait, a relationship, or a circumstance—without any underlying continuity. What was that expectation really rooted in, and how does Ghazali's argument about the need for a common substrate challenge you to seek meaningful transformation instead of magical change?
?
If you believe that God is all-powerful, does that mean He can do what is logically impossible? Read Ghazali's argument carefully: does he limit God or define the scope of power more precisely? How does this affect your trust that God's actions are always rooted in wisdom, not arbitrary force?
