The Philosophers' Proof: Knowledge Requires an Indivisible Locus
Ghazali presents the philosophers' proof using a conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' conditional syllogism arguing that the soul must be indivisible because knowledge is indivisible.

Core Idea
Knowledge that cannot be divided must inhere in a locus that cannot be divided.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' first proof for the soul's indivisibility. They use a conditional syllogism: if the locus of knowledge were a divisible body, knowledge itself would be divisible; but knowledge is not divisible, so its locus cannot be a body. Ghazali affirms the logical validity and the premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional syllogism structure.
The argument is presented as a qiyas sharti.
2
Identify the two premises
First premise: if the locus is divisible, knowledge is divisible. Second premise: knowledge is indivisible.
The second premise is justified by the fact that we cannot imagine splitting knowledge.
3
Understand the logical form
It is a conditional syllogism affirming the contradictory of the consequent to derive the contradictory of the antecedent.
Ghazali says this form is valid by consensus.
4
Reflect on why knowledge is indivisible
Consider Ghazali's example: we cannot suppose eliminating part of knowledge while the rest remains, because knowledge has no parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم الأول : إن العلوم العقلية ، تحل النفوس الإنسانية ، وهي محصورة ، وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم ، فلابد أن يكون محلها أيضاً لا ينقسم ، وكل جسم ، فمنقسم فدل على أن محلها شيء لا ينقسم . ويمكن إيراد هذا على شرط المنطق بأشكاله ، ولكن أقربه أن يقال : إن كان محل العلم جسما منقسما ، فالعلم الحال فيه أيضاً منقسم ، لكن الحال غير منقسم ، فالمحل ليس جسما . وهذا « قياس شرطى ( استثنى فيه « نقيض التالي » ، فينتج » نقيض المقدم ( بالاتفاق ، فلا نظر فى صحة شكل القياس ، ولا نظر أيضاً في المقدمتين ، فإن : الأول ، قولنا : إن كل حال في منقسم ، ينقسم لا محالة بفرض القسمة في محله ، وهو أولى لا يمكن التشكك فيه . والثانى ، قولنا : إن العلم الواحد يحل في الآدمى ، وهو لا ينقسم ، لأنه لو انقسم ، إلى غير نهاية ، كان محالا ، وإن كانت له نهاية ، فيشتمل على آحاد لا محالة لا تنقسم ، وعلى الجملة نحن نعلم أشياء ، ولا نقدر أن نفرض زوال بعضها ، وبقاء البعض ، من حيث إنه لا بعض لها . والاعتراض على مقامين : المقام الأول : أن يقال ، بم تنكرون على من يقول : محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم ، وقد عرف هذا من مذهب المتكلمين . ولا يبقى بعده إلا الاستبعاد ، وهو أنه كيف تحل العلوم كلها ، في جوهر فرد ، وتكون جميع الجواهر المطيفة بها ، معطلة وهى مجاورة ؟ ! والاستبعاد لا خير فيه ، إذ يتوجه على مذهبهم أيضاً ، أنه كيف تكون النفس شيئاً واحداً لا يتحيز ، ولا يشار إليه ، ولا يكون داخل البدن ، ولا خارجه ، ولا متصلا بالجسم ولا منفصلا عنه ؟ ! إلا أننا ، لا نؤثر هذا المقام ، فإن القول في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ طويل ، ولهم فيه أدلة هندسية يطول الكلام عليها ، ومن جملتها قولهم : جوهر فرد ، بین جوهرین ، هل يلاقى أحد الطرفين منه عين ما يلاقيه الآخر ؟ ! أو غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه فهو محال ، إذ يلزم منه تلاقى الطرفين ، فإن ملاقي الملاقي ملاق ، وإن كان ما يلاقيه غيره ، ففيه إثبات التعدد والانقسام ، وهذه شبهة يطول حلها (۱) ، وبنا غنية عن الخوض فيها ، فلنعدل إلى مقام آخر
Their first statement: The rational sciences inhere in human souls, and they are finite, and among them are indivisible units. Therefore, their locus must also be indivisible. And every body is divisible. So this indicates that their locus is something not divisible. This can be presented in the form of a conditional syllogism with its figures, but the closest way is to say: If the locus of knowledge were a divisible body, then the knowledge inhering in it would also be divisible. But the inhering thing (knowledge) is not divisible. Therefore, the locus is not a body. This is a conditional syllogism in which the contradictory of the consequent is affirmed, producing the contradictory of the antecedent unanimously, so there is no need to examine the validity of the syllogism's form, nor the two premises. The first premise: our statement that everything inhering in something divisible becomes divisible necessarily by assuming division in its locus, and this is obvious and cannot be doubted. The second premise: our statement that a single knowledge inheres in a human and it is not divisible, because if it were divisible indefinitely it would be impossible, and if it had a limit, then it would necessarily contain indivisible units. In summary, we know things and we cannot suppose the elimination of some of them while the rest remain, because they have no parts. The objection is from two positions: The first position: it could be said, why do you reject the view of those who say that the locus of knowledge is an individual atom (jawhar fard) that occupies space and is indivisible? This is known from the doctrine of the mutakallimun. Nothing remains after that except to find it improbable: how can all sciences inhere in a single atom while all the surrounding atoms are devoid [of knowledge] despite being adjacent? But improbability is no argument, for the same objection can be made against their own view: how can the soul be a single thing that does not occupy space, cannot be pointed to, is neither inside the body nor outside, neither connected to the body nor separate from it? However, we do not prefer this position, for the discussion of the indivisible atom is lengthy and they have geometric arguments about which much could be said. Among them is their statement: an individual atom between two atoms—does one of its sides meet exactly what the other side meets, or something else? If it is the same, it is impossible because it would necessitate meeting of the two sides, for whatever meets what meets is a meeting. If what it meets is different, that entails multiplicity and division. This is a specious argument whose resolution is lengthy. We are independent of delving into it. So let us turn to another position.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof for the soul's indivisibility and Ghazali's first objection, which he then dismisses. The philosophers argue that rational knowledge consists of indivisible units (since knowledge is not divisible into parts like bodies), so their locus (the soul) must also be indivisible. They formalize this as a conditional syllogism: if the locus (e.g., a body) were divisible, the knowledge inhering in it would be divisible; but knowledge is not divisible, so the locus is not a body. Ghazali endorses the logical form and premises, calling the first premise obvious and the second premise justified by the fact that we cannot conceive of some parts of knowledge remaining while others cease, because knowledge is a unity without parts. Then Ghazali presents his first objection: a critic might say the locus of knowledge could be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), a position held by the mutakallimun. The objection would then accuse the philosophers of merely finding it improbable that all knowledge inheres in a single atom while surrounding atoms lack it. But Ghazali notes that improbability is not a decisive argument; the philosophers' own view that the soul is non-spatial and not inside or outside the body is equally 'improbable'. However, Ghazali chooses not to pursue this line because the debate over the indivisible atom is long and involves geometric arguments (such as the puzzle of what an atom's sides meet). He concludes by setting aside this first objection and moving to a different approach.

Key Terms
محل (locus)
علم (knowledge)
قياس شرطى (conditional syllogism)
منقسم (divisible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical form does the philosophers' proof take?
A) Categorical syllogism
B) Conditional syllogism
C) Disjunctive syllogism
D) Hypothetical syllogism

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that since knowledge is indivisible, its locus cannot be a divisible body; thus the soul must be indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge is indivisible and resides in an indivisible soul, what does that say about the permanence of your identity as a knower? How does that understanding change the way you treat your own mind and soul?
?
The philosophers argue that a single unit of knowledge cannot be divided. Consider a time when you were certain of a truth. Did that certainty have parts? How does the indivisibility of such knowledge challenge the way you handle doubts or distractions?
?
Since the soul is not a body, what does that mean for your attachment to physical things? Are you more invested in your physical body or in the knowledge that defines your soul? What would it take to shift that balance?

Ghazali's Objection: The Indivisible Atom and Why He Moves On
Ghazali raises the atomist objection and explains why he sets it aside.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection to the philosophers' proof—that the locus could be an indivisible atom—and why he chooses not to pursue this line of argument.

Core Idea
The objection from the indivisible atom is plausible but leads to a lengthy debate Ghazali prefers to avoid.

After presenting the philosophers' proof, Ghazali raises an objection from the position of the mutakallimun: why can't the locus of knowledge be an indivisible atom? He explains the objection and notes that it can be countered with accusations of improbability, but such accusations also apply to the philosophers' own view of the soul. However, Ghazali decides not to pursue this atomic debate because it involves intricate geometric arguments that would prolong the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection: the locus could be a jawhar fard (individual atom) as the mutakallimun hold.
This is a direct challenge to the philosophers' conclusion.
2
Understand Ghazali's critique of the objection
Ghazali says that calling it improbable is not a strong argument, because the philosophers' own view of the soul is equally improbable.
3
See why Ghazali drops this objection
He states that the debate on the indivisible atom is lengthy and involves geometric puzzles, so he will move to another position.
He avoids getting sidetracked.
4
Reflect on the geometric puzzle
The puzzle of an atom between two atoms and what its sides meet illustrates the complexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم الأول : إن العلوم العقلية ، تحل النفوس الإنسانية ، وهي محصورة ، وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم ، فلابد أن يكون محلها أيضاً لا ينقسم ، وكل جسم ، فمنقسم فدل على أن محلها شيء لا ينقسم . ويمكن إيراد هذا على شرط المنطق بأشكاله ، ولكن أقربه أن يقال : إن كان محل العلم جسما منقسما ، فالعلم الحال فيه أيضاً منقسم ، لكن الحال غير منقسم ، فالمحل ليس جسما . وهذا « قياس شرطى ( استثنى فيه « نقيض التالي » ، فينتج » نقيض المقدم ( بالاتفاق ، فلا نظر فى صحة شكل القياس ، ولا نظر أيضاً في المقدمتين ، فإن : الأول ، قولنا : إن كل حال في منقسم ، ينقسم لا محالة بفرض القسمة في محله ، وهو أولى لا يمكن التشكك فيه . والثانى ، قولنا : إن العلم الواحد يحل في الآدمى ، وهو لا ينقسم ، لأنه لو انقسم ، إلى غير نهاية ، كان محالا ، وإن كانت له نهاية ، فيشتمل على آحاد لا محالة لا تنقسم ، وعلى الجملة نحن نعلم أشياء ، ولا نقدر أن نفرض زوال بعضها ، وبقاء البعض ، من حيث إنه لا بعض لها . والاعتراض على مقامين : المقام الأول : أن يقال ، بم تنكرون على من يقول : محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم ، وقد عرف هذا من مذهب المتكلمين . ولا يبقى بعده إلا الاستبعاد ، وهو أنه كيف تحل العلوم كلها ، في جوهر فرد ، وتكون جميع الجواهر المطيفة بها ، معطلة وهى مجاورة ؟ ! والاستبعاد لا خير فيه ، إذ يتوجه على مذهبهم أيضاً ، أنه كيف تكون النفس شيئاً واحداً لا يتحيز ، ولا يشار إليه ، ولا يكون داخل البدن ، ولا خارجه ، ولا متصلا بالجسم ولا منفصلا عنه ؟ ! إلا أننا ، لا نؤثر هذا المقام ، فإن القول في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ طويل ، ولهم فيه أدلة هندسية يطول الكلام عليها ، ومن جملتها قولهم : جوهر فرد ، بین جوهرین ، هل يلاقى أحد الطرفين منه عين ما يلاقيه الآخر ؟ ! أو غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه فهو محال ، إذ يلزم منه تلاقى الطرفين ، فإن ملاقي الملاقي ملاق ، وإن كان ما يلاقيه غيره ، ففيه إثبات التعدد والانقسام ، وهذه شبهة يطول حلها (۱) ، وبنا غنية عن الخوض فيها ، فلنعدل إلى مقام آخر
Their first statement: The rational sciences inhere in human souls, and they are finite, and among them are indivisible units. Therefore, their locus must also be indivisible. And every body is divisible. So this indicates that their locus is something not divisible. This can be presented in the form of a conditional syllogism with its figures, but the closest way is to say: If the locus of knowledge were a divisible body, then the knowledge inhering in it would also be divisible. But the inhering thing (knowledge) is not divisible. Therefore, the locus is not a body. This is a conditional syllogism in which the contradictory of the consequent is affirmed, producing the contradictory of the antecedent unanimously, so there is no need to examine the validity of the syllogism's form, nor the two premises. The first premise: our statement that everything inhering in something divisible becomes divisible necessarily by assuming division in its locus, and this is obvious and cannot be doubted. The second premise: our statement that a single knowledge inheres in a human and it is not divisible, because if it were divisible indefinitely it would be impossible, and if it had a limit, then it would necessarily contain indivisible units. In summary, we know things and we cannot suppose the elimination of some of them while the rest remain, because they have no parts. The objection is from two positions: The first position: it could be said, why do you reject the view of those who say that the locus of knowledge is an individual atom (jawhar fard) that occupies space and is indivisible? This is known from the doctrine of the mutakallimun. Nothing remains after that except to find it improbable: how can all sciences inhere in a single atom while all the surrounding atoms are devoid [of knowledge] despite being adjacent? But improbability is no argument, for the same objection can be made against their own view: how can the soul be a single thing that does not occupy space, cannot be pointed to, is neither inside the body nor outside, neither connected to the body nor separate from it? However, we do not prefer this position, for the discussion of the indivisible atom is lengthy and they have geometric arguments about which much could be said. Among them is their statement: an individual atom between two atoms—does one of its sides meet exactly what the other side meets, or something else? If it is the same, it is impossible because it would necessitate meeting of the two sides, for whatever meets what meets is a meeting. If what it meets is different, that entails multiplicity and division. This is a specious argument whose resolution is lengthy. We are independent of delving into it. So let us turn to another position.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof for the soul's indivisibility and Ghazali's first objection, which he then dismisses. The philosophers argue that rational knowledge consists of indivisible units (since knowledge is not divisible into parts like bodies), so their locus (the soul) must also be indivisible. They formalize this as a conditional syllogism: if the locus (e.g., a body) were divisible, the knowledge inhering in it would be divisible; but knowledge is not divisible, so the locus is not a body. Ghazali endorses the logical form and premises, calling the first premise obvious and the second premise justified by the fact that we cannot conceive of some parts of knowledge remaining while others cease, because knowledge is a unity without parts. Then Ghazali presents his first objection: a critic might say the locus of knowledge could be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), a position held by the mutakallimun. The objection would then accuse the philosophers of merely finding it improbable that all knowledge inheres in a single atom while surrounding atoms lack it. But Ghazali notes that improbability is not a decisive argument; the philosophers' own view that the soul is non-spatial and not inside or outside the body is equally 'improbable'. However, Ghazali chooses not to pursue this line because the debate over the indivisible atom is long and involves geometric arguments (such as the puzzle of what an atom's sides meet). He concludes by setting aside this first objection and moving to a different approach.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (individual atom)
متحيز (occupying space)
استبعاد (improbability)
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (indivisible atom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali fully endorses the atomist objection.
Correction: He only uses it to show a weakness and then sets it aside because it leads to lengthy debates.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali decide not to pursue the objection based on the indivisible atom?
A) Because the objection is logically flawed
B) Because the debate over the atom is lengthy and he prefers a different approach
C) Because the philosophers proved him wrong
D) Because the mutakallimun recanted their view

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the atomist position to challenge the philosophers but then avoids the complex atomic debate, shifting to a different argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth claim simply because it seemed improbable, without examining whether your own worldview contains equally improbable elements? In what area of your faith or beliefs are you doing that right now? What would it take to admit that your own position might face the same difficulty?
?
Ghazali sets aside a lengthy debate to focus on what he considers more productive. Is there a theological or personal issue you have been avoiding because it's complex or uncomfortable? What would it mean to turn toward it today, even if resolution is not immediate?
?
The philosophers use geometric puzzles to undermine the atomist view, but Ghazali finds them specious and chooses not to engage. Are there arguments or criticisms you've encountered that seem clever but you sense are misleading? How do you discern when to engage and when to move on to more solid ground?

Comparing Eternal and Temporal Permanence and Attainment
Ghazali contrasts eternal permanence and intense attainment with temporal and physical ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali contrasts eternal permanence and intellectual attainment with temporal, sensory ones to argue for the superiority of the afterlife.

Core Idea
Eternal permanence and intellectual union outshine all temporal and sensory experiences.

Ghazali uses two rhetorical questions to compare the permanence and intensity of attainment of eternal versus temporal things. He argues that eternal permanence is incomparable to the changing permanence of this world, and that intellectual perception, where the intellect and intelligible become one, is far more intense than sensory perception through contact of surfaces. This passage establishes a key premise for his argument about the superiority of the afterlife's pleasures.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage first, then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical questions and exclamations that convey Ghazali's forceful point.
The text uses multiple question and exclamation marks for emphasis.
2
Identify the two comparisons
The passage makes two comparisons: one about permanence (al-dawām) and one about intensity of attainment (shiddat al-wuṣūl). Locate each question and understand what is being compared.
The first compares eternal permanence to temporal permanence; the second compares sensory attainment to intellectual attainment.
3
Understand the mode of intellectual perception
Focus on the description of intellectual attainment: it 'pervades the essence of its recipient until it is as though it is it itself.' Contrast this with sensory attainment, which is merely 'through contact of surfaces.'
This idea is rooted in the Neoplatonic and Avicennan theory of intellection.
4
Reflect on the doctrine of unity
The passage ends by stating that the intellect, the intelligible, and the intelligent are one or close to one. Consider how this supports the superiority of intellectual pleasures and the afterlife.
This is a key philosophical claim that Ghazali uses to elevate intellectual cognition over sensory experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدوام ، فكيف يقاس الدوام الأبدى ، بالدوام المتغير الفاسد ؟ ! وأما شدة الوصول ، فكيف يكون حال ما وصوله بملاقاة السطوح ، بالقياس إلى ما هو سار في جوهره قابله ، حتى يكون كأنه هو هو ، بلا انفصال ؟ ! ! إذ العقل ، والمعقول ، والعاقل ، شيء واحد ، أو قريب من الواحد
As for permanence, how can eternal permanence be compared to temporal and corruptible permanence?! As for intensity of attainment, how can the state of that whose attainment is through contact of surfaces be compared to that which pervades the essence of its recipient, until it is as though it is it itself, without separation?! For the intellect, the intelligible, and the intelligent are one thing, or close to one.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a comparative argument. The author poses two rhetorical questions. First, regarding permanence (al-dawām): how can eternal, everlasting permanence be compared to the changing, corruptible permanence of this world? The implication is that the permanence of the afterlife is utterly superior. Second, regarding the intensity of attainment (shiddat al-wuṣūl): the author contrasts two modes of perception. One mode involves contact of surfaces (mulāqāt al-suṭūḥ), which is presumably how physical senses work (e.g., light hitting the eye). The other mode is that which pervades the essence of its recipient (sārī fī jawharihi qābilihi), such that it becomes as if it is identical with it, without separation. This is a reference to intellectual perception, where the intellect and the intelligible become one (or close to one) in the act of intellection. This is a standard Neoplatonic/Avicennan doctrine that Ghazali is using here to argue for the superiority of intellectual pleasures over sensory ones.

Key Terms

الدوام المتغير الفاسد
شدة الوصول
العقل والمعقول والعاقل شيء واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is denying the reality of sensory pleasures.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying sensory pleasures; he is only comparing their permanence and intensity, arguing that eternal pleasures are superior.

Checkpoint

A) It is through contact of surfaces
B) It is through pervading the recipient's essence, becoming one with it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that eternal permanence and intellectual unity far surpass any temporal, sensory experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Your permanence in this world is literally changing and corrupting. How does that reality make you feel about the pleasures you chase today? Will you let it change your choices?

Eternal Will and Divine Power: The Philosophers' Logical Argument
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument that eternal will does not contradict God's power, using conditional vs categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers used a logical distinction between conditional and categorical propositions to argue that God's eternal will does not limit His power over all possible things.

Core Idea
Conditional statements ('if He willed') can be true even if the condition is never met.

The philosophers must respond to a major objection: if God's will is eternal and unchanging, how can He be truly powerful over all things? After all, power includes the ability to do different things at different times. In this passage, they deploy a precise logical tool — the distinction between conditional and categorical propositions — to show that affirming a conditional power statement does not require actual willing or acting. Ghazali then reveals the shocking implication: this logic forces the world to be eternal and repetitive.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the passage once. The philosophers state that affirming God's power over all things does not contradict His unchanging will. Notice that they define 'power' as a conditional ('if He willed, He would do').
Key phrase: 'لو شاء لفعل'
2
Examine the logical distinction
The philosophers contrast categorical statements (e.g., 'He does not will') with conditional statements (e.g., 'if He willed, He would do'). According to logic, negative categoricals do not contradict positive conditionals. Consider the analogy of a person able to cut his own throat but never willing to do so.
This logical point is from Aristotelian logic.
3
Understand Ghazali's inference
If the philosophers' reasoning is correct, then the proof of an eternal and unchanging will leads to the conclusion that the divine order must be regular and repetitive. Any difference in individual times must itself follow a pattern. Ghazali presents this as the necessary consequence of their position.
This sets up Ghazali's subsequent refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعموا أن هذا لا يناقض قولنا: إن الله تعالى قادر على كل شيء، فإنا نقول: إن الله تعالى قادر على البعث والنشور، وجميع الأمور الممكنة، على معنى أنه لو شاء، لفعل، وليس من شرط صدق قولنا هذا، أن يشاء ولا أن يفعل، وهذا كما أنا نقول: إن فلاناً قادر على أن يجز رقبة نفسه، ويبعج بطن نفسه، ويصدق ذلك، على معنى أنه لو شاء لفعل، ولكنا نعلم أنه لا يشاء ولا يفعل، وقولنا: لا يشاء، ولا يفعل، لا يناقض قولنا: إنه قادر، أنه لو شاء لفعل؛ فإن الحمليات لا تناقض الشرطيات، كما ذكر في المنطق إذ قولنا: لو شاء لفعل، شرطى موجب، وقولنا: ما شاء، وما فعل، حمليتان سالبتان، والسالبة الحملية، لا تناقض الموجبة الشرطية. فإذن الدليل، الذي دلنا على أن مشيئته أزلية، وليست متغيرة، يدلنا على أن مجرى الأمر الإلهى، لا يكون إلا على انتظام واتساق بالتكرر والعود، وإن اختلف في آحاد الأوقات، فيكون اختلافه أيضاً على انتظام واتساق بالتكرر والعود، وأما غير هذا، فلا يمكن.
And they claimed that this does not contradict our saying: 'Indeed, Allah Almighty is powerful over every thing.' For we say: Indeed, Allah Almighty is powerful over resurrection and the gathering, and all possible things, meaning that if He willed, He would do; and it is not a condition of the truth of this saying that He will or that He do. And this is like when we say: Such-and-such a person is able to cut his own neck and rip open his own belly, and this is true in the sense that if he willed, he would do; but we know that he does not will and does not do. And our saying: 'He does not will' and 'He does not do' does not contradict our saying: 'He is able' and 'if He willed, He would do.' For categorical propositions do not contradict conditional propositions, as mentioned in logic. Since our saying: 'If He willed, He would do' is a positive conditional, and our saying: 'He did not will' and 'He did not do' are two negative categoricals, and the negative categorical does not contradict the positive conditional. So then, the proof that demonstrated to us that His will is eternal and not changing demonstrates to us that the course of the divine affair cannot be except with regularity and consistency by repetition and return; and even if it differs in individual times, its difference also occurs with regularity and consistency by repetition and return. And anything other than this is not possible.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents an argument attributed to the philosophers (identified in a footnote as the falasifa). They claim that the earlier statement (that the eternal will has a single course) does not contradict the affirmation that God is powerful over all things. Their reasoning: when we say 'God is able to do X' we mean 'if He willed, He would do it.' The truth of this conditional does not require that He actually wills or acts. They give an analogy: a person can be said to be able to cut his own throat, meaning if he willed, he would do it, even though we know he never wills or does so. Thus, the negative categorical statements 'He does not will' and 'He does not do' do not contradict the positive conditional 'if He willed, He would do.' This logical point is drawn from Aristotelian logic: categorical negatives do not contradict conditional positives. Ghazali then offers his own inference: if this reasoning is followed, the proof that God's will is eternal and unchanging leads to the conclusion that the divine order must be regular and consistent by repetition and return (i.e., the world must be eternal and repetitive). Any difference in individual times would still be part of that regular pattern. He concludes that anything other than this (e.g., a genuinely new event not following a pattern) is not possible. This sets up the problematic implication of the philosophers' position, which Ghazali will subsequently refute.

Key Terms
مشيئة أزلية
قادر
شرطية
حملية
النظام الإلهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny divine power over novelties.
Correction: The argument is about logical consistency: conditional power statements do not require actual willing, so eternal will does not contradict power. The inference to a repetitive world is a separate step.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument in this passage, why does saying 'God is able to do X' not require that He actually wills or does X?
A) Because ability is independent of will.
B) Because the statement 'if He willed, He would do' is a conditional that can be true even if the condition is never fulfilled.
C) Because God's will is unchanging.
D) Because categorical propositions contradict conditionals.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's power over all things is consistent with an eternal, unchanging will because power is defined conditionally; this logical move, however, forces the conclusion that the world operates on a fixed, repetitive pattern.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power over your life is not diminished by His not acting in ways you expect? Or do you secretly think that if He doesn't intervene, He must be unable? How does the conditional definition of power challenge your assumptions about God's involvement in your daily affairs?
?
The philosophers conclude that the divine order is regular and consistent, even when events differ. When your life feels chaotic or new difficulties arise, do you trust that it is still under God's unchanging will and consistent order? What would it cost you to surrender to that belief completely?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, then your current situation—no matter how unexpected—is exactly what He eternally willed. Are you willing to accept that, or does your heart resist? What is one specific area of your life where you need to stop questioning God's wisdom and start trusting His eternal will?

The Perplexing Issue of Pre-Eternity and the Concept of Pre-Eternity by Type
Commentary on the difficulty of the pre-eternity issue and pre-eternity by type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complexity of the pre-eternity debate and how the concept of 'pre-eternity by type' was used by some scholars, including Ibn Taymiyya, to address theological difficulties.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity by type: a nuanced position held by some orthodox scholars.

This lesson dives into a commentary that reveals the struggle of even the greatest minds—Galen, al-Razi, al-Ghazali—with the question of the world's origin. It then introduces a surprising twist: some respected traditionists, like Ibn Taymiyya, proposed that the world is pre-eternal 'by type,' meaning that there is always some individual of the world existing in succession. This view, especially regarding the Throne, shows the depth of the issue.

Guided Path
1
Acknowledge the difficulty
Read the opening lines about Galen and al-Razi to see how even great scholars admitted perplexity. This sets the tone for the complexity of the issue.
Galen's statement 'I do not know' is a powerful admission.
2
Examine al-Ghazali's caution
Al-Ghazali warns against takfir simply based on perceived error in demonstrative matters. This frames the diversity of opinions that follow.
This shows that the issue is not simple enough for blanket accusations.
3
Understand pre-eternity by type
Al-Dawani reports that some later hadith scholars held that the world is pre-eternal by type, meaning a continuous succession of individuals. This is the key doctrinal concept.
Note that this is not the philosophers' claim of one eternal world.
4
Apply to Ibn Taymiyya's view on the Throne
Ibn Taymiyya's literalist stance on God's sitting on the Throne led him to say the Throne is pre-eternal by type—God perpetually creates new thrones—to avoid the Throne being eternal in the sense of a single entity.
This illustrates how even a staunch literalist found a way to accommodate the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه مسألة عويصة حارت فيها العقول وتبلبلت الأفكار يدل على ذلك قول جالينوس لا أدرى العالم قديم أم محدث وتعليق الإمام الرازي عليه بقوله وهذا دليل على أن جالينوس كان منصفا طالبا للحق فإن الكلام في هذه المسألة قد يقع من العسر والصعوبة إلى حيث تضمحل أكثر العقول فيه وإذا رحنا نستفتى الغزالى نفسه عن اضطراب العلماء وزلزلة أفكارهم في أمثال هذه المواطن وجدناه يقول ولا ينبغى أن يكفر بعض النظار بعضا بأن يراه غالطا فيما يعتقده برهانيا فإن ذلك ليس أمرا هينا سهل المدرك على أن بعض العلماء المشهود لهم برسوخ القدم فى علوم الشريعة يروى عنه القول بشيء من ذلك قال الدواني في شرح العضدية وقد قال بالقدم الجنسي بأن يكون فرد من أفراد العالم لا يزال على سبيل التعاقب موجودا بعض المحدثين المتأخرين وقد رأيت في بعض تصانيف ابن تيمية القول به في العرش وقد علق الشيخ محمد عبده على هذا بقوله أي قال بقدم العالم بالجنس أى أنه لا يزال فرد من أفراد العالم موجودا وما من جزء من أجزاء الزمان إلا وقد كان فيه حادث إلى غير النهاية بعض المحدثين الآخذين بظاهر الأحاديث لما أنهم قد رأوا فيها ما يدل على ذلك وبه قال ابن تيمية على ما نقل عنه الشارح وذلك أن ابن تيمية كان من الحنابلة الآخذين بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث القائلين بأن الله استوى على العرش جلوسا فلما أورد عليه أنه يلزم أن يكون العرش أزليا لما أن الله أزلى وأزلية العرش خلاف مذهبه قال إنه قديم بالنوع أى أن الله لا يزال يعدم عرشا ويحدث آخر من الأزل إلى الأبد حتى يكون له الاستواء أزلا وأبدا
This is a difficult issue, minds are perplexed and thoughts confounded. This is indicated by the statement of Galen: 'I do not know whether the world is pre-eternal or originated.' And the comment of Imam al-Razi on it: 'This is evidence that Galen was fair, seeking the truth, for speech about this issue may reach such difficulty and hardship that most minds dissipate in it.' And if we consult al-Ghazali himself about the disturbance of scholars and the shaking of their thoughts in such places, we find him saying: 'It is not befitting that one investigator declare another an unbeliever simply because he sees him as mistaken in what he considers demonstrative, for that is not an easy matter with a facile grasp.' Moreover, some scholars who are recognized for their firm grounding in the sciences of the Law are reported to have said something of that. Al-Dawani said in his commentary on al-‘Adudiyya: 'Some later hadith scholars have spoken about pre-eternity by type - that an individual from among the individuals of the world never ceases to exist in succession. And I have seen in some works of Ibn Taymiyya his statement about it regarding the Throne.' And Shaykh Muhammad Abduh commented on this, saying: 'That is, he said that the world is pre-eternal by type, meaning that an individual from among the individuals of the world always exists, and there is no part of time except that it contains an originated event ad infinitum. Some hadith scholars who take the outward meaning of hadiths, because they have seen in them what indicates that, and Ibn Taymiyya said that according to what the commentator reported from him. And that is because Ibn Taymiyya was among the Hanbalis who take the outward meanings of verses and hadiths, those who say that God established Himself upon the Throne in a sitting manner. When it was objected to him that this necessitates that the Throne be eternal, since God is eternal, and the eternity of the Throne contradicts his school, he said: It is pre-eternal by type, meaning that God does not cease to destroy a Throne and create another from pre-eternity to eternity, so that His establishing upon it is pre-eternal and everlasting.'
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly commentary on the first issue of the pre-eternity of the world. It begins by acknowledging that this is a very difficult matter that has perplexed minds, citing Galen's admission of ignorance. Then it quotes Imam al-Razi, who sees Galen's honesty as praiseworthy. It then cites al-Ghazali's caution against declaring fellow researchers unbelievers simply for error in what they consider demonstrative. The commentator then notes that even some respected scholars have held the view of pre-eternity in a certain sense, specifically 'pre-eternity by type' (al-qidam al-jinsī), meaning that there is always some individual of the world existing in succession. This view is attributed to later hadith scholars and to Ibn Taymiyya regarding the Throne. The commentary explains that Ibn Taymiyya, as a Hanbali who took texts literally, believed in God's sitting on the Throne, which required the Throne to be pre-eternal. To avoid this, he said the Throne is pre-eternal by type, meaning God perpetually destroys and creates thrones so that His sitting is eternal. This shows the complexity of the issue and that even orthodox scholars have entertained ideas that might seem close to the philosophers' claims.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
قدم الجنسي
العرش
ظاهر الأحاديث
ابن تيمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all orthodox Muslim scholars unanimously declared anyone who believed in any form of pre-eternity as a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage shows that some respected scholars, including Ibn Taymiyya, allowed the concept of pre-eternity by type, indicating diversity of opinion and caution against immediate takfir.

Checkpoint
What does the text indicate about the pre-eternity of the world?
A) It is a simple issue agreed upon
B) It is a difficult issue that even some respected scholars have allowed in a certain sense
C) It is unanimously rejected by all Muslims
D) It is only a problem for philosophers, not for theologians

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternity of the world is not a straightforward issue; even orthodox scholars like Ibn Taymiyya acknowledged a form of it (by type) to resolve scriptural and rational tensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a complex theological issue like the pre-eternity of the world, do you feel the same perplexity as Galen, or do you settle for a simplistic answer? Would you be honest enough to say 'I do not know'?
?
The scholars quoted here refused to label each other as unbelievers over this issue. Are you quick to judge others who hold different views on difficult matters? What would it take for you to extend the same fairness?
?
Ibn Taymiyya went to great lengths to reconcile his scriptural commitments with rational objections. Are you willing to struggle deeply with your own faith when it seems to conflict with your understanding, or do you avoid the tension?

The Three Issues for Takfir of the Philosophers
Ghazali states the three issues for which the philosophers are declared unbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific philosophical beliefs that Imam al-Ghazali declares necessitate disbelief (takfir).

Core Idea
Takfir applies only to three precise doctrines, not all philosophy.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to a hypothetical question: does he decisively declare the philosophers as unbelievers? He clarifies that takfir is required only in three specific matters: the pre-eternity of the world, God's knowledge of particulars, and bodily resurrection. This lesson examines these three issues and their incompatibility with Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: a question followed by a decisive answer.
2
Identify the three issues
List the three issues as stated by Ghazali. Understand each one: (1) pre-eternity of the world and atoms, (2) God not knowing particulars, (3) denial of bodily resurrection.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali ends by saying these three do not harmonize with Islam and believing them is falsehood. Consider why these are deemed contradictory to Islamic teachings.
This shows the limits of takfir—only these three specific doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : قد فصلتم مذاهب هؤلاء ، أفتقطعون القول بتفكيرهم ، و وجوب القتل لمن يعتقد اعتقادهم ؟ ! قلنا : تكفيرهم لا بد منه في ثلاث مسائل : إحداها مسألة قدم العالم وقولهم إن الجواهر كلها قديمة والثانية قولهم إن الله تعالى لا يحيط علماً بالجزئيات الحادثة من الأشخاص والثالثة إنكارهم بعث الأجساد وحشرها فهذه المسائل ثلاث لا تلائم الإسلام بوجه ومعتقدها معتقد كذب
If someone says: 'You have detailed the doctrines of these people, do you decisively declare their disbelief and the obligation to kill whoever believes their belief?' We say: Their disbelief is necessary in three issues: One of them is the issue of the pre-eternity of the world and their statement that all atoms are pre-eternal. The second is their statement that God Almighty does not encompass knowledge of particular contingent things, of individuals. The third is their denial of the resurrection of bodies and their gathering. So these three issues do not harmonize with Islam in any way, and whoever believes them has a belief of falsehood.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the question and answer regarding the takfir (declaration of disbelief) of the philosophers. It identifies three specific doctrines for which the philosophers are unequivocally declared unbelievers: 1) The pre-eternity of the world, specifically that all atoms (jawahir) are pre-eternal. 2) The denial that God knows particular contingent events and individuals. 3) The denial of bodily resurrection and gathering. The passage concludes that these three beliefs are incompatible with Islam and constitute falsehood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any philosophical view leads to takfir.
Correction: This passage specifies only three precise issues that necessitate takfir, not all philosophical opinions.

D) Pre-eternity of the soul, denial of resurrection, belief in reincarnation

Key Takeaway
Only three specific philosophical doctrines—pre-eternity of the world, denial of God’s knowledge of particulars, and denial of bodily resurrection—necessitate takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Arguments Allowing Pre-Eternity: Al-Khayali's Thesis
Al-Khayali argues that intention can be essentially prior to creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Khayali argued that the world could be pre-eternal without implying that God is a necessitated agent, and how this avoids Ghazali's objection.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is possible if intention is essentially prior, not temporally.

In this passage, the commentator presents a bold argument from al-Khayali and Abd al-Hakim: the pre-eternity of the world does not necessarily conflict with divine will. They challenge the mainstream kalam claim that whatever issues from will must be originated. This argument opens the door for a different understanding of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how al-Khayali distinguishes between essential priority and temporal priority of intention. This is the core of his argument.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask yourself how this argument avoids the necessity of God being a forced agent (al-ijab). This was Ghazali's main objection against pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل إن بعض العلماء الذين يعتبرون من أركان نهضة علم الكلام قد جوز القول بقدم العالم ير فيه خطرا على العقيدة ذلك هو المولى الخيالى وعبد الحكيم السيالكوتى إذ يقرر سعد الدين التفتازانى شارح العقائد النفسية أن القدم ينا في العدم لأن القديم إن كان واجبا لذاته فظاهر وإلا لزم استناده إليه بطريق الإيجاب إذ الصادر عن الشيء بالقصد والاختيار يكون حادثا بالضرورة إلخ فلا يرضى الخيالي عن قول السعد الصادر عن الشيء بالقصد والاختيار يكون حادثا ويجوز أن يكون الشيء صادرا بالقصد والاختيار ومع ذلك يكون قديما وهذا نصه واعترض عليه بجواز أن يكون تقدم القصد الكامل على الإيجاد كتقدم الإيجاد على الوجود في أنه بحسب الذات لا الزمان فيكون مقارنا للوجود زمانا ومتى جاز مقارنة القصد للإيجاد زمانا والايجاد مقارن للوجود زمانا أيضا كان القصد مقارنا للوجود زمانا فمتى كان القصد قديما وهذا ما لا استحالة فيه كان الوجود قديما ويعلق عبد الحكيم على وجهة نظر الخيالي فيصورها تصويرا واضحا ولا يعقب عليها بنقد مما يدل على رضاه عنها وما دام خوف المتكلمين من القول بعدم العالم راجعا إلى ما يؤدى إليه من لزوم يكون الباري فاعلا بالإيجاب وقد كان هذا هو المحور الذي يدور عليه خلاف الغزالي للفلاسفة وما دام يمكن تفادى هذا اللازم فلا ضير إذن في القول بقدم العالم إن صح دليله وشبيه بموقف الخيالي من هذه المسألة موقف توماس الإكويني حيث يقول الإرادة الحرة لا يمكن الفحص عنها بالنظر الصرف فقد يكون الله خلق العالم منذ القدم وقد يكون خلقه في الزمان ولا يمكن إثبات أحد الطرفين بالبرهان الله بالجزئيات أن هذا الرأي فهم خاص قد سبق في هامش سابق في مسألة علم الله بالجزئيات في عبارة الفلاسفة وأن العبارة محتملة لسواه فلا يصح إذن القطع بكفرهم قد سبق في تعليقنا على هذه المسألة أن هذا فهم خاص في عبارتهم إذ أن في عبارتهم تصريحا ببعث الأجساد فلا يصح أيضا القطع بكفرهم كيف يتصور تكذيب الأنبياء بالنسبة لهذه المسائل الثلاث أما مسألة قدم العالم فالفلاسفة لا يكذبون بها نصوص الأنبياء وإنما ينزلون هذه النصوص كالخلق والفعل على
Rather, some scholars who are considered pillars of the renaissance of the science of kalam allowed the statement of the pre-eternity of the world, seeing no danger in it for creed. That is al-Mawla al-Khayali and Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti. For Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, commentator on al-‘Aqa’id al-Nasafiyya, established that pre-eternity is incompatible with originatedness, because if the pre-eternal is necessary in itself, it is obvious; otherwise, it must be dependent on it by way of necessitation, for that which issues from a thing with intention and choice is necessarily originated, etc. But al-Khayali is not satisfied with al-Sa'd's statement that 'that which issues from a thing with intention and choice is originated.' He allows that something may issue with intention and choice and yet be pre-eternal. This is his text: 'And objection is made to him about the possibility that the precedence of complete intention over bringing into existence is like the precedence of bringing into existence over existence, in that it is according to essence not time, so it is concomitant with existence in time.' And whenever the concomitance of intention with bringing into existence in time is possible, and bringing into existence is concomitant with existence in time also, then intention is concomitant with existence in time. So when intention is pre-eternal—and there is no impossibility in that—then existence is pre-eternal. And Abd al-Hakim comments on al-Khayali's viewpoint, depicting it clearly and not criticizing it, indicating his satisfaction with it. And as long as the fear of the theologians from the statement of the pre-eternity of the world is due to what it leads to of the necessity of the Creator being an agent by necessitation, and this was the axis around which Ghazali's dispute with the philosophers revolved, and as long as this necessary consequence can be avoided, then there is no harm in saying the world is pre-eternal if its proof is sound. And similar to al-Khayali's position on this issue is the position of Thomas Aquinas (1225-1274) where he says: 'Free will cannot be examined by pure reasoning; it is possible that God created the world from pre-eternity, and it is possible that He created it in time, and neither side can be demonstrated by proof.' God's knowledge of particulars—that this opinion is a particular understanding. Already in a previous footnote, concerning the issue of God's knowledge of particulars in the philosophers' statement, that their statement is susceptible to other interpretations, so it is not correct to definitively declare them unbelievers. Already in our commentary on this issue, this is a particular understanding of their statement, for in their statement there is an explicit mention of bodily resurrection, so it is also not correct to definitively declare them unbelievers. How can one imagine the charge of giving lie to the prophets regarding these three issues? As for the issue of the pre-eternity of the world, the philosophers do not give lie to the texts of the prophets by it; rather, they interpret these texts (such as creation and action) as...
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts. The first part presents the argument of al-Khayali and Abd al-Hakim that the pre-eternity of the world is possible without implying that God is a necessitated agent. They argue against Taftazani's claim that whatever proceeds from a willful agent must be originated. Al-Khayali proposes that intention can precede creation essentially, not temporally, so that a pre-eternal intention can coexist with a pre-eternal creation. Abd al-Hakim seems to approve. The commentator notes that this avoids the necessity of God being a forced agent, which was the core of Ghazali's objection. Thus, pre-eternity becomes a permissible possibility if supported by proof. The example of Thomas Aquinas is cited, who similarly held that reason cannot decide between a pre-eternal or temporal creation. The second part consists of three footnotes that argue against takfir on the other two issues: The philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of particulars is a particular interpretation; their text can be read otherwise. Similarly, their denial of bodily resurrection is a misunderstanding because their statements actually affirm it. The third footnote suggests that charging them with giving lie to the prophets is not straightforward because they interpret the texts (like creation) allegorically.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
القصد الكامل
الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity of the world forces God to be a necessitated agent.
Correction: Al-Khayali shows that with essential priority of intention, pre-eternity does not require necessitation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Khayali, what makes it possible for something to be pre-eternal even if it issues from intention and choice?
A) Intention must be temporal, not essential
B) Intention can be essentially prior, not temporally
C) Intention and existence are unrelated
D) Pre-eternity requires no intention at all

Key Takeaway
Al-Khayali's argument shows that pre-eternity can be reconciled with divine will, making takfir on this issue less clear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, do you assume it must be temporally before His actions? Where in your life do you unknowingly impose human limitations on the Divine?
?
If reason cannot decide whether the world is pre-eternal or created in time, would you be content to leave that question uncertain and instead trust what God reveals? What does that reveal about your need for control?
?
Al-Khayali was willing to accept a view that many of his peers considered dangerous. Are you willing to hold a belief that others might criticize, as long as it does not contradict the core of faith? What belief are you holding back from exploring for fear of what others might say?

Caveats on Takfir: Knowledge of Particulars and Resurrection
Commentator argues against takfir on knowledge and resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that the philosophers' denials of God's knowledge of particulars and bodily resurrection may not warrant takfir due to interpretive possibilities.

Core Idea
Philosophers' statements can be interpreted allegorically, so takfir is not certain.

The commentator adds three footnotes addressing the other two issues. He argues that the philosophers' texts on God's knowledge and resurrection are open to other interpretations, and therefore it is not valid to definitively declare them unbelievers. This lesson examines these caveats.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnotes carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the second part of the passage, which contains three caveats about takfir.
2
Identify each caveat
List the three reasons why takfir is not warranted: (1) their statement on knowledge can be interpreted otherwise, (2) they explicitly affirm bodily resurrection, (3) they interpret prophetic texts allegorically.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this affects the overall judgment on the philosophers. Are they still considered unbelievers? The commentator suggests caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل إن بعض العلماء الذين يعتبرون من أركان نهضة علم الكلام قد جوز القول بقدم العالم ير فيه خطرا على العقيدة ذلك هو المولى الخيالى وعبد الحكيم السيالكوتى إذ يقرر سعد الدين التفتازانى شارح العقائد النفسية أن القدم ينا في العدم لأن القديم إن كان واجبا لذاته فظاهر وإلا لزم استناده إليه بطريق الإيجاب إذ الصادر عن الشيء بالقصد والاختيار يكون حادثا بالضرورة إلخ فلا يرضى الخيالي عن قول السعد الصادر عن الشيء بالقصد والاختيار يكون حادثا ويجوز أن يكون الشيء صادرا بالقصد والاختيار ومع ذلك يكون قديما وهذا نصه واعترض عليه بجواز أن يكون تقدم القصد الكامل على الإيجاد كتقدم الإيجاد على الوجود في أنه بحسب الذات لا الزمان فيكون مقارنا للوجود زمانا ومتى جاز مقارنة القصد للإيجاد زمانا والايجاد مقارن للوجود زمانا أيضا كان القصد مقارنا للوجود زمانا فمتى كان القصد قديما وهذا ما لا استحالة فيه كان الوجود قديما ويعلق عبد الحكيم على وجهة نظر الخيالي فيصورها تصويرا واضحا ولا يعقب عليها بنقد مما يدل على رضاه عنها وما دام خوف المتكلمين من القول بعدم العالم راجعا إلى ما يؤدى إليه من لزوم يكون الباري فاعلا بالإيجاب وقد كان هذا هو المحور الذي يدور عليه خلاف الغزالي للفلاسفة وما دام يمكن تفادى هذا اللازم فلا ضير إذن في القول بقدم العالم إن صح دليله وشبيه بموقف الخيالي من هذه المسألة موقف توماس الإكويني حيث يقول الإرادة الحرة لا يمكن الفحص عنها بالنظر الصرف فقد يكون الله خلق العالم منذ القدم وقد يكون خلقه في الزمان ولا يمكن إثبات أحد الطرفين بالبرهان الله بالجزئيات أن هذا الرأي فهم خاص قد سبق في هامش سابق في مسألة علم الله بالجزئيات في عبارة الفلاسفة وأن العبارة محتملة لسواه فلا يصح إذن القطع بكفرهم قد سبق في تعليقنا على هذه المسألة أن هذا فهم خاص في عبارتهم إذ أن في عبارتهم تصريحا ببعث الأجساد فلا يصح أيضا القطع بكفرهم كيف يتصور تكذيب الأنبياء بالنسبة لهذه المسائل الثلاث أما مسألة قدم العالم فالفلاسفة لا يكذبون بها نصوص الأنبياء وإنما ينزلون هذه النصوص كالخلق والفعل على
Rather, some scholars who are considered pillars of the renaissance of the science of kalam allowed the statement of the pre-eternity of the world, seeing no danger in it for creed. That is al-Mawla al-Khayali and Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti. For Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, commentator on al-‘Aqa’id al-Nasafiyya, established that pre-eternity is incompatible with originatedness, because if the pre-eternal is necessary in itself, it is obvious; otherwise, it must be dependent on it by way of necessitation, for that which issues from a thing with intention and choice is necessarily originated, etc. But al-Khayali is not satisfied with al-Sa'd's statement that 'that which issues from a thing with intention and choice is originated.' He allows that something may issue with intention and choice and yet be pre-eternal. This is his text: 'And objection is made to him about the possibility that the precedence of complete intention over bringing into existence is like the precedence of bringing into existence over existence, in that it is according to essence not time, so it is concomitant with existence in time.' And whenever the concomitance of intention with bringing into existence in time is possible, and bringing into existence is concomitant with existence in time also, then intention is concomitant with existence in time. So when intention is pre-eternal—and there is no impossibility in that—then existence is pre-eternal. And Abd al-Hakim comments on al-Khayali's viewpoint, depicting it clearly and not criticizing it, indicating his satisfaction with it. And as long as the fear of the theologians from the statement of the pre-eternity of the world is due to what it leads to of the necessity of the Creator being an agent by necessitation, and this was the axis around which Ghazali's dispute with the philosophers revolved, and as long as this necessary consequence can be avoided, then there is no harm in saying the world is pre-eternal if its proof is sound. And similar to al-Khayali's position on this issue is the position of Thomas Aquinas (1225-1274) where he says: 'Free will cannot be examined by pure reasoning; it is possible that God created the world from pre-eternity, and it is possible that He created it in time, and neither side can be demonstrated by proof.' God's knowledge of particulars—that this opinion is a particular understanding. Already in a previous footnote, concerning the issue of God's knowledge of particulars in the philosophers' statement, that their statement is susceptible to other interpretations, so it is not correct to definitively declare them unbelievers. Already in our commentary on this issue, this is a particular understanding of their statement, for in their statement there is an explicit mention of bodily resurrection, so it is also not correct to definitively declare them unbelievers. How can one imagine the charge of giving lie to the prophets regarding these three issues? As for the issue of the pre-eternity of the world, the philosophers do not give lie to the texts of the prophets by it; rather, they interpret these texts (such as creation and action) as...
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts. The first part presents the argument of al-Khayali and Abd al-Hakim that the pre-eternity of the world is possible without implying that God is a necessitated agent. They argue against Taftazani's claim that whatever proceeds from a willful agent must be originated. Al-Khayali proposes that intention can precede creation essentially, not temporally, so that a pre-eternal intention can coexist with a pre-eternal creation. Abd al-Hakim seems to approve. The commentator notes that this avoids the necessity of God being a forced agent, which was the core of Ghazali's objection. Thus, pre-eternity becomes a permissible possibility if supported by proof. The example of Thomas Aquinas is cited, who similarly held that reason cannot decide between a pre-eternal or temporal creation. The second part consists of three footnotes that argue against takfir on the other two issues: The philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of particulars is a particular interpretation; their text can be read otherwise. Similarly, their denial of bodily resurrection is a misunderstanding because their statements actually affirm it. The third footnote suggests that charging them with giving lie to the prophets is not straightforward because they interpret the texts (like creation) allegorically.

D) Because the prophets did not mention it

Key Takeaway
Takfir on the philosophers is not straightforward because their statements allow alternative interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to judge someone's faith based on a statement they made, without considering that it might have an alternative interpretation? How does this passage challenge you to exercise caution before making judgments about others' iman? Commit to pausing and seeking clarification before assuming the worst.
?
Think of a time when you assumed that someone's allegorical interpretation of a religious text was tantamount to denial. In light of this lesson, what would you do differently now? Are you willing to extend the benefit of the doubt to those whose understanding differs from yours, even when you strongly disagree?
?
The philosophers' ambiguous statements were enough to avoid takfir. But can you honestly say that your own understanding of complex theological issues is free from ambiguity? What does that imply about how you view others who disagree with you? Write down one area where you might be too certain, and resolve to approach it with more humility.

Second and Third Issues of the Philosophers
Lists two issues and declares incompatibility with Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of particulars and bodily resurrection are considered incompatible with Islam, and the nuance in declaring takfir.

Core Idea
Two more philosophical claims—restricted divine knowledge and denial of bodily resurrection—rejected by Islam.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers by addressing two more doctrines: that God does not know particular temporal events, and that they deny the bodily resurrection. He asserts these are utterly incompatible with Islam. However, the editorial footnotes add a layer of caution: the philosophers' expressions may be open to interpretation, so one should not rush to declare them disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the three issues mentioned (the first being from earlier).
The passage includes footnotes; consider them as part of the commentary.
2
Identify the two new issues
The second issue is that God does not encompass knowledge of particular events. The third is denial of bodily resurrection. Recognize these.
3
Understand the editorial caution
The footnotes highlight that these expressions may be interpreted differently, so definitive takfir is not appropriate. Reflect on the difference between the author's stance and the footnote's moderation.
This nuance is important for aqeedah discussions.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Ghazali states that these three issues (including pre-eternity of the world) are incompatible with Islam. Consider why each is problematic.
The footnote also mentions that philosophers interpret prophetic texts allegorically, which is clear disbelief.
5
Reflect on takfir conditions
Think about the conditions for declaring someone a kafir. The footnotes suggest that ambiguous expressions warrant caution.
This lesson is not just about doctrine but also about methodology.

The second is their statement: God Almighty does not encompass knowledge of particular (1) events, of persons. The third: their denial (2) of the resurrection of bodies and their gathering. So these three issues are in no way compatible with Islam, and their believer is a believer in falsehood (3). (1) Already mentioned in a previous footnote – concerning the issue of God's knowledge of particulars – in the expression of the philosophers, and that the expression is open to other interpretation, so it is not valid to definitively declare them disbelievers. (2) Already mentioned in our commentary on this issue that this is a specific understanding of their expression, for their expression explicitly mentions the resurrection of bodies, so it is not valid to definitively declare them disbelievers either. (3) How can one imagine the denial of the prophets regarding these three issues! Commentary
This passage presents the second and third issues of the philosophers according to Ghazali: the claim that God does not know particular events, and the denial of bodily resurrection. The author states that these three issues (including the pre-eternity of the world from earlier) are incompatible with Islam. The footnotes provide editorial clarification: regarding God's knowledge, the philosophers' phrasing may be interpreted differently, so one cannot definitively declare them kuffar; similarly, their denial of resurrection may be a specific interpretation. The third footnote comments on how the philosophers do not reject prophetic texts outright but interpret them allegorically, which the author considers clear kufr not held by any Muslim sect. The lesson ends with the note that if Ghazali had encountered later scholars, he would have found those who hold this view.

Correction: The footnote (2) explains that the philosophers' expression may be interpreted differently, so it is not valid to definitively declare them disbelievers. Caution is needed in takfir.

?
Resurrection of bodies is central to our hope of meeting Allah. Are you living today as if you will stand before Him with this very body, accountable for what it did? What would you change if you realized resurrection is not just a doctrine but your personal reality?

Philosophers' Views on Attributes and Causality and the Ruling on Takfir
Ghazali compares philosophers' doctrines to Mu'tazila and identifies criteria for takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophers' doctrines on divine attributes and causality align with the Mu'tazila, and that only three specific issues warrant a ruling of takfir according to Ghazali.

Core Idea
Only three philosophical doctrines merit takfir; the rest are shared with Islamic sects.

In this passage, Imam Ghazali explains that the philosophers' views on divine attributes and causality are not unique to them; they are similar to those of the Mu'tazila. He then clarifies that among all their teachings, only three core issues constitute disbelief, while others are found among various Islamic groups. This helps us understand Ghazali's nuanced approach to takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysing. Let Ghazali's own presentation come through.
2
Compare philosophers to Mu'tazila
Notice how Ghazali states that their doctrine on divine attributes and tawhid is close to the Mu'tazila. Also note the connection to causality (tawallud).
3
Identify the three exceptions
Ghazali says that except for three principles, all their views have been expressed by some Islamic sects. Understand that takfir applies only to those three.
The three issues are pre-eternity of the world, God's knowledge of particulars, and denial of bodily resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما عدا هذه المسائل الثلاث ، من تصرفهم في الصفات الإلهية ، واعتقاد التوحيد فيها ، فمذهبهم قريب من مذهب المعتزلة ، ومذهبهم في تلازم الأسباب الطبيعية ، هو الذي صرح به المعتزلة ، في التولد ، وكذلك جميع ما نقلناه عنهم ، قد نطق به فريق من فرق الإسلام إلا هذه الأصول الثلاثة فمن يرى تكفير أهل البدع من فرق الإسلام ، يكفرهم أيضاً بها ، ومن يتوقف عن التكفير ، يقتصر على تكفيرهم بهذه المسائل الثلاث وأما نحن فلسنا نؤثر الآن الخوض ، في تكفير (۲) أهل البدع ، وما يصح المعنى الذى هداهم إليه تفكيرهم ، وهذا شيء والتكذيب شيء آخر . وأما المسألتان الأخريان ، فليس فيهما تكذيب ولا تأويل (۱) لو عاش الغزالى حتى عاصر ) الخيالى » و « ابن تيمية ( لوجد من يعتقده أو على الأقل يجوز اعتقاده (۲) غريب من الغزالى هذا التوقف ، وهو مظهر له يجعلنا لا نشك في أنه متزمت ، متشدد ، وأية شدة أبلغ من أن يذهب في هذه العبارات مذهباً ، يوهم أن مخالفة الأشاعرة ، في مسألة من المسائل ، مهما هان أمرها ، ولو كانت القول ، بعدم زيادة الصفات على الذات ، كفر ، يستوجب غضب الجبار ، والخلود الدائم في النار · ولكنا نعرف الغزالى فى مظهر آخر غير هذا المظهر ، نعرف الغزالى الذى يحتاط جد الاحتياط في مسألة الكفر والإيمان ، حتى ليزجر الناس ، عن أن يضعوا كلمة الكفر على أطراف شفاههم . يلفظونها بغير حساب ، ويرمون بها الناس ، لا إحقاقاً لحق ، ولكن جرياً وراء الهوى والغرض ، وذلك حيث يقول : من كفر مسلماً فقد كفر ] أن يثق ونعرف الغزالى سمحاً سهلا ، يؤاخى بين النظار ، ولا يريد لهم أن يرمى بعضهم بعضاً بالكفر لأنهم لا يختلفون إلا في مسائل دقيقة ، ليس إدراك الحق فيها سهلا . ولا هيناً ، فلا ينبغي واحد منهم برأيه ، الوثوق الذي يجعله يعتقد أن رأيه هو الحق الصراح ، ورأى مخالفه هو الكفر المبين ، وذلك حيث يقول في فيصل التفرقة :
As for what is beyond these three issues, from their handling of the divine attributes and their belief in tawhid therein, their doctrine is close to the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, and their doctrine regarding the interconnection of natural causes is what the Mu'tazila stated concerning tawallud. Likewise, all that we have transmitted from them has been articulated by a group among the Islamic sects except for these three principles. So whoever considers declaring the people of innovation from the Islamic sects as disbelievers also declares them disbelievers on account of these, and whoever refrains from takfir confines their takfir to these three issues. As for us, we do not now prefer to delve into the takfir (2) of the people of innovation, and what is correct is the meaning that their reasoning led them to, and this is one thing and denial is another. As for the other two issues, there is no denial in them nor interpretation (1). (1) If Ghazali had lived to the time of al-Khayyali and Ibn Taymiyya, he would have found someone who believes it or at least permits believing it. (2) Strange from al-Ghazali this hesitation, and it is an aspect of him that makes us not doubt that he is rigid, strict. And what strictness is more extreme than that he goes in these expressions in a way that suggests that opposing the Ash'aris on any issue, no matter how trivial, even if it is the statement that there is no increase of attributes over the essence, is kufr that necessitates the wrath of the Almighty and eternal punishment in Hell. But we know al-Ghazali in another aspect other than this aspect; we know al-Ghazali who is extremely cautious regarding the issue of kufr and iman, to the point that he rebukes people from placing the word kufr on the tips of their tongues, uttering it without account, and accusing people with it, not to establish truth but to follow whim and desire. And that is where he says: 'Whoever declares a Muslim a kafir has committed kufr.' And we know al-Ghazali as tolerant and easy, fraternizing the scholars, and not wanting them to accuse one another of kufr because they only differ on subtle issues, the perception of truth in which is neither easy nor trivial. So none of them should be so confident in his opinion that he believes his opinion is the manifest truth and the opinion of his opponent is clear kufr. And that is where he says in Faysal al-tafriqa:
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophers' views outside the three core issues. Their doctrines on divine attributes and tawhid are close to the Mu'tazila. Their view on causality (talawum al-asbab al-tabi'iyya) is what the Mu'tazila stated under 'tawallud'. Thus, many of their positions are shared by Muslim sects. Only the three fundamental issues (pre-eternity of the world, God's knowledge of particulars, denial of bodily resurrection) warrant takfir if one holds them. The author then comments on Ghazali's caution regarding takfir, citing his statement 'whoever declares a Muslim a kafir has committed kufr' and his leniency in Faysal al-tafriqa. The editorial note criticizes Ghazali's apparent strictness in one place but acknowledges his broader caution.

Imam Ghazali's Cautious Approach to Takfir
Commentary on Ghazali's strictness and his caution on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ghazali's cautious stance on declaring takfir, including his statement that 'whoever declares a Muslim a kafir has committed kufr' and his emphasis on not being overconfident in one's own opinion.

Core Idea
Takfir is a grave matter; Ghazali warns against easy accusations of kufr.

In this part of the passage, the commentator reflects on Ghazali's apparent strictness in the previous lines but then highlights Ghazali's well-known caution regarding takfir. Ghazali's principle is that those who call a Muslim a kafir without due cause themselves fall into kufr. He also advocates for tolerance and humility, recognizing that differences on subtle issues do not warrant takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic text, which includes editorial remarks and Ghazali's quoted sayings.
2
Understand the principle 'whoever declares a Muslim a kafir has committed kufr'
This is a famous hadith-like statement of Ghazali. It shows his extreme caution against takfir.
3
Reflect on humility in theological differences
Ghazali urges that scholars should not be overly confident that their view is truth and the opponent's is kufr, especially on subtle issues.

As for what is beyond these three issues, from their handling of the divine attributes and their belief in tawhid therein, their doctrine is close to the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, and their doctrine regarding the interconnection of natural causes is what the Mu'tazila stated concerning tawallud. Likewise, all that we have transmitted from them has been articulated by a group among the Islamic sects except for these three principles. So whoever considers declaring the people of innovation from the Islamic sects as disbelievers also declares them disbelievers on account of these, and whoever refrains from takfir confines their takfir to these three issues. As for us, we do not now prefer to delve into the takfir (2) of the people of innovation, and what is correct is the meaning that their reasoning led them to, and this is one thing and denial is another. As for the other two issues, there is no denial in them nor interpretation (1). (1) If Ghazali had lived to the time of al-Khayyali and Ibn Taymiyya, he would have found someone who believes it or at least permits believing it. (2) Strange from al-Ghazali this hesitation, and it is an aspect of him that makes us not doubt that he is rigid, strict. And what strictness is more extreme than that he goes in these expressions in a way that suggests that opposing the Ash'aris on any issue, no matter how trivial, even if it is the statement that there is no increase of attributes over the essence, is kufr that necessitates the wrath of the Almighty and eternal punishment in Hell. But we know al-Ghazali in another aspect other than this aspect; we know al-Ghazali who is extremely cautious regarding the issue of kufr and iman, to the point that he rebukes people from placing the word kufr on the tips of their tongues, uttering it without account, and accusing people with it, not to establish truth but to follow whim and desire. And that is where he says: 'Whoever declares a Muslim a kafir has committed kufr.' And we know al-Ghazali as tolerant and easy, fraternizing the scholars, and not wanting them to accuse one another of kufr because they only differ on subtle issues, the perception of truth in which is neither easy nor trivial. So none of them should be so confident in his opinion that he believes his opinion is the manifest truth and the opinion of his opponent is clear kufr. And that is where he says in Faysal al-tafriqa:

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophers' views outside the three core issues. Their doctrines on divine attributes and tawhid are close to the Mu'tazila. Their view on causality (talawum al-asbab al-tabi'iyya) is what the Mu'tazila stated under 'tawallud'. Thus, many of their positions are shared by Muslim sects. Only the three fundamental issues (pre-eternity of the world, God's knowledge of particulars, denial of bodily resurrection) warrant takfir if one holds them. The author then comments on Ghazali's caution regarding takfir, citing his statement 'whoever declares a Muslim a kafir has committed kufr' and his leniency in Faysal al-tafriqa. The editorial note criticizes Ghazali's apparent strictness in one place but acknowledges his broader caution.
