Ghazali's Argument on the Will as a Preponderating Factor
Ghazali defines will as a distinguishing attribute and proves its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that divine will is necessary because power alone cannot choose between equally possible alternatives.

Core Idea
Will is the attribute that distinguishes between equals, making it necessary for creation.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim of necessary emanation by showing that all times are equally suitable for creation. Since power alone cannot choose between identical alternatives, a will is required. This lesson examines his definition and proof of the divine will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize that all times are equally suitable for the world's existence, so a preponderating factor is needed.
This sets up the need for something beyond power.
2
Follow Ghazali's critique
If the will is said to give preference, what causes the will to choose? This pushes back the question.
3
Define the will
Ghazali defines will as an attribute that distinguishes one thing from its like.
This is the core definition.
4
Grasp the proof
Since power relates equally to opposites, the occurrence of one must have a preponderating factor, which is the will.
This proves the necessity of will.
5
Conclude
Will is essential for divine action; it is not arbitrary but precisely this distinguishing attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدول الفلاسفة عن دعوى الضرورة ، ولجوؤهم إلى الاستدلال : الأوقات كلها متساوية من حيث الصلاحية لأن يوجد فيها العالم فليس هناك ما يرجح وقتاً على وقت . إن قيل : إن الإرادة رجحت وقتاً على وقت ... قيل : فما الذي جعل الإرادة ترجح ؟ بحث في تحديد اختصاصات الإرادة . الإرادة عند الغزالى صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله . والدليل على أن الله صفة هذا شأنها ، أن القدرة لما تساوت نسبتها إلى الأمرين المتقابلين ، كان وقوع أحدهما دون الآخر لا بد له من مرجح
The philosophers' departure from the claim of necessity, and their turning to reasoning: All times are equal in terms of suitability for the world to exist, so there is nothing that gives preference to one time over another. If it is said: the will gave preference to one time over another... it is said: so what made the will give preference? Investigation into determining the specific functions of the will. The will according to Ghazali is an attribute whose function is to distinguish one thing from its like. And the proof that God has an attribute whose function is this is that since power's relation is equal to the two opposite things, the occurrence of one over the other must have a preponderating factor.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's argument for the necessity of divine will as a distinguishing principle. He notes that if the philosophers abandon the claim of necessary emanation and accept that times are equally suitable for creation, then there must be a factor that prefers one time. If that factor is the will, then what causes the will to prefer? Ghazali defines will as an attribute that distinguishes between identical alternatives. He then provides a proof: since divine power relates equally to opposites, the occurrence of one must require a preponderating factor, which is the will. This is a standard kalam argument for the necessity of will in addition to power.

Misconception: That the will's preference is arbitrary without reason.
Correction: Ghazali defines will precisely as the attribute that distinguishes between equals without needing an external cause—it is the very nature of will to specify.

Ghazali's Definition of Will as Distinguishing Equals
Ghazali defines will and proves its necessity as a divine attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali defines divine will as the attribute that distinguishes something from its equal and how he proves its necessity.

Core Idea
Will is the faculty of choosing when all options are equal.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key objection from the philosophers: if all times are equally suited for creation, what determines God's choice? His answer introduces a precise definition of will as the attribute that distinguishes between equals, followed by a logical proof grounded in the relationship between power and its objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Notice how the philosophers abandon necessity and argue that all times are equal, leaving no natural preference.
This sets up the need for a will.
2
Examine Ghazali's definition of will
Read the definition: will is an attribute whose function is to distinguish something from its equal. Reflect on what 'equal' means here.
3
Follow the proof step-by-step
The proof: power relates equally to opposites; occurrence of one opposite requires a preponderator (murajjih). That preponderator cannot be power itself, so it must be will.
Understand that will is not arbitrary but a rational necessity.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine the definition and proof to see how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' objection: will is precisely what breaks the symmetry of equal possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدول الفلاسفة عن دعوى الضرورة ، ولجوؤهم إلى الاستدلال الأوقات كلها متساوية من حيث الصلاحية لأن يوجد فيها العالم فليس هناك ما يرجح وقتاً على وقت . إن قيل : إن الإرادة رجحت وقتان على وقت ... قيل : فما الذي جعل الإرادة ترجح ؟ بحث في تحديد اختصاصات الإرادة الإرادة عند الغزالى صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله والدليل على أن الله صفة هذا شأنها ، أن القدرة لما تساوت نسبتها إلى الأمرين المتقابلين ، كان وقوع أحدهما دون الآخر لا بد له من مرجح
The philosophers abandoning the claim of necessity and resorting to reasoning: all times are equal in terms of suitability for the world to exist in them, so there is nothing that gives preponderance to one time over another. If it is said: the will gave preponderance to one time over another... it is said: then what made the will give preponderance? A discussion on determining the specializations of will. Will according to al-Ghazali is an attribute whose function is to distinguish something from its equal. And the proof that God has an attribute of this function: since power is equally related to two opposites, the occurrence of one without the other necessarily requires a preponderator.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a definition-with-proof. First, it notes that the philosophers themselves abandoned claiming necessity and turned to reasoning: they argued that all times are equally suitable for the world's existence, so there is no natural preference. Then they object: if you say God's will preferred one time, then what made the will prefer? Ghazali uses this to introduce the definition of will. He defines will as the attribute whose specific function (شأن) is to distinguish something from its equal (تمييز الشيء عن مثله). The proof is that when power (قدرة) relates equally to two opposite possibilities, occurrence of one over the other must have a preponderating factor (مرجح). That preponderator cannot be power since power is equal, so it must be will. Thus will is not arbitrary; it is the necessary principle of determination when no external cause forces a choice.

Key Terms
الإرادة (will)
تمييز الشيء عن مثله (distinguishing something from its equal)
المرجح (preponderator)
القدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali's will is arbitrary or capricious.
Correction: Ghazali defines will as the very faculty of distinguishing between equals, which is a rational necessity when no external cause determines choice.

Checkpoint

B) To distinguish something from its equal.
C) To provide knowledge of the future.
D) To reward and punish.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defines the divine will as the attribute that distinguishes between equal possibilities, proving its necessity from the symmetry of power's relation to opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face two equally good options, do you rely on external signs or do you exercise your own will to choose? What does that reveal about your reliance on God's will versus your own?
?
If God's will is not arbitrary but distinguishes between equals, how does that challenge your moments of indecision? Will you trust that God gives you the ability to choose even when everything seems equal?
?
Ghazali proves will is necessary because power alone cannot determine. Where in your life have you mistaken mere capability (power) for actual choice (will)? How can you deliberately activate your will to break the symmetry of inaction?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Philosophers' Objection to Divine Will
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that distinguishing equals is unintelligible using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two arguments refuting the philosophers' claim that an attribute distinguishing equals is unintelligible.

Core Idea
God's will is incomparable to human will; rational proof establishes it.

This passage presents Ghazali's response to a key philosophical objection: how can God will something when all options are equal? The philosophers argued that distinguishing equals is impossible. Ghazali offers two counterarguments: first, the analogy to human will fails because God's attributes are incomparable; second, the philosophers' own example of created will actually undermines their point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' objection
Read the passage and notice how the philosophers argue that distinguishing something from its equal is unintelligible, using the example of created will.
The objection is that equality and distinction are contradictory.
2
Examine Ghazali's first argument: the analogy is corrupt
Ghazali argues that comparing God's will to human will is invalid because God's knowledge and will differ fundamentally from ours. The claim of impossibility is not based on necessity but on reasoning, and rational proof actually proves such an attribute for God.
Ghazali also notes that the name 'will' is permissible by Law, even if it implies purpose.
3
Understand the role of rational proof
Notice that Ghazali shifts the burden: the philosophers' claim of impossibility is not self-evident; it is based on reasoning, and stronger reasoning (the need for a preponderator) supports the attribute.
4
Consider the second argument: created will cannot preponderate
Ghazali points out that in created will, preponderance of one equal over another is not conceivable. This actually shows that human will lacks the power to distinguish equals, so the philosophers' analogy fails.
This argument is incomplete in the source; it implies that divine will can do what human will cannot.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this refutation defends divine attributes as unique and beyond human comparison, and how Ghazali uses rational argument alongside scriptural permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرى الغزالى أن قول القائل : ولماذا رجحت الإرادة أحد الأمرين المتساويين ؟ مثل قول القائل : لماذا اقتضى العلم الإحاطة بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه ؟ ادعاء الفلاسفة أن إثبات صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله غير معقول ؛ فإن كون الشىء مثلا يقتضى أنه غير متميز ، وكونه متميزاً يقتضى أنه ليس مثلا ، والاستشهاد على ذلك بالإرادة الحادثة التي لا يتصور فيها ذلك . اعتراض الغزالى على ادعاء الفلاسفة هذا من وجهين : الوجه الأول : أن التمثيل بالإرادة الحادثة طريقة فاسدة فإن علم الله يخالف علومنا فى أمور كثيرة ، فلا يبعد أن تكون إرادة الله مخالفة لإرادتنا . ودعوى استحالة إثبات صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ، علام تقوم ؟ أتقوم على الضرورة ؟ أم تقوم على النظر ... ؟ إن الأمر على العكس من ذلك ؛ إذ قد دل دليل العقل على إثبات صفة لله هذا شأنها ، فإن أريد لها أن لا تسمى إرادة فلا مشاحة في الأسماء . إطلاق اسم الإرادة على هذه الصفة ، مأذون فيه من الشرع و إلا فالإرادة موضوعة فى اللغة لتعيين ما فيه غرض ، ولا غرض في أفعال الله . الوجه الثانى : أن الإرادة الحادثة لا يتصور فيها ترجيح أحد الأمرين المتساويين على الآخر .
Al-Ghazali holds that the statement of one who says: why did the will prefer one of the two equal things? is like the statement of one who says: why did knowledge require encompassing the known as it is? The philosophers' claim that proving an attribute whose function is to distinguish something from its equal is unintelligible; because something being equal requires that it is not distinguished, and being distinguished requires that it is not equal, and they cite as evidence the created will in which that is not conceivable. Al-Ghazali's objection to this claim of the philosophers is from two aspects: The first aspect: that the analogy with the created will is a corrupt method, for God's knowledge differs from our knowledges in many matters, so it is not unlikely that God's will is different from our will. And the claim of the impossibility of proving an attribute whose function is to distinguish something from its equal, on what does it rest? Does it rest on necessity? Or does it rest on reasoning? The matter is the opposite; for rational proof has indicated the proof of an attribute of God of this function, and if it is desired not to call it 'will', there is no contention over names. The application of the name 'will' to this attribute is permitted by the Law, otherwise 'will' is placed in the language for designating that in which there is purpose, and there is no purpose in God's actions. The second aspect: that in the created will, preponderance of one of two equal things over the other is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (objection-and-answer) of a philosophers' objection. Ghazali begins by comparing the question 'why did the will prefer one of two equals?' to the question 'why does knowledge encompass the known as it is?'—both are improper because they ask for a reason beyond the definition of the attribute. The philosophers then object that an attribute distinguishing equals is unintelligible because the concepts of equality and distinction are contradictory, and they cite human will as an example where this does not occur. Ghazali's first refutation: the analogy to human will is invalid because God's attributes are incomparable with human attributes. Furthermore, the claim of impossibility is not based on necessity but on reasoning; but rational proof (the need for a preponderator) already establishes such an attribute for God. Whether we call it 'will' or not is a matter of naming, and the term is permitted by the Law. The second aspect (as given) notes that in the created will, preponderance of one equal over another is not conceivable, implying that the philosophers' example actually disproves their point (since human will cannot distinguish equals, but God's will can).

Key Terms
تمييز الشيء عن مثله
الإرادة الحادثة
الضرورة
النظر
لا مشاحة في الأسماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali rejects analogy altogether here.
Correction: He specifically rejects the analogy to human will because God's attributes are incomparable, not any analogy of any kind.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali counter the philosophers' analogy to created will?
A) He accepts the analogy but modifies it.
B) He argues that God's knowledge and will are incomparably different from human ones.
C) He denies that will is an attribute of God.
D) He says the philosophers misunderstood the definition of will.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends divine will as an attribute that distinguishes equals, contrary to human will, and argues that rational proof and scriptural permission justify it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's will is incomparably different from yours. Where in your life have you been assuming that God must act like a human, and how does this lesson force you to repent of that assumption?
?
Ghazali shows that rational proof can establish truths about God even when they seem impossible to our limited minds. What is one belief about God that you have struggled to accept because it defies your logic, and will you now submit to it based on this proof?
?
The philosophers' error was limiting God by their own experiences. In what specific area of your worship or theology have you been 'domesticating' God to fit your comfort zone, and what concrete step will you take today to honor His otherness?

Ghazali's First Objection: The Flawed Analogy with Human Will
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' analogy and argues for a divine attribute of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the philosophers' analogy between human will and divine will, and how rational proof supports a distinguishing attribute in God.

Core Idea
God's will differs from human will; what is impossible for us is possible for Him.

Ghazali begins his refutation by attacking the philosophers' key analogy. They claim that just as human will cannot choose between two truly equal things, divine will cannot either. But Ghazali points out that this analogy is invalid because God's attributes, like knowledge, are fundamentally different from ours. He then questions the very basis of their impossibility claim and asserts that rational proof actually establishes the attribute they deny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ghazali structures his objection.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find the statement that a distinguishing attribute is impossible and their evidence from temporal will. Understand their reasoning.
3
Examine Ghazali's critique of the analogy
Note his argument that God's knowledge differs from ours, so His will may also differ. Why does this break the analogy?
4
Analyze his questioning of impossibility
See how he asks whether the impossibility claim is based on necessity or rational inquiry, and then asserts that rational proof actually establishes the attribute.
This flips the burden of proof.
5
Reflect on the naming issue
Understand that the name 'will' is permitted by Law but language defines it with purpose, which God lacks. This clarifies the attribute's nature.
This shows Ghazali is not denying divine will, but refining its conception.

ادعاء الفلاسفة أن إثبات صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله غير معقول ؛ فإن كون الشىء مثلا يقتضى أنه غير متميز ، وكونه متميزاً يقتضى أنه ليس مثلا ، والاستشهاد على ذلك بالإرادة الحادثة التي لا يتصور فيها ذلك. اعتراض الغزالى على ادعاء الفلاسفة هذا من وجهين : الوجه الأول : أن التمثيل بالإرادة الحادثة طريقة فاسدة فإن علم الله يخالف علومنا فى أمور كثيرة ، فلا يبعد أن تكون إرادة الله مخالفة لإرادتنا . ودعوى استحالة إثبات صفة من شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ، علام تقوم ؟ أتقوم على الضرورة ؟ أم تقوم على النظر ... ؟ إن الأمر على العكس من ذلك ؛ إذ قد دل دليل العقل على إثبات صفة لله هذا شأنها ، فإن أريد لها أن لا تسمى [إرادة] فلا مشاحة في الأسماء. إطلاق اسم [الإرادة] على هذه الصفة ، مأذون فيه من الشرع و إلا فالإرادة موضوعة فى اللغة لتعيين ما فيه غرض ، ولا غرض في أفعال الله. أن الإرادة الحادثة لا يتصور فيها ترجيح أحد الأمرين المتساويين على الآخر .
The philosophers' claim that establishing an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like is unintelligible; for a thing being a like necessitates that it is not distinguished, and being distinguished necessitates that it is not a like. And they cite in evidence of that the temporal will in which such distinction is inconceivable. Al-Ghazali's objection to this claim of the philosophers is from two aspects: The first aspect: that the analogy with the temporal will is a corrupt method, for God's knowledge differs from our knowledge in many things, so it is not far-fetched that God's will be different from our will. And the claim of the impossibility of establishing an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like, on what does it rely? Does it rely on necessity? Or does it rely on rational inquiry...? The matter is the opposite of that; for rational proof has indicated the establishment of an attribute of God with this function. If one intends that it not be called 'will', there is no dispute over names. The application of the name 'will' to this attribute is permitted by the Law; otherwise, 'will' is placed in language to denote that which contains a purpose, and there is no purpose in God's acts. That the temporal will cannot conceive of preference of one of two equal things over the other.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect of Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that an attribute which distinguishes between two similar things is impossible. He begins by stating the philosophers' argument: if a thing is similar to another, it is not distinct; if distinct, it is not similar. They support this by pointing to temporal human will, which cannot choose between truly equal options. Ghazali first critiques their use of analogy: God's knowledge is different from ours, so His will may also be different. He then questions the basis of their impossibility claim—whether it is self-evident (necessary) or derived from reasoning. He asserts that rational proof actually establishes such an attribute for God. Even if one refuses to call it 'will', the name is not essential; the Law permits it, and language defines 'will' as relating to purpose, but God has no purpose. Thus, the temporal will's limitation does not apply to God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that Ghazali denies that God has a will altogether because he argues against the philosophers' conception.
Correction: Ghazali actually affirms a divine attribute that distinguishes, and only objects to calling it 'will' if that implies a purpose-driven choice like humans. He accepts the term by legal permission.

Checkpoint

B) Because God's knowledge differs from ours and hence His will may also differ.
C) Because human will cannot choose between equals, but God's can.
D) Because the philosophers have no proof for their claim.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' analogy between human and divine will fails, and rational proof establishes an attribute of distinction in God, even if we call it 'will' only by legal permission.

?
Ghazali argues that calling God's attribute 'will' is permitted by the Law even though linguistically it implies purpose. Are there aspects of God's nature that you find difficult to affirm because they clash with human categories? Will you submit to the divine self-disclosure even when it challenges your logic?
?
The philosophers demanded that God's will must function like human will to be intelligible. Where in your life do you insist that God must act in a way that makes sense to you? Can you ask Him today to expand your understanding and help you worship Him as He truly is, not as you expect Him to be?

Ghazali's First Objection: True Agency Requires Will
Ghazali argues that for God to be truly an agent, He must act by will and choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true agency, according to Ghazali, necessarily requires will, choice, and knowledge, and why the philosophers' concept of emanation by nature fails to qualify as genuine agency.

Core Idea
True agency is impossible without will and choice.

Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers by focusing on the very meaning of 'agent'. He argues that agency is not simply being a cause; it requires intentional will. The following passage presents his first objection and the ensuing debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the dialogue structure: Ghazali states his position, the philosophers respond, and Ghazali refutes.
2
Identify Ghazali's definition of agent
Ghazali defines the agent as one from whom the act proceeds with will, choice, and knowledge. Find this definition in the text.
This definition is the key to his objection.
3
Understand the philosophers' counter-argument
The philosophers claim that emanation by nature (like fire burning) is still action and making. Contrast this with Ghazali's definition.
They rely on a broader notion of causation.
4
Follow Ghazali's refutation
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own admission that inanimate objects have no action to argue that calling natural processes 'agency' is metaphorical.
He appeals to the universal proposition that action belongs only to living beings.
5
Examine the linguistic dispute and conclusion
The philosophers give Arabic examples of natural causality. Ghazali dismisses them as metaphorical. The passage ends with the philosophers clarifying that by 'agent' they mean 'cause'.
Notice the unresolved tension: the two sides use different concepts of agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
11 - مسألة في تلبسهم بقولهم إن الله فاعل العالم وصانعه , وأن العالم صنعه وفعله , وبيان أن ذلك مجاز عندهم , وليس بحقيقة . اتفقت الفلاسفة على أن للعالم صانعاً وأن العالم فعل الله وصنعته . قال الغزالي : وهذا غير متصور على مذهبهم من ثلاثة أوجه : الوجه الأول : من جهة الفاعل ; فإن الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل , مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار , ومع العلم بالمراد . وعندكم أن العالم صدر من الله تعالى صدور المعلول من العلة , ولزم منه لزوماً ضرورياً لا يتصور من الله دفعه . قال الفلاسفة : إن الصدور بالطبع فعل وصنع . قال الغزالي : هذا فاسد ; لما صح من قولهم : الجماد لا فعل له وإنما الفعل للحيوان , وذلك من الكليات المشهورة الصادقة ; فإن سمي الجماد فاعلاً فبالاستعارة ; وهذا يقتضي دخول الاختيار في مفهوم معنى الفاعل . قالت الفلاسفة : إن تسمية الفاعل فاعلاً تعرف من اللغة , وإلا فقد ظهر في العقل أن ما يكون سبباً للشيء ينقسم إلى ما يكون مريداً , وإلى ما لا يكون , ووقع النزاع في أن اسم الفاعل على كلا القسمين حقيقة , أم لا ? ... والعرب تقول : النار تحرق , والسيف يقطع , والثلج يبرد . إلخ . قال الغزالي : إن قولهم : النار تحرق , والسيف يقطع , تعبيرات مجازية , وإنما الفعل الحقيقي ما يكون بالإرادة . قالت الفلاسفة : نحن نعني كون الله فاعلاً أنه سبب لكل موجود .
11 - A problem about their being confused by their statement that God is the agent (fā'il) of the world and its maker, and that the world is His artifact and act, and showing that this is metaphorical for them, not literal. The philosophers agreed that the world has a maker and that the world is God's act and artifact. Al-Ghazali said: This is inconceivable according to their school from three aspects: The first aspect: from the aspect of the agent. For the agent is an expression for one from whom the act proceeds, with will (irāda) for the act by way of choice (ikhtiyār), and with knowledge of the intended. And according to you, the world proceeds from God most high as the effect proceeds from the cause, and it follows from it with a necessary following that God cannot repel. The philosophers said: The proceeding by nature (al-ṣudūr bi-l-ṭab') is action and making. Al-Ghazali said: This is corrupt; because it is established from their statement that the inanimate (al-jamād) has no action, and action is only for the living being, and this is among the well-known true universal propositions. So if the inanimate is called an agent, it is metaphorically. And this requires the inclusion of choice in the concept of the meaning of agent. The philosophers said: The naming of the agent as 'agent' is known from language. Otherwise, it has appeared in the intellect that what is a cause of a thing is divided into what is willing and what is not, and the dispute has occurred over whether the name 'agent' applies to both categories literally or not. And the Arabs say: fire burns, the sword cuts, snow cools, etc. Al-Ghazali said: Their statement 'fire burns' and 'sword cuts' are metaphorical expressions, and indeed true action is what is by will. The philosophers said: We mean by God being an agent that He is a cause of every existent.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. It begins with a heading: a problem showing that the philosophers' claim that God is the agent of the world is merely metaphorical on their principles. Al-Ghazali then states that this claim is inconceivable on their school from three aspects, but only the first aspect is presented here. The first aspect concerns the nature of the agent. Ghazali defines an agent as one who performs an act with will, choice, and knowledge of the intended. He points out that, according to the philosophers, the world proceeds from God as a necessary effect from a cause, without any possibility of repulsion. This contradicts the notion of agency that requires will. The philosophers respond by claiming that 'proceeding by nature' (such as fire burning) is still action and making. Ghazali refutes this by referencing the philosophers' own admission that inanimate objects have no action—action belongs only to living beings. Calling an inanimate object an agent is metaphorical, not literal. Therefore, since God's emanation is likened to natural causes, calling God an agent would be metaphorical. The philosophers then argue that the term 'agent' is linguistic and can apply to both voluntary and non-voluntary causes, giving examples from Arabic (fire burns, sword cuts). Ghazali counters that these are metaphorical, and true action requires will. Finally, the philosophers clarify that by calling God an agent, they simply mean He is a cause of all existents. This passage highlights the crucial difference between voluntary agency and natural causation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers and theologians agree on the meaning of God as agent — it means He created with will.
Correction: Ghazali shows that philosophers reduce agency to necessary causation without will, making it metaphorical, while true agency requires will and choice.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot create the world.
B) The philosophers deny God's existence.
C) The philosophers' concept of agency excludes will and choice, making it metaphorical.
D) The world is not created by God.

Key Takeaway
True agency requires will and choice; without them, calling God an agent is merely metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Problem of Multiplicity in the First Emanation
Ghazali challenges whether the first emanation's intellections are identical to its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that a necessary multiplicity arises in the first emanation if its intellection of itself and its principle are distinct, and why Ibn Sina rejected the alternative view.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in the First Emanation reveals a deeper problem in the philosophers' system.

In this passage, Ghazali presses one of his most incisive objections against the emanationist model. He asks a simple question about the first produced intellect: does its intellection of its source (the First Principle) differ from its own existence? The answer leads to a dilemma that either denies multiplicity in the intellect or introduces it into the First itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Start by reading Ghazali's opening question: is the intellection of the first emanation identical to its existence or other? Notice the two horns that follow.
2
Examine the philosophers' claim
Ghazali presents the philosophical position that the First only intellects itself and that intellection is identical to essence. See how this attempts to avoid multiplicity.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Ghazali cites Ibn Sina and other verifiers abandoning this view due to its repugnance. Consider what 'repugnance' means in this context.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Ghazali argues that the fear of multiplicity leads to an even greater multiplicity when applied to the first effect. Trace this reasoning.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this objection undermines the neat emanationist scheme. Why does multiplicity appear even at the first stage?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : هل عقله مبدؤه عين وجوده ؟ وعين عقله نفسه ؟ أم غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه ، فلا كثرة في ذاته وإن كان غيره ، فهذه الكثرة موجودة في الأول ... فإن قال الفلاسفة : الأول لا يعقل إلا ذاته ، وعقله ذاته هو عين ذاته ... قيل في جوابه : أولا : إن هذا المذهب لشناعته هجره ابن سينا ، وسائر المحققين وثانياً : أن من ذهب إلى أن الأول لا يعقل إلا نفسه ، إنما حاذر من لزوم الكثرة ؛ إذ لو قال به لزمه أن يقال : عقله غيره ، غير عقله نفسه . . . فكذلك يقال في المعلول الأول
And among them: Is its intellection of its principle identical to its existence? And is its intellection of itself identical to itself or other? If it is identical, then there is no multiplicity in its essence; if it is other, then this multiplicity exists in the first... If the philosophers say: The First does not intellect except its essence, and its intellection of its essence is identical to its essence... It is said in answer: First, this doctrine, due to its ugliness, was abandoned by Ibn Sina and all the verifiers. Second, whoever holds that the First does not intellect except itself only feared the necessary consequence of multiplicity; for if he asserted it, it would necessarily be said: its intellection of other than itself is other than its intellection of itself. So likewise it is said concerning the first effect.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage contains two related objections. First, Ghazali asks about the first emanation: is its intellection of its principle the same as its existence? And is its intellection of itself the same as itself or different? If the same, then there is no multiplicity in its essence (contrary to the three aspects). If different, then multiplicity already appears in the first intellectual being. Then Ghazali considers a potential defense from the philosophers: they might claim that the First (God) only intellects His own essence, and that intellection is identical to His essence. Ghazali responds: (1) Ibn Sina and other serious thinkers have abandoned this repugnant view; (2) those who hold that the First only knows Himself do so out of fear of multiplicity, but if they assert that, then they must concede that His knowledge of something other than Himself is different from His knowledge of Himself. And the same reasoning applies to the first effect (the first intellect), leading to similar problems.

كثرة
ابن سينا

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers were unanimous that the First only knows itself.
Correction: Ghazali points out that Ibn Sina and other verifiers abandoned this view due to its repugnance.

B) Because of its ugliness (شناعته)
C) Because Ibn Sina believed the First knows everything
D) Because Ibn Sina was not a philosopher

Key Takeaway
Ghazali forces the philosophers to admit that their system either denies multiplicity in the first intellect or introduces it into the First Principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The philosophers feared multiplicity in the First, yet their own system introduced it. In what areas of your faith do you find yourself creating unnecessary complications while trying to preserve simplicity? Will you commit to examining those areas today?

Ghazali's citation of Ibn Sina's abandonment shows even great thinkers can err. Is there a belief you hold simply because a respected scholar held it, without critically examining its coherence? What would it cost you to reconsider it in light of this lesson?

Refuting the Identification of God's Intellect with His Essence
Ghazali refutes the identification of God's intellect with His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two-pronged refutation of the philosophers' view that the First's intellect is identical to His essence, and why he considers this view repugnant and self-defeating.

Core Idea
The First's intellect cannot be identical to His essence without implying multiplicity.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that the First only knows Himself and that His knowledge is identical to His essence. He argues that this view was abandoned even by Ibn Sina and leads to the very multiplicity it seeks to avoid. In this passage, he presents two devastating reasons why the identification fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the structure: an objection followed by a two-part refutation.
2
Identify the philosophical claim
Notice the claim: the First's intellect is identical to His essence, and He only knows Himself. This is meant to avoid multiplicity.
3
Examine the first refutation
Ghazali points out that this repugnant doctrine was abandoned by Ibn Sina and other verifiers. This undermines its authority.
Ibn Sina's rejection is significant because he is a leading philosopher.
4
Examine the second refutation
If the First only knows Himself, then His knowledge of anything else would be distinct from His self-knowledge, introducing multiplicity. Thus the view fails.
This shows the view is internally inconsistent.
5
Reflect on the implication for the first caused
The same reasoning applies to the first intellect (the first caused), extending the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : هل عقله مبدؤه عين وجوده ؟ وعين عقله نفسه ؟ أم غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه ، فلا كثرة في ذاته وإن كان غيره ، فهذه الكثرة موجودة في الأول فإن قال الفلاسفة : الأول لا يعقل إلا ذاته ، وعقله ذاته هو عين ذاته قيل في جوابه : أولا : إن هذا المذهب لشناعته هجره ابن سينا ، وسائر المحققين وثانياً : أن من ذهب إلى أن الأول لا يعقل إلا نفسه ، إنما حاذر من لزوم الكثرة ؛ إذ لو قال به لزمه أن يقال : عقله غيره ، غير عقله نفسه . . . فكذلك يقال في المعلول الأول
And among them: Is His intellect—its principle—identical to His existence? And is His intellect of Himself identical to His essence or other than it? For if it is identical to Him, then there is no multiplicity in His essence; and if it is other than Him, then this multiplicity exists in the First. Then the philosophers said: The First does not intellect except His own essence, and His intellection of His essence is identical to His essence. It was said in response: First, this doctrine, due to its repulsiveness, was abandoned by Ibn Sina and all the verifying scholars. And second, he who held that the First does not intellect except Himself only feared the occurrence of multiplicity; for if he had stated it, it would necessitate that one say: His intellection of other than Himself is other than His intellection of Himself. Likewise is said concerning the first caused.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts: an objection and a refutation. The objection raises the question of whether the First's intellect is identical to His existence and whether His intellection of Himself is identical to His essence. If identical, no multiplicity; if different, multiplicity arises in the First. The philosophers respond by claiming that the First only intellects Himself, and that intellection is identical to His essence. Ghazali refutes this in two ways. First, he notes that even Ibn Sina and other verifiers abandoned this view due to its repulsiveness. Second, he argues that those who hold this view only did so to avoid multiplicity, but their position actually leads to the same problem: if the First intellects something other than Himself, that intellection would be distinct from His intellection of Himself, implying multiplicity. The same applies to the first caused intellect.

Objection on Self-Knowledge of the First Caused Intellect
Ghazali questions whether the first caused intellect's knowledge of itself is identical to its essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the first caused intellect's knowledge of itself cannot be identical to its essence, and how this objection applies to the First Principle.

Core Idea
If knowledge and essence are distinct for the first caused, they must also be distinct for Allah.

This passage presents a sharp objection from Ghazali against the philosophers' model of emanation. He questions a key assumption: that the first caused intellect's self-knowledge is identical to its essence. By analyzing two possible answers, Ghazali exposes a contradiction that undermines the philosophers' claim about the First Principle's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note that Ghazali presents a dilemma: either the intellect's knowledge is identical to its essence or it is not.
The objection is part of a larger argument against the philosophers.
2
Analyze the first possibility
If knowledge is identical to essence, Ghazali says it's impossible because knowledge is always distinct from the known object. Reflect on why this is impossible.
3
Analyze the second possibility
If knowledge is other than essence, then the same logic must apply to the First Principle. That contradicts the philosophers' claim that Allah's knowledge is identical to His essence.
This shows the inconsistency in their system.
4
Understand the broader implication
Ghazali's objection is not just about the first caused intellect; it forces the philosophers to admit that self-knowledge implies multiplicity in the First Principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : أن عقل المعلول الأول ذات نفسه ، عين ذاته ، أو غيره؟ فإن قيل عينه ، فذلك محال ؛ لأن العلم غير المعلوم ؛ وإن قيل غيره ، فليكن كذلك في المبدأ الأول
And among them: That the intellection of the first caused of its own self—is it identical to its essence or other than it? If it is said identical, that is impossible because knowledge is other than the known; and if it is said other, then let it be likewise in the First Principle.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents an objection concerning the first caused intellect (the first emanated intellect). The objection asks: Is the first caused intellect's knowledge of itself identical to its essence or distinct? If it is identical, that is impossible because knowledge is distinct from the known object. If it is distinct, then the same applies to the First Principle: His knowledge of Himself would be distinct from His essence. This undermines the philosophers' claim that the First's intellect is identical to His essence.

معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali is denying self-knowledge altogether or that the objection is about the possibility of self-knowledge in the first caused intellect.
Correction: The objection is not about the possibility of self-knowledge but about the logical impossibility of knowledge being identical to the knower's essence. Even if self-knowledge occurs, it must be distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the first caused intellect's knowledge of itself is not identical to its essence?
A) The first caused would have multiplicity
B) The same must apply to the First Principle
C) Knowledge would be impossible
D) The first caused would not exist

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's dilemma forces the philosophers to admit that if the first caused intellect's self-knowledge is distinct from its essence, then the same must be true for Allah, contradicting their own position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own self-knowledge, do you ever distinguish between your 'self' and your 'knowledge of yourself'? How does this distinction affect your understanding of who you are before Allah?
?
If you were pressed to defend the position that Allah's knowledge is identical to His essence, how would you answer Ghazali's dilemma? Would your answer show reliance on reason or on revelation?
?
The passage reveals a fundamental limit: human logic cannot fully capture the nature of the Creator. Are you comfortable submitting to that limit, or do you find yourself wanting to fully reason out God's attributes? What does that say about your heart?

Five Types of Multiplicity Denied of God
Ghazali enumerates five types of multiplicity denied of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of multiplicity that philosophers deny of God and why each is incompatible with divine simplicity.

Core Idea
God transcends all forms of composition—physical, conceptual, attributive, generic, and essential.

Ghazali begins by presenting the philosophers' classification of multiplicity, establishing that God is free from every kind of composition. This lesson introduces the five distinct types of multiplicity that are negated of the First Principle, setting the stage for understanding absolute divine oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the five types in Arabic
Read the Arabic passage listing the five types once without translating. Notice the repetitive structure emphasizing negation.
Each type begins with 'النوع ...' and ends with 'منفي/منفية عن...'
2
Identify each type's definition
For each of the five types, identify what kind of multiplicity is being denied and why it is considered impossible for God.
Type 1: physical division. Type 2: conceptual division into matter and form. Type 3: multiplicity of attributes. Type 4: composition of genus and differentia. Type 5: composition of essence and existence.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on the common thread: all five types imply some form of composition (tarkib) in God, which would make Him dependent on parts. The philosophers assert that God is absolutely simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكثرة خمسة أنواع:
النوع الأول : قبول الانقسام فعلا، أو وهما في الوهم، وهو محال على المبدأ الأول.
النوع الثانى : قبول الانقسام فى العقل إلى معنيين مختلفين، لا بطريق الكمية، كانقسام الجسم إلى الهيولى والصورة، وهذا النوع أيضاً منفى عن الله تعالى.
النوع الثالث : الكثرة بالصفات بتقدير العلم والقدرة والإرادة. وهذا النوع أيضاً منفى عن الواجب.
النوع الرابع : كثرة عقلية تحصل بتركب الجنس والفصل، وهذا أيضاً منفى عن الواجب.
النوع الخامس : كثرة تلزم من تقدير ماهية وتقدير وجود زائد عن تلك الماهية. وهذه الكثرة أيضاً منفية عن الواجب.
قالت الفلاسفة : ذات المبدأ الأول واحدة، وإنما تكثر الأسماء بإضافة شيء إليه، أو إضافته إلى شيء، أو سلب شيء عنه. ولا الإضافة والسلب لا كثرة في ذات المسلوب عنه توجب كثرة. فلا ينكر كثرة الأسلوب، ولا كثرة الإضافات. فإذا قيل له [ أول ] فهو إضافة إلى الموجودات بعده. وإذا قيل ( موجود ) فمعناه [ معلوم ] . . . إلخ.
And multiplicity (al-kathra) is of five types:
The first type: Accepting division actually, or in imagination (wahm), and that is impossible for the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal).
The second type: Accepting division in the intellect into two different meanings, not by way of quantity, like the division of the body into matter (hayula) and form (sura), and this type is also negated of God.
The third type: Multiplicity by attributes (sifat) such as knowledge, power, and will. And this type is also negated of the Necessary.
The fourth type: Intellectual multiplicity arising from the composition of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl), and this is also negated of the Necessary.
The fifth type: Multiplicity entailed by positing an essence (mahiyya) and positing an existence additional to that essence. And this multiplicity is also negated of the Necessary.
The philosophers said: The essence (dhat) of the First Principle is one, but names multiply by adding something to it, or by adding it to something, or by negating something from it. Neither negation nor relation causes multiplicity in the essence of that from which something is negated. So multiplicity of negations and multiplicity of relations should not be denied. For when it is said of Him 'First' (awwal), it is a relation to the existents after Him. And when it is said 'Existent' (mawjud), its meaning is 'known' (ma'lum), etc.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the five types of multiplicity that the philosophers deny of God, followed by an explanation of how divine names can be many without implying multiplicity in essence. The five types are: (1) physical division—actual or imaginary—impossible for the First Principle; (2) conceptual division into different meanings, like matter and form in a body; (3) multiplicity of attributes such as knowledge, power, and will; (4) intellectual composition of genus and differentia; (5) composition of essence and an existence added to it. The philosophers then assert that God's essence is one, and names multiply only through relations (e.g., 'First' relates to what comes after) or negations (e.g., 'Existent' negates non-existence, meaning 'known'). Neither relations nor negations introduce multiplicity into the essence itself.

Key Terms
الكثرة
انقسام
هيولى
صورة
صفات
جنس
فصل
ماهية
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny all forms of multiplicity absolutely, leaving no room for any divine names or attributes.
Correction: They specifically deny types implying composition (tarkib) but later affirm relational and negative multiplicity for names.

Checkpoint
Which type of multiplicity involves the composition of genus and differentia?
A) The first type
B) The third type
C) The fourth type
D) The fifth type

Key Takeaway
God is free from five types of multiplicity that imply composition, affirming absolute unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever imagine Him as having parts or being composed of attributes? If so, what mental image must you uproot to truly affirm His absolute oneness? Are you willing to let go of any concept that reduces His majesty to a composite being?
?
The fifth type teaches that existence is not an addition to God's essence. How does your own existence depend on God every moment? Do you ever feel you 'own' your existence as an independent possession? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly lived knowing your essence and existence are inseparable from God's sustaining will?
?
If God's attributes are identical with His essence, then when you praise His knowledge or power, you are praising Him directly. Does your heart ever divide its reverence, honoring God's power separately from His love? How can you unify your worship so that every quality you adore in Him is a direct encounter with His single, indivisible reality?

How Divine Names Multiply Without Multiplicity in Essence
Ghazali explains how names can be many without multiplicity in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers explain the multiplicity of divine names through relations and negations without compromising the oneness of God's essence.

Core Idea
Names multiply through relations and negations, not through multiplicity in essence.

After enumerating the types of multiplicity denied of God, Ghazali explains the philosophers' solution: many names can be predicated of God without implying any real plurality in His essence. This lesson unpacks how relations (e.g., 'First') and negations (e.g., 'Existent') allow for a rich theological language while preserving divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophical explanation
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قالت الفلاسفة' to see how they respond to the problem of multiple names.
Notice the key phrase: 'وإنما تكثر الأسماء بإضافة شيء إليه، أو إضافته إلى شيء، أو سلب شيء عنه'
2
Identify the three sources of names
The passage identifies three ways names are derived: addition of something to God (relation from Him to creation), addition of God to something (relation from creation to Him), and negation of something from God.
Examples: 'First' is a relation to what comes after; 'Existent' is a negation of non-existence.
3
Understand why relations and negations don't cause multiplicity
Relations and negations are mental considerations that do not add any real ontological multiplicity to the essence. The essence remains one.

The Five Types of Multiplicity Negated from God
Enumeration of five types of multiplicity negated from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of multiplicity that philosophers negate from the First Principle to preserve divine unity.

Core Idea
God’s oneness excludes all forms of composition — physical, conceptual, and attributive.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' doctrine that multiplicity in any form cannot apply to God. He enumerates five distinct types of multiplicity, each representing a way something could be multiple or composite. Understanding these negations clarifies the philosophers' concept of absolute divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the complete passage from 'And multiplicity is of five types' through the fifth type. Try to identify each type as you read.
The passage is enumerative; note the numbering.
2
Identify the five types
List each type: 1) physical division, 2) matter-form composition, 3) multiplicity of attributes like knowledge, 4) genus-species composition, 5) essence-existence distinction.
Each type is introduced by 'النوع' plus ordinal.
3
Reflect on the common thread
Notice that all types involve composition or multiplicity that would compromise God's simplicity. Think about why each is considered incompatible with the First Principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقسام الجسم · والكثرة خمسة أنواع : النوع الأول : قبول الانقسام فعلا ، أو وهما في الوهم ، وهو محال · المبدأ الأول النوع الثانى : قبول الانقسام فى العقل إلى معنيين مختلفين ، لا بطريق الكمية ، كانقسام الجسم إلى الهيولى والصورة ، وهذا النوع أيضاً منفى عن الله تعالى النوع الثالث : الكثرة بالصفات بتقدير العلم والقدرة والإرادة . وهذا النوع أيضاً منفى عن الواجب النوع الرابع : كثرة عقلية تحصل بتركب الجنس والفصل ، وهذا أيضاً منفى عن الواجب النوع الخامس : كثرة تلزم من تقدير ماهية وتقدير وجود زائد عن تلك الماهية . وهذه الكثرة أيضاً منفية عن الواجب قالت الفلاسفة : ذات المبدأ الأول واحدة ، وإنما تكثر الأسماء بإضافة شيء إليه ، أو إضافته إلى شيء ، أو سلب شيء عنه وجب 6 ولا الإضافة والسلب لا كثرة في ذات المسلوب عنه توجب كثرة . فلا ينكر ون كثرة الأسلوب [؟] ، ولا كثرة الإضافات . فإذا قيل له [ أول ] فهو إضافة إلى الموجودات بعده . وإذا قيل ( موجود ) فمعناه [ معلوم ] . . . إلخ .
Division of the body. And multiplicity is of five types: the first type: acceptance of division actually, or in estimation (wahm), and that is impossible. The first principle. The second type: acceptance of division in the intellect into two different meanings, not by way of quantity, like the division of the body into matter and form. And this type also is negated from God most high. The third type: multiplicity by attributes, namely knowledge, power, and will. And this type also is negated from the Necessary. The fourth type: intellectual multiplicity resulting from composition of genus and differentia. And this also is negated from the Necessary. The fifth type: multiplicity entailed by positing an essence and positing an existence additional to that essence. And this multiplicity also is negated from the Necessary. The philosophers said: The essence of the first principle is one, but names multiply by attributing something to it, or its attribution to something, or negation of something from it. And it is necessary? 6. Negation and relation do not entail multiplicity in the essence of that from which something is negated. So they deny multiplicity of modes? and multiplicity of relations. So if He is called 'First', it is a relation to the existents after Him. And if He is called 'Existent', its meaning is 'known'... etc.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of five types of multiplicity (kathra) that the philosophers negate from the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) in order to preserve His absolute unity. The author lists these types in sequence. The first type is the acceptance of division actually or in estimation (wahm), which is impossible for God. The second type is intellectual division into two different meanings not quantitative, such as matter and form in bodies; this is also negated. The third type is multiplicity by attributes like knowledge, power, and will – negated. The fourth type is multiplicity from composition of genus and differentia – negated. The fifth type is multiplicity from essence and existence as distinct – negated. Then the philosophers explain that the essence is one, and names multiply only through external relations, attributions, or negations, which do not affect the essence's unity. For example, 'First' indicates a relation to later existents, and 'Existent' means 'known' (i.e., known to us). The text then notes a return to further discussion of these types and their refutation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only physical multiplicity is negated from God.
Correction: All five types of multiplicity—physical, compositional, attributive, definitional, and existential—are negated.

Key Takeaway
God is free from all five forms of multiplicity: physical division, matter-form composition, multiple attributes, genus-differentia composition, and essence-existence distinction.

?

The Philosophers' Defense of Divine Unity: Relations and Negations
Philosophers justify multiple names for God through relations and negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers justify attributing multiple names to God without compromising His essential unity.

Core Idea
Relations and negations do not add real multiplicity to God’s essence.

After listing the five negated multiplicities, Ghazali reports the philosophers' positive argument: despite affirming unity, we call God by many names. They explain that these names arise either from a relation (like 'First' in relation to others), a negation (like 'Holy' meaning not impure), or from something attributed to Him. Such relations and negations do not multiply His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
Read from 'The philosophers said' to the end of the passage. Notice their claim that the essence is one and names multiply through external factors.
The passage contains a list of examples: 'First', 'Existent'.
2
Identify the three sources of names
The philosophers identify three ways names come about: by something being attributed to Him, by His attribution to something (relation), or by negation. Find each in the text.
The text gives 'First' as relation; 'Existent' as known (maybe attribution).
3
Understand why relations and negations don't imply composition
Reflect on why a relation like 'being first relative to creation' does not add an entity to God's essence. It is a mind-dependent relation.

?

?
Are there any names of Allah that you have treated as if they are independent attributes, forgetting that they all point to the same indivisible Divine Being? What will you change today?

First Argument Against Attributes: Independence and Its Refutation
Ghazali presents and refutes the philosophers' first argument against attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first argument against divine attributes and Ghazali's critical distinction between efficient and receptive causation in his refutation.

Core Idea
Dependence of attributes does not undermine God's necessity.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the first of two arguments used by philosophers to deny that God has attributes. He then offers a nuanced refutation that distinguishes between different types of causal dependence. This discussion is crucial for understanding the Islamic position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' trichotomy
Read the three logical possibilities when an attribute and its subject are distinct: both independent, both dependent, or one dependent on the other. Notice why the philosophers reject the first two.
The philosophers aim to prove that no attribute can be affirmed of the Necessary Being.
2
Identify Ghazali's choice
See that Ghazali selects the third possibility: the essence is independent, while attributes depend on the essence. This preserves God's necessity.
3
Examine the distinction between causes
Focus on Ghazali's objection: 'needy' does not automatically disqualify something from being necessary. He separates efficient cause (what creates) from receptive cause (what receives). The essence has no efficient cause, but attributes have a receptive cause (the essence).
This is the key insight that undermines the philosophers' argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
14 - مسألة اتفقت الفلاسفة على استحالة إثبات العلم والقدرة والإرادة للمبدأ ، كما اتفقت عليه المعتزلة . . . ولهم في إثباته مسلكان : الأول المسلك الأول : أنهم قالوا : إن كل واحد من الصفة والموصوف ، إذا لم يكن هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا : فإما أن يستغنى كل واحد منهما عن الآخر في وجوده واجبا وجود . . . وذاك محال . . . لأنها اثنينية فهما أو فتقر كل واحد إلى الآخر فلا يكون واحد منهما واجب ... أو مستغنى واحد ، ويفتقر واحد وجود ... معلول ، فلا يكون واجب وجود ... فما احتاج فهو ، قال الغزالى : والاعتراض على هذا أن يقال : نختار القسم الأخير فإن الذات فى قوامها غير محتاجة إلى الصفات ، والصفات محتاجة إلى الموصوف . وقولهم : إن المحتاج لا يكون واجب وجود ، يقال في رده ، إن أردتم بواجب الوجود ما لا علة له فاعله ، فما معنا كذلك و إن أردتم به ما لاعلة له قابلة . . . فما الدليل على استحالة هذا؟
14 – Issue: The philosophers agreed on the impossibility of affirming knowledge, power, and will for the First Principle, as the Mu'tazila also agreed. And they have two approaches in affirming this: the first approach: that they said: Every one of the attribute and the qualified, when this is not that and that is not this, then either each one of them is independent of the other in its existence, both being necessary in existence – and that is impossible because it is duality; or each needs the other, so neither of them is necessary; or one is independent and the other needs [the first] – then the needy is an effect, so it is not necessary of existence, for whatever is needy [is not necessary]. Al-Ghazali said: The objection to this is to say: We choose the last division, for the essence in its constitution is not in need of attributes, while attributes are in need of the qualified. And their statement: 'The needy cannot be necessary of existence' – in refutation of it, it is said: If by necessary of existence you mean that which has no efficient cause, then that is what we mean. And if you mean that which has no receptive cause, then what is the evidence for the impossibility of this?
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a segment of a larger dispute concerning the affirmation of attributes for the First Principle. It states that the philosophers and the Mu'tazila agree on the impossibility of affirming knowledge, power, and will for God. Then it introduces the first of two arguments (maslak) that the philosophers use to prove this. The argument proceeds as follows: When we have an attribute and a subject that are not identical, three cases are possible: (1) both are independent of each other in existence – this would imply two necessary beings, which is impossible; (2) each depends on the other – then neither is necessary; (3) one is independent and the other depends on it – the dependent one is an effect, thus not necessary of existence. The philosophers conclude that the needful cannot be necessary. Al-Ghazali then raises an objection: He chooses the third case, asserting that the essence is independent, while attributes depend on it. He then challenges the philosophers' claim that what is needy cannot be necessary. He distinguishes two meanings of 'necessary of existence': if they mean 'that which has no efficient cause', then Muslims agree; if they mean 'that which has no receptive cause', then why is it impossible? This is a key critique distinguishing different types of causal dependence.

Key Terms
الصفة
الموصوف
واجب الوجود
معلول
العلة الفاعلة
العلة القابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali agrees with the philosophers that the needy cannot be necessary. In fact, he shows they equivocate on 'need' by distinguishing efficient and receptive causation.
Correction: Ghazali accepts that the essence is not needy in terms of efficient cause, but attributes are needy in terms of receptive cause; this does not make God contingent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's objection, what does the term 'necessary of existence' mean for Muslims?
A) That which has no receptive cause
B) That which has no efficient cause
C) That which has no cause at all
D) That which is independent in existence

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends divine attributes by showing that the philosophers' argument conflates efficient and receptive causality, allowing attributes to be dependent without making God contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this lesson, did you ever feel that affirming attributes for God required a compromise in His absolute oneness? Now that you have seen Ghazali's distinction between efficient and receptive cause, ask yourself: Is my heart fully at peace with the idea that God's attributes do not limit His independence, or do I still secretly harbor doubts? Write down one area where you need to strengthen your conviction.
?
The philosophers argued that any form of dependence negates necessity. In your own life, how often do you view reliance on Allah as a weakness rather than the very essence of servitude? This lesson shows that dependence on the Necessary Being is not a flaw but a mark of creation. Identify one relationship or situation where you are tempted to seek independence from Allah, and resolve to turn that area into a conscious act of dependence.
?
Ghazali's refutation hinges on a precise understanding of causation. When you face trials, do you sometimes look for secondary causes (people, circumstances) as if they are independent? This lesson warns against confusing receptive and efficient causes. Think of a recent difficulty: Did you blame an 'efficient cause' other than Allah? Commit to recounting that event and seeing Allah's sole efficient causality in it.

Second Approach: Analogy with Human Attributes
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second argument from human attributes and critiques it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second argument against divine attributes based on analogy with human attributes, and Ghazali's critique that the philosophers cannot avoid attributing an additional quality to God.

Core Idea
Analogy with human attributes fails because even philosophers must admit an attribute in God.

The philosophers offer a second argument against divine attributes: in humans, knowledge and power are not part of our essence, so the same must hold for God. Ghazali quickly points out an inconsistency: the philosophers themselves affirm that God is 'knowing,' which appears to be an attribute added to His essence. In response, they refine their position, claiming that God knows only Himself as a principle, not others directly. This lesson unpacks the argument, objection, and defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the three distinct voices: the philosophers' argument, Ghazali's objection, and the philosophers' rejoinder.
The passage is dense; take your time with each part.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
The philosophers argue that just as knowledge and power are not part of our essence, they cannot be part of God's essence. This would imply that if God had attributes, they would be additional, leading to composition.
3
Find Ghazali's objection
Ghazali objects that the philosophers themselves cannot avoid admitting an attribute: they affirm that God is 'knowing', which seems additional to mere existence. If He is knowing, then knowledge is added to His essence.
This undermines their claim that all descriptions reduce to essence.
4
Understand the philosophers' defense
The philosophers respond that God does not know others 'by first intention' but knows His own essence as a principle. This means His knowledge is not really about others but is identical with His self-awareness as the source of all things.
They attempt to avoid multiplicity by making knowledge equivalent to essence.
5
Reflect on the critique
Consider whether the defense is successful. Ghazali would argue that even self-knowledge is an attribute distinct from the essence itself, so the problem remains.
This shows the tension between affirming omniscience and denying attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسالك الثانى : قولهم : إن العلم والقدرة فينا لبسا داخلين في ماهية ذاتنا، فتكون كذلك فى حق الأول ... وهذا المسلك راجع للأول أن قال الغزالي : إنهم لا يقدرون على رد جميع ما يصفون به الذات ، إلى نفس الذات ، فإنهم أثبتوا له [ كونه عالماً ] ويلزمهم يكون ذلك زائداً على مجرد الوجود قالت الفلاسفة : هو لا يعلم الغير بالقصد الأول ، بل يعلم ذاته مبدأ .
The second approach: their statement: Knowledge and power in us are not included in the essence of our selves, so they are likewise in the case of the First... And this approach reduces to the first. Al-Ghazali said: They are not able to reduce all that they describe the Essence with back to the Essence itself, for they affirm for Him [His being knowing] and it necessarily follows that that is additional to mere existence. The philosophers said: He does not know the other by first intention, but rather He knows His essence as a principle.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (maslak) that the philosophers use to negate attributes from God, along with Ghazali's critique and the philosophers' rejoinder. The argument is based on analogy with human attributes: in us, knowledge and power are not part of our essence (māhiyya); they are additional. Therefore, the same should hold for God – if He had attributes, they would be additional, implying composition, which is impossible. Ghazali points out that the philosophers themselves cannot reduce all descriptions of the Essence to the Essence itself, because they affirm that He is 'knowing' – this is a description that seems additional to mere existence. The philosophers respond that He does not know others 'by first intention' (al-qasd al-awwal); rather, He knows His own essence as a principle (mabda'), implying that His knowledge is not really additional but the same as His essence. This defense attempts to avoid multiplicity, but Ghazali's objection remains: they still affirm a cognitive attribute.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الماهية
القصد الأول
مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers completely deny any knowledge in God.
Correction: The passage shows they actually affirm a self-knowledge that is identical with His essence, but they deny knowledge of others by first intention.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, how does God know others?
A) By first intention
B) By knowing His essence as a principle
C) Through acquired knowledge
D) He does not know anything other than Himself

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' second argument fails because they cannot avoid making 'knowing' an attribute additional to essence, despite their attempts to reduce it to self-knowledge.

The Philosophers' First Proof Against Attributes
Ghazali presents the philosophers' first disjunctive proof against multiple attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that multiple attributes in God would lead to dependence or plurality, undermining His necessary existence.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that distinct attributes make God dependent or duplicative.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the first philosophical objection against affirming real attributes distinct from God's essence. The philosophers use a disjunctive argument to show that if essence and attribute are distinct, then either both are independent (impossible), both dependent (also impossible), or one independent and one dependent—which they claim makes the dependent attribute contingent and thus not truly divine. This sets the stage for Ghazali's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the trilemma become clear.
Notice the use of 'إما أن' which introduces the three cases.
2
Identify the three cases in the disjunction
The passage presents three possibilities: both independent, both dependent, or one independent and one dependent. Find each one and note why each is claimed impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that in all cases, distinct attributes cannot be necessary. Ask yourself: why does this lead them to deny real attributes?
This connects to the broader debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الأول : أنهم قالوا : إن كل واحد من الصفة والموصوف ، إذا لم يكن هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا : فإما أن يستغنى كل واحد منهما عن الآخر في وجوده ... وذاك محال ... لأنها اثنينية فهما ... أو فتقر كل واحد إلى الآخر فلا يكون واحد منهما واجب ... أو مستغنى واحد ، ويفتقر واحد ... معلول ، فلا يكون واجب وجود ... فما احتاج فهو ، قال الغزالى : والاعتراض على هذا أن يقال : نختار القسم الأخير فإن الذات فى قوامها غير محتاجة إلى الصفات ، والصفات محتاجة إلى الموصوف . وقولهم : إن المحتاج لا يكون واجب وجود ، يقال في رده ، إن أردتم بواجب الوجود ما لا علة له فاعله ، فما معنا كذلك و إن أردتم به ما لاعلة له قابلة . . . فما الدليل على استحالة هذا؟
The first approach: They said: Every one of the attribute and the described, if this is not that and that is not this: then either each one of them is independent of the other in its existence... and that is impossible... because they are a duality... or each one needs the other, so neither is necessary... or one is independent and one is dependent... [the dependent is] caused, so it is not necessary existence... so whatever is dependent [is not necessary]. Al-Ghazali said: The objection to this is to say: We choose the last case, for the essence in its constitution is not in need of attributes, and attributes are in need of the described. And their saying: The dependent cannot be necessary existence, it is said in response: If by necessary existence you mean what has no efficient cause, then [the essence] is so. And if you mean what has no receptive cause, then what is the proof of its impossibility?
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the philosophers' first argument against attributing real attributes to God. The philosophers argue that if essence and attribute are distinct, then either both are independent (which would require two necessary existents, impossible), or both are dependent (so neither is necessary), or one is independent and the other dependent. The dependent one would be caused and thus not necessary. So no attribute can be co-eternal with essence. Ghazali chooses the third option: essence is independent, attributes are dependent on essence. He then challenges the notion of 'necessary existence' by distinguishing two kinds of cause: efficient cause (that which brings something into existence) and receptive cause (that which receives existence). The essence has no efficient cause, but it may still be receptive to attributes. So the philosophers have not proven that a dependent attribute is impossible if the dependence is on an essence that is itself uncaused.

صفة
موصوف
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Attributes are real and eternal.
B) Attributes cannot be necessary because they are dependent.
C) Essence and attribute are identical.
D) There are multiple necessary existents.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first proof uses a trilemma to argue that if attributes are distinct from essence, they cannot be necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this argument unsettle you? Commit to researching the Ash'ari view that attributes are not distinct in essence but distinct in meaning.
?
This trilemma forces a choice: either God is independent in every way or He is not God. Where in your life do you rely on something other than God? Does reflecting on God's absolute independence make you more aware of your own neediness? Write down one area where you can increase your reliance on Him today.
?
The argument assumes that any distinction implies dependence. Do you feel comfortable with that assumption? Have you ever felt that your own personal traits (like knowledge, kindness) make you independent or dependent? How does that help you understand the difference between human and divine attributes? Be honest: do you find the philosophers' logic compelling, or do you intuitively disagree? Pray for guidance to the truth.

Ghazali's Refutation: Distinguishing Efficient and Receptive Causes
Ghazali refutes the philosophers by distinguishing efficient from receptive causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the philosophers' first proof, specifically his distinction between efficient and receptive causes.

Core Idea
Ghazali challenges the notion of dependence by distinguishing efficient cause from receptive cause.

In this passage, Ghazali directly responds to the philosophers' first proof. He selects the third case of the trilemma—essence independent, attributes dependent—and then challenges the philosophers' assumption that dependence always negates necessary existence. By distinguishing between efficient cause (that which brings into existence) and receptive cause (that which receives existence), Ghazali shows that an attribute can be dependent on essence without implying contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's objection
Read the Arabic passage of Ghazali's response. Notice how he starts by choosing the third case.
2
Identify the distinction he makes
Ghazali distinguishes two meanings of 'necessary existence': one that has no efficient cause, and one that has no receptive cause. Find these two meanings in the text.
The terms are 'علة فاعلة' and 'علة قابلة'.
3
Understand how this refutes the philosophers
Attributes depend on essence as a receptive cause, not an efficient cause. So the essence is still necessary in the sense of having no efficient cause. Ask: does the philosophers' argument hold if dependence is only receptive?
This is the core of Ghazali's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
