Ghazali's Two Objections to the Philosophers' Theory of Divine Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God knows all by knowing His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two objections to the philosophers' claim that God knows all things by knowing His essence as their principle.

Core Idea
If God knows other things, His knowledge must be multiple, not one.

This passage presents the core of Ghazali's critique against the philosophers' theory of divine knowledge. The philosophers argued that God knows all things indirectly, but Ghazali exposes two fatal flaws: the arbitrariness of their claim and the unavoidable multiplicity it entails. Read on to see how Ghazali dismantles their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Start by reading the claim that God knows only His essence directly, and all else indirectly through knowing His essence as a principle.
This is the view Ghazali will attack.
2
Understand the first objection
Ghazali argues that the claim that His essence is a 'principle' for all things is arbitrary; why not simply say He knows His own existence?
This challenges the basis of the theory.
3
Understand the second objection
Even if we accept the first point, knowing multiple distinct objects requires multiple acts of knowledge, implying multiplicity in God.
This directly contradicts their denial of multiplicity.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider that Ghazali does not deny omniscience; he is rejecting a specific philosophical theory about how God knows.
The passage ends with a rhetorical flourish about infinite universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : هو لا يعلم الغير بالقصد الأول ، بل يعلم ذاته مبدأ للكل ، فيلزمه العلم بالكل بالقصد الثاني قال الغزالي : والجواب من وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن قولكم ، إنه يعلم ذاته مبدأ تحكم" ، بل ينبغي أن يعلم وجود ذاته فقط الوجه الثانى : أن قولهم ، إن الكل معلوم له بالقصد الثاني ، كلام غير معقول ؛ فإنه مهما كان علمه محيطاً بغيره ، كما يحيط بذاته ، كان له معلومان متغايران ، وكان له علم بهما ، وتعدد المعلوم يوجب تعدد العلم قال الغزالي : ليت شعرى كيف يقدم على نفى الكثرة من يقول : إنه لا يعزب عن علمه مثقال ذرة فى السموات ولا في الأرض ، إلا يعلم الكل بنوع كلى ، والكليات المعلومة لا تتناهى فكيف يكون العلم المتعلق بها واحدا من كل وجه ؟
The philosophers said: He does not know other than Himself by first intention, but He knows His essence as a principle for everything, so knowledge of everything follows by second intention. Al-Ghazali said: The answer is in aspects: The first aspect: Your saying that He knows His essence as a principle is arbitrary; rather, He should know only His own existence. The second aspect: Their saying that everything is known to Him by second intention is unintelligible speech; for whenever His knowledge encompasses other than Himself, just as it encompasses His essence, He has two distinct known objects, and He has knowledge of them, and multiplicity of the known necessitates multiplicity of knowledge. Al-Ghazali said: I wonder how one who says that not an atom's weight escapes His knowledge in the heavens or on earth can deny multiplicity? Does He not know everything in a universal way? And the known universals are infinite, so how can the knowledge related to them be one from every aspect?
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' claim about God's knowledge and Ghazali's two objections. The philosophers assert that God knows only His essence directly, but since His essence is the principle of all things, He knows all things indirectly. Ghazali first objects that this claim that His essence is a principle is arbitrary—why not say He knows only His existence? Second, even if He knows His essence and other things, those are two distinct objects, requiring two distinct acts of knowledge. He then points out the inconsistency: the philosophers claim God's knowledge is one, yet they admit He knows infinite universals, which seems to require multiple knowledge.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some might think Ghazali denies that God knows particulars or has omniscience.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying omniscience; he is arguing against the philosophers' specific theory of how God knows, not the fact of His knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what problem arises from the philosophers' claim that God knows everything by second intention?
A) It implies that God is ignorant.
B) It leads to multiplicity of knowledge.
C) It denies God's essence.
D) It is against revelation.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the philosophers' theory of divine knowledge leads to an unavoidable multiplicity, contradicting their own denial of multiplicity.

?

How does this lesson push you to affirm God's perfect knowledge without reducing His majesty to human categories?

Unified Knowledge of Multiple Objects
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that one knowledge can relate to multiple objects using the example of son and father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical debate about whether one knowledge can relate to multiple objects, and Ghazali's dialectical engagement with this issue.

Core Idea
One knowledge cannot relate to multiple distinct objects if it is truly one from every aspect.

The philosophers claimed that a single act of knowledge can encompass multiple objects, using the example of knowing a son and thereby knowing the father, paternity, and sonship. Ghazali refutes this by arguing that if knowledge is truly unitary, it cannot attach to two distinct knowns. This exchange leads to a critical counter-argument that challenges Ghazali's own position on God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim and example
Read the philosophers' statement that one knowledge can relate to multiple objects, and their example of knowing the son and thereby knowing the father, paternity, and sonship.
Notice how the example seems intuitive but conceals a logical issue.
2
Understand Ghazali's reply
Read Ghazali's reply that if knowledge is truly one in every aspect, it cannot relate to two different knowns. Consider why the example fails.
Ghazali implies that the knowledge of son and father are not identical in every aspect.
3
Reflect on the philosophers' counter-argument
The philosophers point out that Ghazali's own position on God's unitary knowledge of infinite objects faces the same problem. Think about how this challenge might be answered.
This dialectical move is a standard technique in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : ليس محالا أن يتحد العلم ويتعدد المعلوم ؛ فإن من علم الابن ، علم بنفس العلم ، الأب ، والأبوة ، والبنوة . قال الغزالي في رده : مهما كان العلم واحدا من كل وجه ، لم يتصور تعلقه بمعلومين . قالت الفلاسفة : إن هذا ينقلب عليكم في معلومات الله سبحانه وتعالى ، فإنها غير متناهية ، والعلم عندكم واحد

Al-Ghazali said in reply: Whenever knowledge is one from every aspect, it cannot be conceived to be related to two knowns. The philosophers said: This turns against you regarding the objects of God's knowledge, for they are infinite, and with you knowledge is one.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents a further exchange. The philosophers give an example: when you know a son, you automatically know the father, paternity, and sonship by the same act of knowledge. Therefore, one knowledge can have multiple objects. Ghazali replies that if the knowledge is truly one in every respect (no distinctions), it cannot relate to multiple objects. The example fails because the knowledge of the son is not identical in every respect to the knowledge of the father; they are different aspects. Then the philosophers counter that this argument applies to Ghazali's own position about God's knowledge being one while He knows infinite objects. But the passage does not give Ghazali's response here; it ends there. This is a typical dialectical move.

Correction: Ghazali is not denying divine knowledge's unity; he is arguing against the philosophers' specific claim that one knowledge can relate to multiple objects in the same way, and the counter-argument forces him to refine his own position.

Checkpoint

B) That the example is irrelevant.
C) That Ghazali has no proof.
D) That the philosophers have a better example.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that truly unitary knowledge cannot relate to multiple objects, but this raises a challenge for his own view of God's unitary knowledge of infinite things.

Ghazali's Two Answers on Divine Knowledge by Second Intention
Ghazali gives two arguments against philosophers' claim that God knows all by second intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two arguments against the philosophers' claim that God knows all things by a single knowledge via second intention.

Core Idea
Ghazali argues that the philosophers' model of divine knowledge collapses when its own logic is pushed.

Ghazali here gives two sharp responses to the philosophers' claim that God knows all things via a single intention aimed at His essence. The first challenges their premise about how God knows the world; the second forces a logical dilemma: multiple objects of knowledge require multiple acts of knowledge. Together, they expose a fatal contradiction in their theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct responses introduced by 'الوجه الأول' and 'الوجه الثاني'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find where each argument begins. The first argument is about the principle of knowledge, the second about the multiplicity of known objects.
3
Understand the first argument
Focus on Ghazali's claim that the philosophers' statement that God knows His essence as a principle leads to a contradiction. He says God should only know His existence.
This argument attacks the premise that God's knowledge of His essence implies knowledge of the world.
4
Understand the second argument
See how Ghazali argues that if God's knowledge encompasses another object, then there are two known objects, requiring two acts of knowledge. This refutes the claim of a single knowledge by second intention.
The key phrase: 'تعدد المعلوم يوجب تعدد العلم'.
5
Reflect on the overall logic
Consider how both arguments work together to show that the philosophers' conception of divine knowledge is incoherent.
Ghazali does not deny God's knowledge of particulars, only the specific philosophical model.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للكل ، فيلزمه العلم بالكل بالقصد الثاني قال الغزالي : والجواب من وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن قولكم ، إنه يعلم ذاته مبدأ تحكم" ، بل ينبغي أن يعلم وجود ذاته فقط الوجه الثانى : أن قولهم ، إن الكل معلوم له بالقصد الثاني ، كلام غير معقول ؛ فإنه مهما كان علمه محيطاً بغيره ، كما يحيط بذاته ، كان له معلومان متغايران ، وكان له علم بهما ، وتعدد المعلوم يوجب تعدد العلم
For the whole, so knowledge of the whole follows by second intention. Al-Ghazali said: The answer is in several aspects: The first aspect: that your statement that He knows His essence as a principle [is false]; rather, He should only know His existence. The second aspect: that their statement that the whole is known to Him by second intention is unintelligible; for whenever His knowledge encompasses another as it encompasses His essence, He has two distinct known things, and He has knowledge of them, and multiplicity of the known necessitates multiplicity of knowledge.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage provides two of al-Ghazali's responses (وجوه) to the philosophers' claim that God knows all things by a single knowledge via 'second intention' (بالقصد الثاني). The first response challenges the premise that God knows His essence as a principle (مبدأ) of others; al-Ghazali argues that if that were the case, He would know others, but the philosophers claim He only knows Himself. Instead, He should know only His own existence. The second response argues directly that if knowledge encompasses another object, then there are two distinct known objects (معلومان متغايران), which necessitates two acts of knowledge, contradicting the claim of a single, undivided divine knowledge. Al-Ghazali thus exposes a logical inconsistency in their position.

Key Terms
العلم بالقصد الثاني
تعدد المعلوم
تعدد العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ghazali denies God's knowledge of particulars altogether.
Correction: Al-Ghazali does not deny God's knowledge of particulars; he only argues against the specific philosophical model of that knowledge as being by second intention. His own position affirms God's comprehensive knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's second answer in this passage, what does multiplicity of the known (تعدد المعلوم) necessarily entail?
A) Unity of knowledge
B) Multiplicity of knowledge
C) Ignorance of particulars
D) Knowledge by second intention

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's two arguments aim to show that the philosophers' model of divine knowledge as a single intention directed at the world is logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge of your smallest actions, do you imagine it as a 'second intention'—something God knows only indirectly? How does Ghazali's argument challenge you to affirm that God knows directly and fully, without any second-hand knowledge? What might need to change in how you speak about God's awareness of your life?
?
Have you ever relied on a philosophical or mental model of God that, upon reflection, reduces His majesty to a logical system? How can you ensure that your understanding of God's attributes is shaped more by revelation and less by your own intellectual constructs?
?
If God's knowledge is one and simple, yet encompasses every detail, does that truth make you feel more accountable or more distant? How can you live today in a way that acknowledges that you are directly and fully known by the One whose knowledge is not divisible or indirect?

The Problem of Infinite Knowns
Al-Ghazali questions how one knowledge can encompass infinite universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's argument that an infinite number of known universals cannot be encompassed by a single, simple knowledge.

Core Idea
Infinite knowns cannot be known by one simple knowledge — a contradiction.

Al-Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is both all-encompassing and absolutely simple. He points to a logical tension: if the known universals are infinite, how can a single, uncomplex knowledge account for them? This rhetorical question foreshadows deeper issues with the philosophers' theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read al-Ghazali's rhetorical question in Arabic and English. Notice his tone of incredulity ('Would that I knew...').
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two claims in tension
The philosophers affirm both that God's knowledge has no multiplicity and that He knows all particulars (even an atom's weight). Al-Ghazali claims these cannot both be true if the known universals are infinite.
The key phrase is 'how can the knowledge related to them be one from every aspect?'
3
Reflect on the force of the objection
Ask yourself: Is al-Ghazali proving impossibility, or just highlighting a difficulty? Consider whether an infinite set could be known by a simple act of knowledge.
This is a preview of the wider debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي : ليت شعرى كيف يقدم على نفى الكثرة من يقول : إنه لا يعزب عن علمه مثقال ذرة فى السموات ولا في الأرض ، إلا يعلم الكل بنوع كلى ، والكليات المعلومة لا تتناهى فكيف يكون العلم المتعلق بها واحدا من كل وجه ؟
Al-Ghazali said: Would that I knew how one who says 'not an atom's weight escapes His knowledge in the heavens or the earth' dares to deny multiplicity, except that He knows the whole by a universal kind, and the universal knowns are infinite—so how can the knowledge related to them be one from every aspect?
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is al-Ghazali's own rhetorical objection (not part of the formal 'وجهان') to the philosophers' position. He points to a contradiction: they affirm that God knows all particulars (even an atom's weight) but claim His knowledge is one and simple. Al-Ghazali notes that the universals (كليات) known are infinite; how can an infinite number of knowns be known by a single knowledge that is one from every aspect? This is a separate, self-contained argument building on the previous responses.

العلم المتعلق بها

Correction: Al-Ghazali argues that infinite knowns logically require a corresponding multiplicity in knowledge, undermining the claim that divine knowledge is one from every aspect.

Checkpoint

C) God only knows universals

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's rhetorical objection highlights a logical tension: infinite knowns cannot be known by an absolutely simple knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Ghazali's Logical Critique of Ibn Sina on Divine Knowledge
Al-Ghazali points out an inconsistency in Ibn Sina's view on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency that al-Ghazali identifies in Ibn Sina's position on divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's position on divine knowledge is internally inconsistent: he denies multiplicity yet affirms God's knowledge of others.

In this brief passage, al-Ghazali exposes a tension in Ibn Sina's theology. While Ibn Sina shares with other philosophers the concern to avoid multiplicity in God, he diverges by claiming that God knows things other than Himself. Al-Ghazali's rhetorical question challenges the coherence of holding both positions simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and its literal translation carefully to grasp al-Ghazali's concise critique.
Notice the question format; it is rhetorical.
2
Identify the two positions
Other philosophers limit divine knowledge to self-knowledge. Ibn Sina agrees on denying multiplicity but affirms knowledge of others.
3
Understand the inconsistency
Why is it inconsistent to deny multiplicity yet affirm knowledge of others? Knowledge of others would seem to introduce some form of multiplicity.
This is the core of al-Ghazali's critique.
4
Reflect on the broader context
This critique is part of al-Ghazali's larger attack on the philosophers. Consider how this logical flaw affects Ibn Sina's system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي : وقد خالف ابن سينا فى هذا ، غيره من الفلاسفة الذين ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، احترازا من لزوم الكثرة فكيف شاركهم فى نفى الكثرة ؟ ثم باينهم في إثبات العلم بالغير ...؟
Al-Ghazali said: And Ibn Sina disagreed in this with other philosophers who held that He knows only Himself, out of caution against the implication of multiplicity. So how did he share with them in denying multiplicity, then differ from them in affirming knowledge of the other...?
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
In this brief but pointed critique, al-Ghazali highlights an inconsistency in Ibn Sina's position. Other philosophers, to avoid multiplicity in God, limited divine knowledge to self-knowledge only. Ibn Sina, while also denying multiplicity, affirmed that God knows others. Al-Ghazali asks how Ibn Sina can join them in denying multiplicity yet depart from them by affirming knowledge of others. The implication is that his position is internally inconsistent.

Key Terms
نفى الكثرة
إثبات العلم بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's position on divine knowledge is consistent and coherent.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows it is inconsistent because Ibn Sina both denies multiplicity and affirms knowledge of others, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint

A) Ibn Sina denies multiplicity but affirms knowledge of others
B) Ibn Sina affirms multiplicity but denies knowledge of others
C) Ibn Sina agrees with all other philosophers
D) Ibn Sina says God knows only Himself

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's rhetorical question reveals that Ibn Sina's attempt to avoid divine multiplicity while affirming God's knowledge of others is logically untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Ghazali on Unitary Knowledge and Multiple Objects
Philosophers object with an example; Ghazali responds with a logical principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the philosophers' claim that one knowledge can relate to multiple objects, focusing on the condition of absolute unity.

Core Idea
If knowledge is absolutely one, it cannot relate to two distinct objects.

The philosophers try to defend their position on divine attributes using an example from human knowledge: knowing a son implies knowing the father. But Ghazali immediately seizes on a logical flaw. Before we can assess their argument, we need to grasp the key distinction between knowledge that is one in every respect and knowledge that is only relatively one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection first, then response.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and example
The philosophers say it is not impossible for one knowledge to have multiple objects. Their example: knowing the son gives knowledge of the father, fatherhood, and sonship by the same act.
What is the implicit principle they are using?
3
Examine Ghazali's response
Ghazali states a condition: if knowledge is truly one from every aspect (واحدا من كل وجه), it cannot relate to two knowns. This condition is the crux.
The phrase 'from every aspect' (من كل وجه) is critical.
4
Reflect on why the example fails
Ask yourself: In the example, does knowing the son involve absolutely unitary knowledge? The commentary helps: the example involves relative unity, not absolute. Ghazali's principle applies to divine simplicity.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : ليس محالا أن يتحد العلم ويتعدد المعلوم ؛ فإن من علم الابن ، علم بنفس العلم ، الأب ، والأبوة ، والبنوة . قال الغزالي في رده : مهما كان العلم واحدا من كل وجه ، لم يتصور تعلقه بمعلومين .
The philosophers said: It is not impossible that knowledge be one and the known be multiple; for whoever knows the son, knows by the same knowledge the father, fatherhood, and sonship. Al-Ghazali said in response: Whenever knowledge is one from every aspect, it cannot be conceived to relate to two knowns.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents a classic objection from the philosophers and al-Ghazali's immediate rebuttal. The philosophers give an example: knowing the son automatically implies knowing the father and the relations (fatherhood, sonship) by the same act of knowledge. They argue this shows one knowledge can have multiple objects. Al-Ghazali counters that if knowledge is truly one from every aspect (واحدا من كل وجه)—simple and undivided—it cannot relate to two distinct knowns. The example, he implies, involves a unitary knowledge that is not absolutely simple, or the objects are not truly distinct in the way required.

Key Terms
اتحاد العلم
واحدا من كل وجه
تعلقه بمعلومين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ghazali denies that in human knowledge one concept can imply another, or that the philosophers' example is invalid for humans.
Correction: Al-Ghazali does not deny that in human knowledge one idea can lead to another; he is specifically addressing divine simplicity. His condition of absolute unity applies to God's knowledge, not to human knowledge which is composite.

Checkpoint

A) He accepts it as proof of multiple objects in one knowledge
B) He argues that if knowledge is absolutely one, it cannot relate to two knowns
C) He says the example is irrelevant to God
D) He claims the father is not known by the same knowledge

Key Takeaway
One absolutely simple knowledge cannot have multiple distinct objects; the philosophers' example fails because it involves relative unity, not absolute unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Denying Genus-Species Composition in God
Ghazali introduces the philosophers' claim that God has no genus-species composition and sets up its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' position that God (the First) has no genus-species composition and why Ghazali aims to refute it.

Core Idea
The philosophers strip God of genus-species structure, reducing common attributes to mere necessary concomitants.

In this issue, Ghazali presents the philosophers' view that God is incomposite by genus and species. They argue that when we say God exists, is a substance, or is a cause, these are not genus terms but 'common necessary attributes' that do not imply composition. Ghazali introduces this claim to set the stage for his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: 'جنس', 'فصل', 'لازم عام'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers assert that God cannot share a genus with others or be divided by species. Find the sentence that states this.
They deny any composition by genus and species in the First.
3
Understand the distinction
The philosophers say that God's participation with the First Effect in existence, substance, and causality is not in genus but in a 'common necessary attribute' (لازم عام). Observe how they distinguish between genus and necessary attribute.
This distinction is crucial for their defense of divine simplicity.
4
Recognize Ghazali's purpose
The title says 'فى إبطال قولهم' (refuting their statement). Ghazali is not presenting this as a truth claim but as a claim he will disprove. Keep this in mind as you proceed.
This is the opening of a refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥ - مسألة فى إبطال قولهم : إن الأول لا يجوز أن يشارك غيره في جنس ، ويفارقه بفصل ، وأنه لا يتطرق إليه انقسام في حق العقل بالجنس والفصل وقد اتفق الفلاسفة على ذلك ، وذهبوا إلى أن مشاركته للمعلول الأول في كونه موجوداً ، وجوهراً ، وعلة لغيره ، ليس مشاركة في الجنس ، بل فى لازم عام . وفرق بين الجنس واللازم
15 - Issue: On refuting their statement that the First cannot share with another in genus and differ by a species, and that no division by genus and species applies to Him in the view of reason. And the philosophers have agreed upon that, and they held that His participation with the First Effect in being existent, substance, and cause for another is not participation in genus, but in a common necessary attribute; and there is a difference between genus and necessary attribute.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage introduces the 15th issue of the Tahafut. The philosophers' claim is that God (the First) does not share a genus with any other being, nor does He have a species that distinguishes Him—meaning He is not composite in the way material beings are. The philosophers agree that when God is said to exist, be a substance, or be a cause, these are not genus terms but 'necessary concomitant attributes' (لازم عام). They distinguish between genus and such attributes. Al-Ghazali's aim in this issue is to refute this claim (فى إبطال قولهم). The passage sets the stage for the detailed refutation that follows in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
الجنس
الفصل
لازم عام
الانقسام بالجنس والفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the philosophers deny any commonality between God and creation, or that they treat existence, substance, and causality as mere homonyms.
Correction: The philosophers admit commonality but reinterpret it as 'common necessary attributes' (lazim 'amm), not genus. They do not deny commonality; they deny that it implies composition by genus.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, how do they explain God's participation with the First Effect in existence, substance, and causality?
A) It is participation in genus
B) It is participation in a common necessary attribute (لازم عام)
C) It is equivocal predication
D) It is mere analogy

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' avoidance of genus-species composition in God leads them to treat shared predicates (existence, substance, cause) as 'common necessary attributes' rather than true genera.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is existent' or 'God is a cause', do you truly believe these attributes are unique to Him in a way that no created being shares? The philosophers reduced them to mere necessary attributes. How does that affect the reverence in your heart when you recite His names?
?
The philosophers avoided saying God shares a genus with anything. But in your own mind, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as similar to something you know? What specific thought or image do you need to repent from?
?
If God's attributes are not like the categories we use for creation, then how should this change the way you speak about Him in your daily conversations? Are you willing to stop using casual descriptions of God that might imply similarity to creation?

The Philosophers' Claim and Ghazali's Demand for Proof
Ghazali introduces the philosophers' claim and his first approach (demand for proof).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the First cannot share genus and species, and Ghazali's first response demanding proof.

Core Idea
Ghazali demands proof before accepting the philosophers' denial of composition.

The philosophers assert that the First Being cannot be categorized by genus and species because that would imply composition, which they deem impossible. They distinguish between sharing in genus and sharing in a common necessary consequence. Ghazali begins his refutation by challenging the evidential basis of their claim, demanding that they prove why such composition is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Understand the philosophers' claim: the First cannot share genus or differ by differentia. Note their distinction between genus and necessary consequence.
They agree that the First shares existence with the first effect, but argue it is not generic sharing.
2
Identify their reasoning
The philosophers argue that sharing and differentiation would necessitate composition, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. This is their core argument.
3
Follow Ghazali's first approach
Ghazali demands proof: 'From where have you known the impossibility of this composition?' He refers to his earlier discussion on attributes.
This is a methodological challenge, not a substantive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥ - مسألة فى إبطال قولهم : إن الأول لا يجوز أن يشارك غيره في جنس ، ويفارقه بفصل ، وأنه لا يتطرق إليه انقسام في حق العقل بالجنس والفصل وقد اتفق الفلاسفة على ذلك ، وذهبوا إلى أن مشاركته للمعلول الأول في كونه موجوداً ، وجوهراً ، وعلة لغيره ، ليس مشاركة في الجنس ، بل فى لازم عام . وفرق بين الجنس واللازم قالوا : إن المشاركة والمباينة تقتضى التركيب ، والتركيب في حق الواجب ، محال قال الغزالى : ولنا معهم مسلكان : المسلك الأول المطالبة ، فنقول لهم ، من أين عرفتم استحالة هذا التركيب ؟ وأحال الغزالي في هذه المسألة إلى ما سبق له في مسألة زيادة الصفات المسلك الثانى : الإلزام ، فنقول لهم : الأول عقل مجرد ، والمعلول الأول ، عقل مجرد ، وحقيقة كل منهما هي هذه العقلية ، فهما مشتركان فى أمر جوهرى ذاتى ، ولا بد أن يختلفا فى أمر آخر . فالواجب إذن مركب
Question 15: Concerning the refutation of their statement that the First cannot share with another in genus and differ by differentia, and that division by genus and species cannot apply to Him in the intellect. The philosophers have agreed on this, and they hold that His sharing with the first effect in being existent, substance, and cause for others is not sharing in genus, but rather in a common necessary consequence. And they distinguish between genus and necessary consequence. They said: Sharing and differentiation necessitate composition, and composition is impossible with respect to the Necessary. Al-Ghazali said: We have two approaches with them: The first approach is the demand [for proof], so we say to them: From where have you known the impossibility of this composition? And al-Ghazali referred in this question to what preceded for him in the question of the increase of attributes. The second approach is the [showing of] obligation, so we say to them: The First is a pure intellect, and the first effect is a pure intellect, and the reality of each of them is this intellectuality, so they share in an essential, intrinsic matter, and they must differ in another matter. Therefore, the Necessary is composed.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (ibṭāl) of a philosophical claim. The philosophers assert that God (the First) cannot be described in terms of genus and species, nor differentiated by a differentia, because that would imply composition (tarkīb), which is impossible for the Necessary Being. They distinguish between sharing in a 'common necessary consequence' (lāzim 'āmm) and sharing in genus. Ghazali presents two approaches. The first is 'demand for proof' (muṭālaba): he asks them for evidence that such composition is impossible, referring back to his earlier discussion on the attributes. The second approach is 'obligation' (ilzām): he forces a contradiction. If the First is a pure intellect and the first effect (the first created intellect) is also a pure intellect, then both share an essential intrinsic reality ('aqlīyah). Since they are distinct, they must also differ in some other essential aspect. This means the Necessary Being is 'composed' (murakkab) of a common element and a differentiating element—undermining their denial of composition.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
تركيب
لازم عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers' denial of composition is self-evident.
Correction: Ghazali shows it is not self-evident; proof is required, and their distinction between genus and necessary consequence does not automatically prove impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the first approach Ghazali uses against the philosophers?
A) He argues that the First and first effect are both pure intellects
B) He demands they prove the impossibility of composition
C) He cites revelation to show God has attributes
D) He denies the existence of the first effect

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's initial strategy is to require the philosophers to produce evidence for their claim, rather than accepting it at face value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about God simply because it was widely held or sounded reasonable, without demanding the same level of proof Ghazali requires here? What might change if you refused to accept unproven claims about the divine?

Ghazali's Obligation: The First and First Effect as Pure Intellects
Ghazali presents his second approach (ilzām) showing that the First is composed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own premises to show that the First Being must be composed of a shared essence and a differentiator.

Core Idea
If both are pure intellects, they share an essence—so composition is unavoidable.

Ghazali now shifts from demanding proof to obliging the philosophers to accept a conclusion from their own framework. He points out that both the First and the first effect are described as 'pure intellects.' If they share this essential reality, and yet are distinct, then each must have a differentiating factor—this directly implies composition, contradicting their denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common premise
Both the First and the first effect are called 'pure intellect' (ʿaql mujarrad). This is a key premise that the philosophers accept.
The first effect in their cosmology is the first created intellect.
2
Analyze the implication
If both share the same essential reality (intellectuality), then they share in a 'genuine essential matter' (amr jawharī dhātī). This is a generic commonality.
This sharing is not a mere necessary consequence but a true genus.
3
Follow the conclusion
Since they are two distinct entities, they must differ in some other essential matter. This makes the First composed of a common element and a differentiating element.
Thus, the philosophers' own description forces composition onto the First.

Refuting the Simplicity of God's Existence
Ghazali begins refuting the claim that God's existence is pure without essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's initial refutation of the claim that God's existence is pure without essence, and his assertion that God has a real essence that is existent.

Core Idea
God has a real essence that is existent, not that He is existence alone.

The philosophers claim that God's existence is simple, meaning He has no quiddity or essence—He is pure existence. Ghazali challenges this by first demanding proof, then arguing that God does have a reality and quiddity, but that reality is existent. This passage sets up a critical debate on the relationship between essence and existence in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the philosophers' claim and Ghazali's response. Notice the structure: the philosophers' argument, Ghazali's demand for proof, his counter-assertion, and their rejoinder.
The passage is incomplete; the final objection from the philosophers cuts off.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the philosophers' position: God's existence is simple, no quiddity. Identify Ghazali's position: God has a reality and quiddity that is existent.
3
Examine Ghazali's first approach
Ghazali's first approach is demanding proof. He then counters the philosophers' reasoning by accusing them of obfuscating the term 'necessary existence', and he affirms that God has a real essence.
He does not deny essence; he says it is existent.
4
Consider the incomplete argument
The passage ends with the philosophers' rejoinder that the quiddity would cause existence. Reflect on why this matters and what Ghazali's full response might be.
This shows the debate is ongoing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦ - مسألة في إبطال قولهم إن وجود الأول بسيط . أى هو وجود محض ، ولا ماهية ولا حقيقة يضاف الوجود إليها ، بل الوجود الواجب له ، كالماهية لغيره قال الغزالي : ولنا معهم مسلكان : المسلك الأول : المطالبة بالدليل ، فإن قالوا : لأنه لو كانت له ماهية ، لكان الوجود مضافاً إليها ... فيكون الوجود الواجب معلولا وهو تناقض قيل لهم : هذا رجوع إلى منبع التلبيس فى إطلاق لفظ الوجود الواجب ؛ فإنا نقول : له حقيقة وما هية ، وتلك الحقيقة موجودة ، أي ليست معدومة فإن قال الفلاسفة : فتكون الماهية سبباً للوجود ، فيكون الوجود معلولا :
Question 16: Concerning the refutation of their statement that the existence of the First is simple. That is, it is pure existence, and there is no quiddity (māhīyah) or reality to which existence is attributed, rather necessary existence is His, like quiddity for others. Al-Ghazali said: We have two approaches with them: The first approach is the demand for proof. For if they say: Because if He had a quiddity, then existence would be attributed to it... so necessary existence would be an effect, and that is a contradiction. It is said to them: This is a return to the source of obfuscation in applying the term 'necessary existence'; for we say: He has a reality and a quiddity, and that reality is existent, i.e., it is not nonexistent. Then if the philosophers say: Then the quiddity would be a cause of existence, so existence would be an effect:
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God's existence is simple (basīṭ), meaning pure existence without any quiddity or essence to which existence is added. They argue that for God, existence itself is His reality, unlike other beings where quiddity is separate. Ghazali outlines two approaches: first, demanding proof. The philosophers' argument, as presented, is that if God had a quiddity, existence would be attributed to it, making necessary existence an effect (ma'lūl), which is contradictory. Ghazali retorts that this argument is obfuscation in the use of the term 'necessary existence'. He affirms that God does have a reality and quiddity, but that reality is existent, not non-existent. Then the philosophers object that if He has a quiddity, that quiddity would be a cause of existence, making existence an effect. The passage cuts off at this point, leaving Ghazali's full response incomplete within this window.

Ghazali's Twofold Refutation of Composition in the Necessary Being
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim with two approaches: demanding proof and obligating argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two-fold refutation of the philosophers' claim that participation and distinction imply composition in the Necessary Being, and how he uses both a demand for proof and an obligating argument.

Core Idea
Participation and distinction do not automatically imply impossibility; Ghazali demands proof and turns the tables.

The philosophers argue that if the Necessary Being shares an attribute with something else and differs in another, that would imply composition, which they consider impossible for God. Ghazali challenges this on two fronts: first by questioning how they know such composition is impossible, and second by constructing an argument that, on their own principles, the Necessary Being would indeed be composed. Let's examine his two approaches.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Start by reading the opening line: 'They said: Participation and distinction necessitate composition...' Do you understand why they think composition is impossible for the Necessary Being?
This sets up the problem Ghazali will refute.
2
Identify Ghazali's first approach
Ghazali's first approach is the 'demand for proof' (مطالبة). He asks: 'How do you know the impossibility of this composition?' Notice he refers to his earlier discussion on the increase of attributes.
This is a direct challenge to the philosophers' assumption.
3
Examine the second approach
The second approach is the 'obligating argument' (إلزام). Ghazali argues that both the First and the first effect are described as 'pure intellects', so they share an essential attribute and must differ in something else, implying composition.
This forces the philosophers to either accept composition or revise their criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : إن المشاركة والمباينة تقتضى التركيب ، والتركيب في حق الواجب ، محال. قال الغزالى : ولنا معهم مسلكان : المسلك الأول المطالبة ، فنقول لهم ، من أين عرفتم استحالة هذا التركيب ؟ وأحال الغزالي في هذه المسألة إلى ما سبق له في مسألة زيادة الصفات. المسلك الثانى : الإلزام ، فنقول لهم : الأول عقل مجرد ، والمعلول الأول ، عقل مجرد ، وحقيقة كل منهما هي هذه العقلية ، فهما مشتركان فى أمر جوهرى ذاتى ، ولا بد أن يختلفا فى أمر آخر . فالواجب إذن مركب.
They said: Participation and distinction necessitate composition, and composition with respect to the Necessary Being is impossible. Al-Ghazali said: We have with them two approaches: The first approach is the demand (for proof). So we say to them: How do you know the impossibility of this composition? And al-Ghazali referred in this matter to what preceded him in the issue of the increase of attributes. The second approach: the obligating (argument). So we say to them: The First is a pure intellect, and the first effect is a pure intellect, and the reality of each of these is this intellectuality. So they both share in an essential substantial matter, and they must differ in another matter. Therefore, the Necessary Being is composed.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that participation (sharing in an attribute) and distinction (being different in another) imply composition, which they deem impossible for the Necessary Being. Ghazali presents two approaches. First, he demands the proof: How do they know that such composition is impossible? He then refers to his earlier discussion on the increase of attributes. Second, he uses an obligating argument: Both the First (the Necessary Being) and the first effect (the first intellect) are pure intellects; they share in the essential attribute of intellectuality, yet they differ in something else. Therefore, the Necessary Being is composed. This challenges the philosophers' own criteria.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is claiming that the Necessary Being is actually composed and that this is his own theological position.
Correction: Ghazali is not endorsing composition in God; he is using the philosophers' own premises to show that their argument leads to a contradiction. The statement 'the Necessary Being is composed' is a reductio ad absurdum.

Checkpoint

B) Obligating argument
C) Denial of attributes

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers by demanding proof of the impossibility of composition and by showing that their own description of the Necessary Being and the first effect implies composition.

Objection and Answer on Cause and Essence
Ghazali presents an objection and answer regarding cause and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between two meanings of 'cause' (efficient cause vs. necessary condition) and why only the second is acceptable for God's essence and existence.

Core Idea
Between efficient cause and necessary condition, Ghazali safeguards divine simplicity.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a philosophical objection about whether God's essence causes His existence. He unpacks the term 'cause' to show that the only problematic meaning is the efficient cause, which does not apply to God. Instead, a weaker sense—that God is not independent of His essence—is both innocuous and necessary for affirming divine self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: an objection followed by Ghazali's response.
The objection uses an analogy from contingent things to the Eternal.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'cause'
The response distinguishes two possible interpretations of 'cause'. Find them and note how each is treated: one is rejected, the other accepted.
The first is 'efficient cause', the second is 'another aspect' (necessary condition).
3
Understand why Ghazali accepts only the second
Reflect on why the efficient cause interpretation fails (based on contingent analogy) and why the necessary condition interpretation is acceptable and not impossible.
The key is that 'not independent of essence' does not imply causal efficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : إن عنيتم بالسبب الفاعل ، فالماهية في الأشياء الحادثة لا تكون سبباً للوجود ، فكيف فى القديم ؟ وإن عنيتم بالسبب وجهاً آخر ، وهو أنه لا يستغنى عنه ، فليكن كذلك ، ولا استحالة فيه
It was said to them: If you mean by the cause the efficient cause, then the essence in contingent things is not a cause for existence, so how [can it be] in the Eternal? And if you mean by the cause another aspect, namely that He is not independent of it, then let it be so, and there is no impossibility in it.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the relationship between essence and existence in God. The objector argues that if essence were a cause of existence, then in God His essence would cause His existence, which seems problematic. Ghazali responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'cause': the efficient cause (al-sabab al-fāʿil) and the necessary condition (the aspect of not being independent). In contingent things, essence is not an efficient cause of existence. For God, if by 'cause' they mean that He is not independent of His essence, then this is acceptable and not impossible. The nuance: Ghazali is not saying God's essence is an efficient cause of His existence; he is only allowing the weaker sense of essential dependence.

Key Terms
سبب فاعل (efficient cause)
ماهية (essence)
وجود (existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali is accepting that God's essence is an efficient cause of His existence, just like in creatures.
Correction: Ghazali specifically rejects the efficient cause meaning and only accepts a weaker sense: that God is not independent of His essence. This does not imply causal efficiency.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's response, what does allowing the second meaning of 'cause' imply about God?
A) God's essence is an efficient cause of His existence
B) God is not independent of His essence
C) God's essence is separate from His existence
D) There is an impossibility in God's existence

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes two meanings of 'cause' to defend divine simplicity: God's essence is not an efficient cause of His existence, but He is not independent of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to 'explain' God's unity by treating His attributes as separate parts? How does Ghazali's distinction challenge that tendency? Will you stop trying to grasp the Unknowable and simply submit?
?
When you say 'Allah is self-sufficient,' do you truly feel your own complete neediness in return? This lesson shows that even God's existence is not independent of His essence—what does that say about your complete dependence on Him? How will you let that dependence shape your next action?
?
Ghazali distinguishes meanings to protect divine simplicity. Are you willing to hold back your own intellect from forcing explanations where the revelation is silent? Can you leave a question unanswered for the sake of tawheed, without anxiety?

Critiquing Ibn Sina's Proof from Pure Intellect
Ghazali critiques Ibn Sina's first proof for God's knowledge of other things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of Ibn Sina's first argument for God's knowledge of other things, and why the argument fails due to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's proof of God's knowledge is circular: it assumes what it tries to prove.

In this passage, Ghazali examines Ibn Sina's first attempt to prove that God knows things other than Himself. Ibn Sina argues that because God is immaterial, He is pure intellect, and thus all effects are known to Him. Ghazali points out a fatal flaw: the argument only works if 'intellect' already means 'knowing all things', which is exactly the point in dispute. Let's see how this critique unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Note the structure: Ibn Sina's argument followed by Ghazali's response.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'عقل محض' and 'معلولات'.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's premise and conclusion
What is Ibn Sina's starting point? What conclusion does he draw? Trace the logical steps: immaterial ? pure intellect ? knowledge of effects.
3
Locate Ghazali's concession and his critical question
Ghazali first concedes the premise about immateriality. Then he asks: what does 'intellect' mean? Why is this question crucial?
Notice he says 'فهو نفس المطلوب' – that is exactly what is sought.
4
Reflect on circularity
Explain in your own words why Ghazali considers the argument circular. Does it actually prove God's knowledge, or does it assume it?
The argument begs the question: the definition of 'intellect' already includes knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لابن سينا مسلكان في إثبات علم الإله بغيره :
المسلك الأول : أن الأول موجود لا فى مادة ، فهو عقل محض ، وكل ما هو عقل محض فجميع المعلولات منكشفة له
قال الغزالى : قولهم إن الأول موجود لا في المادة . إن كان المعنى به ،
أنه ليس بجسم ولا منطبع في جسم ، فهو مسلم . فيبقى قولكم
:
وما هذه صفته فهو عقل مجرد ، فماذا يعنى بالعقل ؟ إن عنى به
أنه يعقل سائر الأشياء ، فهو نفس المطلوب
Ibn Sina has two paths for proving God's knowledge of others:
The first path: that the First exists not in matter, so He is pure intellect, and everything that is pure intellect, all effects are unveiled to Him.
Al-Ghazali said: Their statement that the First exists not in matter. If what is meant by it is that He is not a body and not impressed in a body, that is conceded. Then remains your statement: And whatever has this description is a pure intellect; so what is meant by 'intellect'? If by it you mean that He intellects all things, then that is exactly what is sought.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's first argument for God's knowledge of things other than Himself, followed by Ghazali's critique. Ibn Sina argues that because God exists without matter, He is pure intellect ('aql maḥḍ). And anything that is pure intellect has all effects (maʿlūlāt) unveiled to it. Therefore, God knows all things. Ghazali's response focuses on the definition of 'intellect'. He first concedes the premise: if by 'exists not in matter' they mean God is neither a body nor imprinted in a body, that is acceptable. But then the argument claims that such a being is a pure intellect. Ghazali asks: what does 'intellect' mean here? If they mean by it that He cognizes all things, then that is the very point they are trying to prove – the argument is circular. The move from immateriality to 'pure intellect' is ambiguous; it does not establish knowledge unless 'intellect' is already taken to mean 'knowing all things'. Thus, the argument fails as a proof.

Key Terms
عقل محض (pure intellect)
مادة (matter)
منطبع (imprinted)
معلولات (effects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is denying that God knows all things.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying God's knowledge; he is critiquing the logical validity of Ibn Sina's argument.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's objection to Ibn Sina's argument that God is pure intellect?
A) God cannot be immaterial
B) The term 'intellect' already assumes what they want to prove
C) God's knowledge is limited to essences
D) The argument relies on a false premise

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that Ibn Sina's first argument for God's knowledge is circular because it assumes knowledge in the definition of 'intellect'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'all-knowing', do you ever treat it as a mere label that you assume to be true without letting it reshape your daily accountability? How would your life change if you genuinely believed that every effect—including your smallest actions—is fully unveiled to Him?
?
Ibn Sina's argument assumed that 'pure intellect' automatically knows everything. Think about your own assumptions about God: Are there ways you have claimed to know something about God (like His will or His attributes) without examining whether your reasoning is circular or based on unclear definitions? What step can you take today to ground your understanding more carefully in revelation?

The First Approach: God as Pure Intellect
Ghazali examines Ibn Sina's first argument for divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of Ibn Sina's first argument for God's knowledge, focusing on the ambiguity of 'intellect' and the incomplete proof.

Core Idea
Calling God 'pure intellect' does not automatically prove His knowledge of others.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles Ibn Sina's first of two approaches to prove that God knows other beings. The argument seems straightforward: God is immaterial, therefore He is pure intellect, and pure intellect knows all effects. But Ghazali presses on the meaning of 'intellect' and whether immateriality alone guarantees knowledge. Let's see how he dismantles the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali first grants a premise then questions the conclusion.
Pay attention to the structure: Ibn Sina's claim followed by Ghazali's response.
2
Identify the core argument
Trace Ibn Sina's reasoning: immaterial → pure intellect → knowledge of all effects. Then spot where Ghazali objects.
The key is the definition of 'intellect'.
3
Analyze Ghazali's objections
Ghazali asks: what does 'intellect' mean? If it means knowledge, the argument is circular. He also questions whether matter is the only obstacle to knowledge.
These two objections form the heart of the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في تعجيز من يرى منهم أن الأول يعلم غيره ، ويعلم الأنواع والأجناس بنوع كلى. لابن سينا مسلكان في إثبات علم الإله بغيره : المسلك الأول : أن الأول موجود لا فى مادة ، فهو عقل محض ، وكل ما هو عقل محض فجميع المعلولات منكشفة له. قال الغزالى : قولهم إن الأول موجود لا في المادة . إن كان المعنى به ، أنه ليس بجسم ولا منطبع في جسم ، فهو مسلم . فيبقى قولكم : وما هذه صفته فهو عقل مجرد ، فماذا يعنى بالعقل ؟ إن عنى به أنه يعقل سائر الأشياء ، فهو نفس المطلوب. فإن قالت الفلاسفة : إن المانع من إدراك الأشياء المادة ، ولا مادة. قيل : نسلم أنها مانع ، ولا نسلم أنها المانع فقط.
Issue concerning incapacitating those among them who hold that the First knows other than Himself, and knows the species and genera in a universal manner. Ibn Sina has two approaches in proving the knowledge of the Divine of other than Himself: The first approach: that the First exists not in matter, so He is pure intellect (ʿaql maḥḍ), and everything that is pure intellect has all effects disclosed to it. Al-Ghazali said: Their statement that the First exists not in matter. If what is meant by it is that He is not a body nor imprinted in a body, then it is granted. So your statement remains: 'And whoever has this description is a pure intellect.' So what is meant by intellect? If what is meant by it is that He knows all things, then that is precisely what is sought. If the philosophers say: The obstacle to perceiving things is matter, and He has no matter. It is said: We grant that it is an obstacle, but we do not grant that it is the only obstacle.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage deals with the first of Ibn Sina's two approaches to prove God's knowledge of others. The argument: because God is immaterial, He is pure intellect, and pure intellect knows all effects. Ghazali grants that God is not a body or material, but then asks: what does 'intellect' mean? If it means He knows things, that is just the claim being made. If it means something else, we need clarification. The philosophers then add that matter is the obstacle to knowledge, and since God has no matter, He knows. Ghazali responds: we agree matter is an obstacle, but it may not be the only obstacle. So the proof is incomplete.

Key Terms
عقل محض
مادة
معلولات
منكشفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies that God is pure intellect.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny that God is pure intellect; he questions whether calling Him 'pure intellect' automatically proves He knows all things. He also does not accept that immateriality alone removes all obstacles to knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot be pure intellect
B) The term 'intellect' is ambiguous and the proof is circular
C) Matter is not an obstacle to knowledge
D) The First knows only universals, not particulars

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's critique shows that the term 'intellect' must be precisely defined, and immateriality alone does not guarantee knowledge of all things.

In your own study of Islamic theology, are you quick to accept definitions without questioning their implications? Can you pinpoint one attribute of Allah that you have not thought about critically? What will you do to gain a deeper, more precise understanding?
?
The philosophers assumed that matter is the only obstacle to knowledge. Have you ever made similar assumptions about Allah—thinking, 'Since Allah is unlike X, surely He must have Y'? How can you guard yourself against such unwarranted extrapolations in your faith? Commit to one concrete action to train yourself in rigorous theological thinking starting today.

Ghazali's Two Objections to the Agency Argument
Ghazali refutes Ibn Sina's argument that God knows the world because He is its agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two objections to Ibn Sina's argument that God knows the world because He is its agent.

Core Idea
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' own view of God's agency undermines their proof for His knowledge.

Ibn Sina argues that since the world is God's act, He must know it. Ghazali responds with two incisive objections. The first challenges the type of agency, the second limits what knowledge would follow. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument and refutation.
Notice the structure: Ibn Sina's claim, then Ghazali's two objections.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's argument
Ibn Sina claims that the world is God's act, and an agent knows his effect. So God must know the world. Grasp this premise before seeing how Ghazali attacks it.
3
Examine the first objection
Ghazali distinguishes between volitional and natural agency. Knowledge follows only from volitional acts. Since the philosophers consider God a natural agent, the argument fails.
This objection directly targets the philosophers’ own view that God acts by nature.
4
Examine the second objection
Even if we grant that an agent knows his effect, only one thing emanates from God, so only knowledge of that one thing is necessitated, not knowledge of all things.
This limits the scope of any inferred knowledge.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ghazali is not denying God's knowledge; he is showing that the philosophers' specific argument does not prove it. This is a logical refutation, not a theological denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثانى فى إثبات علم الواجب بغيره عند ابن سينا : أن العالم فعل الله ، والفاعل يدرى مفعوله. قال الغزالي : لنا في رد هذا وجهان : الوجه الأول : أن الفعل إن كان إراديا ، لزم علم الفاعل بمفعوله ، وإن كان طبيعيا لم يلزم ، والله عندكم فاعل بالطبع لا بالإرادة ، فلم يازم علمه بفعله. قال ابن سينا : إن صدور العالم عن الله سببه علمه به ، فلابد أن يكون الله عالماً بالعالم . قال الغزالي : إن غيرك يا بن سينا يخالفك فى هذا الرأى ، ويرى أن العالم صدر عن الله لا من حيث علمه به ، فما المحيل لهذا الرأى ؟ الوجه الثاني : هو أنه إن سلم لهم أن صدور الشيء عن الفاعل يقتضى علم الفاعل بالصادر عنه ، فالصادر عن الله شيء واحد فقط ، فلا يلزم إلا العلم به.
The second approach in proving the knowledge of the Necessary (al-wājib) of other than Himself according to Ibn Sina: that the world is the act of God, and the agent knows his effect. Al-Ghazali said: We have two ways of refuting this: The first way: that if the act is volitional (irādī), then the agent's knowledge of his effect follows, but if it is natural (ṭabīʿī), it does not follow. According to you, God is an agent by nature, not by will, so His knowledge of His act does not follow. Ibn Sina said: The emanation of the world from God is due to His knowledge of it, so God must be knowing of the world. Al-Ghazali said: Someone else, O Ibn Sina, disagrees with you on this opinion, and holds that the world emanated from God not from the aspect of His knowledge of it; so what prevents this opinion? The second way: Even if we grant them that the emanation of something from the agent necessitates the agent's knowledge of that which emanates, what emanates from God is only one thing, so only knowledge of it would be necessitated.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's second argument for God's knowledge: the world is God's act, and an agent knows his effect. Ghazali gives two objections. First, knowledge follows only if the act is volitional. But according to the philosophers, God acts by nature, not will, so the analogy fails. Ibn Sina counter-argues that the world emanates because of God's knowledge, so knowledge is necessary. Ghazali replies that other philosophers disagree that the emanation is due to knowledge; they see it as a natural emanation without knowledge. So Ibn Sina's claim is not proven. Second, even if we accept that an agent knows his effect, the world is a single effect (the first intellect or something), so we only get knowledge of that one thing, not of all things. Thus, the argument does not prove full divine knowledge.

Key Terms
فعل (act)
فاعل (agent)
إرادي (volitional)
طبيعي (natural)
صدور (emanation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is denying that God knows the world.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying God's knowledge; he is showing that the philosophers' argument fails to prove it.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Ghazali's first objection to the second approach?
A) God cannot be an agent
B) Knowledge only follows volitional acts, not natural ones
C) The world is not an act
D) The agent does not know his effect

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's critique does not reject God's knowledge but undermines the philosophers' specific proof from agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a philosophical argument for a belief about Allah, only to later find it weak? How does Ghazali's method of refuting arguments while affirming the truth challenge you to be both critical and firm in your faith? What specific step will you take this week to ground your belief in Allah's knowledge on revelation rather than mere reasoning?
?
The philosophers assumed God acts by nature, not will. Do you ever act as if God is distant or mechanical, forgetting His personal will and care for you? How can you remind yourself daily that Allah's actions are volitional and full of wisdom? Commit to reciting and reflecting on a verse about Allah's will (e.g., Al-Baqarah 2:117) each morning for a week.

Ghazali's Response: Matter as Obstacle, Not Exclusive
Ghazali responds to philosophers' claim that matter is the only obstacle to perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's method of refuting the philosophers' claim that matter is the sole obstacle to perception, by conceding part of their premise while denying its exclusivity.

Core Idea
Ghazali concedes matter is an obstacle but denies it is the only one.

In this brief exchange, Ghazali engages with the philosophers' argument that matter prevents perception. While he agrees that matter can be an obstacle, he challenges the claim that it is the exclusive blocker. This illustrates a key dialectical strategy: partial concession can invalidate a conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the structure: a claim followed by a response.
2
Identify each part
Find where the philosophers' claim ends and where Ghazali's response begins. Note the wording of the concession and the denial.
The response begins with 'qīla' (it is said).
3
Understand the logical move
Ghazali concedes that matter is an obstacle (a partial agreement) but denies that it is the only obstacle. This blocks the conclusion that a lack of matter guarantees perception.
The crux is the word 'only' (faqaṭ).
4
Reflect on broader significance
Think about why this refutation matters for divine attributes: God's perception is not limited by matter, but other factors could also impede perception. Ghazali preserves divine transcendence without conceding the philosophers' full argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالت الفلاسفة : إن المانع من إدراك الأشياء المادة ، ولا مادة قيل : نسلم أنها مانع ، ولا نسلم أنها المانع فقط .
For if the philosophers say: 'The obstacle to perceiving things is matter, and there is no matter.' It is said: 'We concede that it is an obstacle, but we do not concede that it is the only obstacle.'
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents a brief objection-and-answer exchange. The philosophers argue that matter is the impediment to perception, and since God is immaterial, He should perceive all things. Ghazali responds by conceding that matter is an obstacle but denying that it is the exclusive obstacle; thus the inference is invalid. The passage is a concise refutation of a specific premise within a larger argument.

Key Terms
مانع (obstacle)
إدراك (perception)
مادة (matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali denies matter as an obstacle entirely.
Correction: He only denies that it is the exclusive obstacle, not that it is an obstacle at all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Ghazali respond to the philosophers' claim that matter is the only obstacle to perception?
A) He denies that matter is an obstacle at all.
B) He concedes matter is an obstacle but denies it is the only one.
C) He argues that God cannot perceive anything.
D) He accepts the claim without objection.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's refutation shows that conceding a premise does not require conceding its exclusivity, a key dialectical skill.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'matter' in your life do you assume is the only barrier between you and spiritual clarity? What other obstacles—pride, distraction, attachment—might you be ignoring?
?
When you encounter a compelling argument, do you accept it wholesale or do you question whether it hinges on an exclusive premise? How can you train yourself to see hidden assumptions?
?
Think of a time you conceded a point in a debate but felt you had more to say. Are you willing to distinguish between what is true and what is the whole truth, even when it is easier to stay silent?

Ghazali's Two Objections to Ibn Sina's Proof
Ghazali refutes Ibn Sina's proof that God knows the world as His effect with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two key objections to Ibn Sina's proof that God's knowledge of the world follows from being its agent.

Core Idea
Agency does not automatically imply knowledge unless the act is volitional.

Ibn Sina argues that since the world is God's act, God must know it. But is that necessarily true? Ghazali challenges this by distinguishing between volitional and natural agency. In this passage, he presents two incisive counterarguments that expose the weakness of the philosophers' reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the dialectical exchange between Ghazali and Ibn Sina.
Notice how Ghazali uses 'if ... then' reasoning.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage contains two distinct objections. Find each one and understand how Ghazali structures them. The first relies on the nature of agency; the second on what actually emanates.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: does Ghazali deny that God knows the world? Or does he only deny that this particular proof is valid? This connects to the broader debate about divine attributes.
The common misunderstanding touches on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثانى فى إثبات علم الواجب بغيره عند ابن سينا : أن العالم فعل الله ، والفاعل يدرى مفعوله قال الغزالي : لنا في رد هذا وجهان : الوجه الأول : أن الفعل إن كان إراديا ، لزم علم الفاعل بمفعوله ، وإن كان طبيعيا لم يلزم ، والله عندكم فاعل بالطبع لا بالإرادة ، فلم يلزم علمه بفعله قال ابن سينا : إن صدور العالم عن الله سببه علمه به ، فلابد أن يكون الله عالماً بالعالم . قال الغزالي : إن غيرك يا بن سينا يخالفك فى هذا الرأى ، ويرى أن العالم صدر عن الله لا من حيث علمه به ، فما المحيل لهذا الرأى ؟ الوجه الثاني : هو أنه إن سلم لهم أن صدور الشيء عن الفاعل يقتضى علم الفاعل بالصادر عنه ، فالصادر عن الله شيء واحد فقط ، فلا يلزم إلا العلم به
The second method for proving the knowledge of the Necessary Being of other-than-Him according to Ibn Sina: that the world is the act of God, and the agent knows his effect. Al-Ghazali said: We have two points in refuting this: The first point: that if the act is volitional, the agent's knowledge of his effect follows; but if it is natural, it does not follow; and in your view, God is an agent by nature, not by will, so His knowledge of His act does not follow. Ibn Sina said: The emanation of the world from God is caused by His knowledge of it, so God must be knowing of the world. Al-Ghazali said: Someone other than you, O Ibn Sina, disagrees with this view, and holds that the world emanated from God not by virtue of His knowledge of it; so what prevents this view? The second point: that if we concede to them that the emanation of a thing from an agent necessitates the agent's knowledge of the emanated thing, then what emanates from God is only one thing, so only knowledge of that one thing is necessitated.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Ibn Sina's second proof for God's knowledge of others. Ibn Sina argues that since the world is God's act, the agent must know his effect. Ghazali presents two counterarguments. First, he distinguishes between volitional and natural agency: if the act is volitional, knowledge of the effect is necessary, but if it is natural (as the philosophers claim God's emanation is necessary and without will), then knowledge does not follow. Ibn Sina interjects that the world emanates from God because of His knowledge of it, but Ghazali rebuts that other philosophers (possibly referring to the ancients) disagree and see no impossibility in emanation without such knowledge. Second, even if agency necessitates knowledge of the effect, only one thing emanates from God (the First Intellect), so only knowledge of that one thing is proven, not of all things. The passage is highly argumentative and follows a dialectical style.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
فاعل (agent)
إرادة (will)
طبع (nature)
صدور (emanation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali completely denies any knowledge of the world for God.
Correction: Ghazali only denies that the philosophers' arguments prove that knowledge; he does not deny divine knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ghazali's first objection to Ibn Sina's second method?
A) God's act must be volitional for knowledge to follow.
B) God's act is natural, so knowledge does not follow.
C) The world emanates from God's ignorance.
D) Ibn Sina misunderstood Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' own assumptions about God's necessary emanation undermine their proof that He knows the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge of your actions, do you unconsciously imagine it as similar to a human knowing what he is doing? After reading Ghazali's distinction, how might your assumption differ from the philosophers' view? Will you let this lesson reshape how you conceive of divine knowledge, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
Ghazali argues that if God acts by nature rather than by will, then His knowledge of the effect does not necessarily follow. In your own life, do you ever treat God as a mechanical cause—assuming His actions are predictable based on certain inputs—rather than as a personal, volitional being? What would change in your relationship with Him if you fully embraced that His agency is utterly transcendent and not bound by human categories?
