Ghazali's Incapacitation: Proving God's Self-Knowledge
Ghazali argues that based on their own principles, philosophers cannot even prove God knows Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own denial of will and necessity to undermine their ability to prove even God's self-knowledge.

Core Idea
If God's emanation is necessary, self-knowledge is not required.

After dismantling their arguments for God's knowledge of particulars, Ghazali now strikes at a more fundamental claim: that God knows Himself at all. He shows that given the philosophers' own premises—denying will and origination, positing necessary emanation—they have no basis to assert that God knows Himself. This passage is a compact dialectical exchange that reveals a deep inconsistency in their system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, argumentative style.
2
Identify the philosophers' premises
Ghazali mentions two key premises denied by the philosophers: will (al-irāda) and origination (al-ījād). They instead claim emanation is by necessity (ḍarūra) and nature (ṭab‘). Why does this matter?
3
Understand the objection and response
The philosophers object that a being without self-knowledge would be dead. Ghazali cleverly replies: we don't claim that, but your principles logically lead to the possibility. Focus on his dialectical strategy.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why is this incapacitation effective? How does it shift the burden of proof? What does it reveal about the philosophers' overall position?
Connect to the broader critique in the Tahāfut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٠ - مسألة في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أنه يعرف ذاته أيضاً قال الغزالي : ما دام الفلاسفة قد نفوا عن الله الإرادة، والإحداث، وزعموا أن ما يصدر عنه يصدر على سبيل الضرورة والطبع فأى بعد فى أن يكون ما هذا شأنه لا يعلم نفسه ؟ فإن قالت الفلاسفة : من لا يعلم نفسه ، فهو ميت، قيل لهم : نحن لا ندعى عليكم ذلك ، ولكنه يلزمكم على أصول مذهبكم
Issue 20: On incapacitating them from establishing a proof that He knows His own self as well. Al-Ghazali said: Since the philosophers have denied will and origination (al-ījād) to God, and claimed that what emanates from Him emanates by necessity and nature, then what improbability is there that such a being does not know Himself? For if the philosophers say: One who does not know himself is dead. It is said to them: We do not claim that about you (i.e., that God is dead), but it is necessitated upon you according to the principles of your school.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a new issue (mas'ala) in which Ghazali seeks to show that the philosophers cannot even prove that God knows His own self, given their own premises. He reminds that they have denied God will and creatorial origination (al-ījād), asserting that everything emanates from Him by necessity and nature. On such a view, why could it not be that such a being does not know Himself? The philosophers object that anyone who does not know himself is dead (implying an absurdity). Ghazali responds cleverly: they are not claiming God is dead, but rather that their own principles logically entail that God (as a necessary natural emanation) might not know Himself, even if that is not their stated belief. Thus the burden is on them to show otherwise from their own premises. The exchange follows a typical dialectical pattern: Ghazali forces an implication from their own doctrines.

Key Terms
تعجيز (incapacitation)
إرادة (will)
إحداث (origination)
ضرورة (necessity)
طبع (nature)
علم النفس (self-knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is claiming that God is dead.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says 'We do not claim that about you.' He is only showing that the philosophers' principles do not rule it out, shifting the burden of proof.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise Ghazali uses to challenge the philosophers' ability to prove God's self-knowledge?
A) God must be volitional to have self-knowledge.
B) The philosophers deny will and origination, and claim emanation is necessary.
C) The philosophers believe God is dead.
D) Self-knowledge requires a material body.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' own principles do not guarantee God's self-knowledge, thereby incapacitating them from proving it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What premises have you unconsciously adopted about God that, if taken to their logical conclusion, might conflict with what you believe about His attributes? Will you examine them today?
?
The philosophers assumed that their understanding of emanation was sufficient. Are there aspects of your faith where you rely on assumptions rather than the revealed text? What would it cost you to question those assumptions?
?
Ghazali shows that an opponent's own principles can be used to undercut their position. Think of a belief you hold strongly about God: can you actually defend it from your own most basic principles, or are you just repeating what you've been taught? If you can't, what are you willing to do about it?

The Philosophers' Claim and Its Threat to Religion
Ghazali summarizes the philosophers' position and its religious implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God cannot know changing particulars and why Ghazali considers this view destructive to religious law.

Core Idea
Denying God's knowledge of particulars undermines the entire religious law.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a dangerous philosophical claim: that God only knows fixed realities, not changing particulars. He summarizes their reasoning and immediately warns that this view uproots the very foundation of religion. Read on to see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' doctrine
Read the first part where Ghazali summarizes the philosophers' view: reality changes, knowledge of changing things changes, change is impossible for God.
2
Identify the consequence
Notice the marginal note from Ibn Sina's al-Ishārāt and Ghazali's conclusion: this view implies God does not know particular acts like Zayd's obedience or disobedience.
This is a devastating religious implication.
3
Reflect on the condemnation
Ghazali says this principle 'uproots the religious laws entirely.' Ask yourself: why would lack of knowledge of human actions destroy the basis of reward and punishment?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۱ - مسألة في إبطال قولهم : إن الله - تعالى عن قولهم - لا يعلم الجزئيات المنقسمة بانقسام الزمان : إلى الكائن ، وما كان ، وما يكون وخلاصة مذهبهم في ذلك : أن الواقع متغير ، والعلم بالشيء المتغير متغير ، والتغير على الله محال . . . لهذا كان علمه بالواقع لا من حيث إنه متغير ، بل من جهة ثباته واستقراره نصوص من كتاب الإشارات لابن سينا « هامش » قال الغزالي : هذه قاعدة استأصلوا بها الشرائع بالكلية ؛ إذ مضمونها أن زيداً مثلا لو أطاع الله تعالى أو عصاه ، لم يكن تعالى عالماً بذلك ؛ إذ هى حوادث جزئية متغيرة قال الغزالي : ويعترض عليهم من وجهين : الوجه الأول : أن يقال : ما المانع من أن يكون الله تعالى علم واحد بالشيء يشمل أحواله الماضية ، والراهنة ، والمستقبلة ، وبذلك يكون الله عالماً بالشيء في جميع أحواله ، من غير أن تتوارد عليه أغيار
Issue 21: On refuting their statement that God—exalted above their words—does not know particulars divided by the division of time: into what is, what was, and what will be. And the summary of their doctrine in that: that reality is changing, and knowledge of a changing thing is changing, and change is impossible for God. For this reason, His knowledge of reality is not insofar as it is changing, but from the aspect of its fixity and stability. Texts from the Book of Pointers (al-Ishārāt) by Ibn Sina — margin. Al-Ghazali said: This is a principle by which they have uprooted the religious laws entirely; for its implication is that Zayd, for example, if he obeyed God or disobeyed Him, God would not be knowing of that, because these are particular, changing events. Al-Ghazali said: And he objects to them from two aspects: The first aspect: that it is said: What prevents that God has a single knowledge of a thing that encompasses its past, present, and future states, and thereby God is knowing of the thing in all its states, without successive changes coming upon Him?
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a new issue (mas'ala) aimed at refuting the philosophers' claim that God does not know changing particulars. Ghazali first summarizes their doctrine: reality changes, knowledge of a changing thing changes, and change is impossible for God; thus God knows reality only in its fixed and stable aspect. He then quotes a marginal note from Ibn Sina's al-Ishārāt. Ghazali condemns this view as uprooting the entire religious law (sharā'i'), because it implies God does not know particular human actions like obedience or disobedience. He then announces objections from two aspects, but only the first is given here: why can God not have a single, encompassing knowledge that includes all states (past, present, future) without undergoing change? This objection directly counters the philosophers' premise that knowledge of changing things must itself change. The passage is a powerful critique showing the theological consequences of the philosophers' view and offering a positive alternative.

Key Terms
جزئيات (particulars)
تغير (change)
شرائع (religious laws)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali claim that the philosophers' view 'uproots the religious laws'?
A) Because it denies God's existence.
B) Because it implies God does not know particular acts of obedience or disobedience.
C) Because it says God changes.
D) Because it denies prophecy.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that God knows only fixed realities, not changing particulars, leads to the devastating conclusion that God does not know human actions, destroying the basis of religious law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God did not know your private sins, would you still feel accountable? How does Ghazali's warning against the philosophers' view make you reconsider the seriousness of any doubt about God's knowledge of your actions?

?
The philosophers claimed God knows only unchanging aspects. Have you ever acted as if God only knows the broad strokes of your life but not the details? What must you change to live with the conviction that Allah knows every particular of your obedience and disobedience?

Ghazali presents the first of two objections to the philosophers' view

Learning Objective

Core Idea
God's knowledge can encompass past, present, and future without changing.

After exposing the dangerous implications of the philosophers' view, Ghazali offers his first objection. He asks: why can't God have a single, unchanging knowledge that includes all states of a thing? This objection directly challenges the assumption that knowledge of change must itself change.

Guided Path

2
Analyze the logic

This is a key move in the debate.
3
Consider the implication

We will see their response later.

The Philosophers' Denial of Divine Knowledge of Particulars
Ghazali states the philosophers' doctrine and declares its uprooting of divine law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argued that the philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of changing particulars uproots the foundations of divine law.

Core Idea
If God cannot know changing deeds, reward and punishment become meaningless.

Ghazali begins his refutation by stating the philosophers' doctrine clearly, then immediately draws its devastating consequence for religion. This passage exposes the practical stakes behind a seemingly abstract theological debate. Understanding this argument is key to seeing why kalam scholars insisted on God's knowledge of temporal particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim and reasoning
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the three premises: reality is changing, knowledge of a changing thing changes, change is impossible for God.
Notice the ellipsis in the source; it marks a pause in the reasoning.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the argument: from the nature of reality to the nature of knowledge to the impossibility of change in God. See how they conclude that God knows only the fixed aspect.
3
Grasp Ghazali's conclusion
Ghazali says this principle 'uproots the Sharia entirely.' Consider the example of Zayd's obedience or disobedience: if God does not know that particular event, how can He reward or punish?
This shows the practical implication of the philosophical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢١ - مسألة في إبطال قولهم : إن الله - تعالى عن قولهم - لا يعلم الجزئيات المنقسمة بانقسام الزمان : إلى الكائن ، وما كان ، وما يكون

وخلاصة مذهبهم في ذلك : أن الواقع متغير ، والعلم بالشيء المتغير متغير ، والتغير على الله محال . . . لهذا كان علمه بالواقع لا من حيث إنه متغير ، بل من جهة ثباته واستقراره

21st Problem: On Refuting Their Saying that God – exalted above their saying – does not know the particulars divided by the division of time: into what is (present), what was (past), and what will be (future). The summary of their doctrine in this is that reality is changing, and knowledge of a changing thing is changing, and change is impossible for God. Therefore, His knowledge of reality is not insofar as it is changing, but in terms of its fixity and stability. Al-Ghazali said: This is a principle by which they uproot the divine laws entirely; for its implication is that if Zayd, for example, obeyed God or disobeyed Him, God would not be knowledgeable of that, because they are particular changing events.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage defines and criticizes the philosophers' position on God's knowledge of temporal particulars. It begins by stating the topic: refuting their claim that God does not know particulars divided by time (past, present, future). The philosophers' summary holds that reality (al-wāqiʿ) is constantly changing, knowledge of a changing thing would itself be changing, but change (taghayyur) is impossible for God. Hence God's knowledge of reality pertains only to its stable, fixed aspect – the universal, not the changing particular. Ghazali then draws the devastating consequence: this principle uproots the entire Sharīʿa. If Zayd obeys or disobeys, God would not know that particular action, so reward and punishment become meaningless. Thus the philosophers' view, logically carried out, negates the very foundation of revealed law.

Correction: Ghazali shows that their view effectively denies divine awareness of individual temporal actions, which undermines the basis for reward and punishment in the Sharia.

Ghazali's First Objection: A Single Knowledge for All Temporal States
Ghazali presents his first objection: a single knowledge can cover all temporal states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection against the philosophers concerning God's knowledge of particulars, and how he proposes a single timeless knowledge to avoid change in God.

Core Idea
One timeless knowledge can cover all temporal states without change in God.

This passage presents the first of Ghazali's two objections against the philosophers. He challenges their claim that God cannot know particulars without implying multiplicity or change. Instead, Ghazali asks: why not a single knowledge that encompasses past, present, and future? This lesson unpacks that question and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali introduces two aspects, then gives the first.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the rhetorical question.
2
Identify the core objection
What is Ghazali questioning? He asks: what prevents God from having a single knowledge that covers all states of a thing? This is the heart of the objection.
3
Examine the proposed solution
Ghazali proposes that one knowledge (ʿilm wāḥid) can encompass past, present, and future without implying multiple knowledges or change in God.
This preserves God's knowledge of particulars without the problem of multiplicity.
4
Connect to the philosophers' goal
Ghazali notes that the philosophers' aim is to negate change (taghayyur) in God. His proposal meets that goal, so the objection challenges the necessity of denying particular knowledge.
Both sides agree that God does not change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي : ويعترض عليهم من وجهين :

الوجه الأول : أن يقال : ما المانع من أن يكون الله تعالى علم واحد بالشيء يشمل أحواله الماضية ، والراهنة ، والمستقبلة ، وبذلك يكون الله عالماً بالشيء في جميع أحواله ، من غير أن تتوارد عليه أغيار

قال الغزالي : إن غرض الفلاسفة نفى التغير عن الله ، وهو أمر متفق عليه
Al-Ghazali said: And he objects to them from two aspects. The first aspect: that it may be said: What prevents God Almighty from having one single knowledge of a thing that encompasses its past, present, and future states, and thereby God is knowledgeable of the thing in all its states, without new knowledges succeeding upon Him? Al-Ghazali said: The purpose of the philosophers is to negate change from God, and that is an agreed-upon matter.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's first objection against the philosophers. He structures it as one of two aspects. The objection asks: why can't God have a single, undivided knowledge of a particular thing that covers all its temporal modes – past, present, future? Such a knowledge would not be multiple or successive; it would be one timeless act of knowing encompassing all states. This would avoid any change or succession (taghayyur) in God. Ghazali then adds a clarifying remark: the philosophers' goal is to deny change in God, and this is a point both sides have agreed upon. The objection thus challenges the necessity of the philosophers' conclusion: perhaps their aim (negating change) can be met without denying God's knowledge of particulars, if that knowledge is a single, all-inclusive act.

Key Terms
علم واحد (ʿilm wāḥid)
أغيار (aghyār)
تغير (taghayyur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is proposing that God has multiple knowledges corresponding to each temporal state.
Correction: On the contrary, Ghazali proposes a single knowledge (ʿilm wāḥid) that encompasses all states without multiplicity.

Checkpoint
What specific proposal does Ghazali make in his first objection to the philosophers?
A) That God has multiple knowledges corresponding to each change.
B) That God can have one single knowledge encompassing all past, present, and future states of a thing.
C) That God does not need to know particulars at all.
D) That change in God is necessary for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first objection proposes that a single unchanging knowledge can encompass all temporal states, preserving God's knowledge of particulars without implying change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the idea that God's single knowledge encompasses your past, present, and future in one timeless act challenge your tendency to worry about the unknown or regret the past? What specific step can you take this week to live with greater trust in His comprehensive awareness?
?
If God's knowledge never changes, how does that affect your view of His forgiveness? Does it make you more or less motivated to repent, knowing that He already knew your sin before you committed it? Commit today to a specific change in your repentance practice.
?
Ghazali argues that God's knowledge of all your states is one, without change. Does your daily life reflect a belief that God's plan for you is complete and wise, or do you act as if God's knowledge is fragmented? Identify one area of your life where you will submit more fully to His single, all-encompassing will.

Ghazali's Second Objection: Temporal Knowledge in God
Ghazali presents his second objection citing Jahm and Karramiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second objection to the philosophers, in which he proposes that temporal knowledges (ʿulūm ḥāditha) could succeed in God's essence as a result of His knowledge of changing particulars.

Core Idea
Ghazali uses the views of Jahm and Karramiyya to question the philosophers' rejection of temporal knowledge in God.

Ghazali now raises a second objection directly against the philosophers' position that no change can occur in God. He points out that even the philosophers accept an eternal world subject to change, so why not allow temporal knowledge in God? He cites two earlier theological groups who held such views, forcing the philosophers to either reject them (which would be inconsistent with their own principles) or accept the possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection carefully. Notice how Ghazali frames it as 'What prevents according to your principle?'
Ghazali is using the philosophers' own principles against them.
2
Identify the cited groups
Note the positions of Jahm (who held God's knowledges of events are created) and the Karramiyya (who believed God is a locus for accidents). Understand why they were criticized by others.
These groups are mentioned as precedents for temporal knowledge in God.
3
Analyze the inconsistency
Ghazali argues that the philosophers cannot consistently reject temporal knowledge in God while accepting change in the eternal world. Trace this reasoning.
This is a key point of Ghazali's dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن يقال : ما المانع على أصلكم أن تتوارد عليه علوم نتيجة علمه بالحوادث الجزئية المتغيرة ؟ كما ذهب ( جهم ) إلى علومه بالحوادث حادثة ، وكما اعتقد الكرامية أنه محل للحوادث ولم ينكر عليهم ذلك إلا من جهة أن ما يلحقه التغير حادث . وأما أنتم فمذهبكم أن العالم قديم ، وإن كان لا يخلو عن التغير .

فإن قالت الفلاسفة : إنما أحلنا قيام العلم الحادث بذاته ؛ لأن العلم الحادث فى ذاته لا يخلو إما أن يصدر من جهته أو من جهة غيره لا جائز أن يصدر من جهته ؛ لأنه لا يصدر من القديم حادث ، على ما بان في المسألة الأولى ولا جائز أن يصدر من جهة غيره ؛ فإنه يؤدى إلى أن يكون غيره مؤثراً فيه :

قال الغزالي : كل واحد من القسمين غير محال على أصلكم أما أنه لا يصدر من القديم حادث ، فقد أبطلناه في المسألة الأولى العلم فيه من غيره ، فلا يستحيل أيضاً على هذا وأما صدور أصولكم
The second aspect: that it may be said: What prevents, according to your principle, that knowledges succeed upon Him as a result of His knowledge of changing particular events? As Jahm held that His knowledges of events are temporal, and as the Karramiyya believed that He is a locus for events, and they were not denied except from the aspect that what is subject to change is temporal. But as for you, your doctrine is that the world is eternal, even though it is not free from change. If the philosophers say: We have deemed impossible the subsistence of temporal knowledge in His essence, because the temporal knowledge in His essence is either proceeding from Him or from another. It is not possible that it proceeds from Him, because nothing temporal proceeds from the eternal, as was established in the first problem. And it is not possible that it proceeds from another, for that would lead to another being an agent (muʾaththir) upon Him. Al-Ghazali said: Each of the two divisions is not impossible according to your principles. As for its not proceeding from the eternal, we have already refuted that in the first problem. As for its proceeding from another, that is also not impossible according to this... and the emanation of your principles...
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a longer argument comprising Ghazali's second objection, the philosophers' response, and the beginning of Ghazali's counter-refutation. Ghazali first poses the second objection: why cannot temporal knowledges (ʿulūm ḥāditha) succeed upon God as a result of His knowledge of changing particulars? He cites the positions of Jahm ibn Ṣafwān (who held God's knowledges of events are created) and the Karramiyya (who held God is a locus for accidents) as precedents. The only objection to them was that whatever undergoes change is temporal – but the philosophers themselves hold that the world is eternal yet subject to change, so their own position is inconsistent. The philosophers then respond with two arguments: temporal knowledge in God cannot proceed from God Himself (because nothing temporal comes from the eternal) nor from another (because that would make another an agent influencing God). Ghazali begins his refutation by asserting that both alternatives are not impossible on the philosophers' own principles. The first (nothing temporal from the eternal) he claims to have already refuted in the first problem (likely the earlier discussion on the eternity of the world). The second (knowledge from another) is also not impossible, though the text breaks off mid-sentence. Ghazali seems to be arguing that the philosophers' own principles do not rule out these possibilities.

Key Terms
علم حادث (ʿilm ḥādiṯ)
جهم (Jahm)
كرامية (Karrāmiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali himself believes that God has temporal knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali is using this as a reductio argument against the philosophers; he does not affirm temporal knowledge as his own view.

Checkpoint
Which two groups does Ghazali cite as holding that God has temporal knowledge of events?
A) Jahm and the Karramiyya
B) The Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya
C) The philosophers and the Sufis
D) The Zaydis and the Imamis

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second objection challenges the philosophers to explain why temporal knowledge in God is impossible given their own acceptance of change in an eternal world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah because it seemed to introduce change, while accepting change in your own life without question? What does this say about your understanding of Allah's transcendence?
?
The philosophers held a beautiful system but Ghazali exposed its flaw. Are you willing to let a single inconsistency dismantle a comfortable belief you hold? What would that cost you?
?
Ghazali used the views of others (Jahm, Karrāmiyya) not to endorse them but to argue by their own principles. When you debate, do you seek to understand your opponent's position deeply enough to find its internal contradictions? What pride or fear prevents you from doing so?

The Philosophers' Dilemma: Two Impossible Sources
Philosophers argue temporal knowledge cannot be from God or from another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' response to Ghazali's second objection: that temporal knowledge cannot arise in God because it would have to come either from God Himself (impossible as nothing temporal proceeds from the eternal) or from another (impossible as that would make another an agent upon God).

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that temporal knowledge in God is impossible from either side: from God or from another.

The philosophers now counter Ghazali's proposal with a dilemma. They argue that any temporal knowledge subsisting in God's essence must have a source: either it proceeds from God Himself or from something external. Both options are deemed impossible on their principles. This lesson examines each horn of the dilemma and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' response
Read the Arabic and translation where the philosophers present their dilemma. Identify the two horns: knowledge from God or from another.
2
Understand the first horn
Why can't temporal knowledge proceed from God? Because the philosophers hold that nothing temporal (ḥādith) can proceed from the eternal (qadīm). This was 'established' in the first problem (the eternity of the world).
This is a key premise in the philosophers' system.
3
Understand the second horn
Why can't temporal knowledge proceed from another? Because that would mean another being is an agent (muʾaththir) upon God, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.
This would imply dependence on another.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Note that the philosophers present these as exhaustive and impossible alternatives, thereby rejecting Ghazali's proposal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن يقال : ما المانع على أصلكم أن تتوارد عليه علوم نتيجة علمه بالحوادث الجزئية المتغيرة ؟ كما ذهب ( جهم ) إلى علومه بالحوادث حادثة ، وكما اعتقد الكرامية أنه محل للحوادث ولم ينكر عليهم ذلك إلا من جهة أن ما يلحقه التغير حادث . وأما أنتم فمذهبكم أن العالم قديم ، وإن كان لا يخلو عن التغير .

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can't temporal knowledge proceed from God Himself?
A) Because God does not know temporal things.

Ghazali's Refutation: Both Horns Are Not Impossible
Ghazali begins to refute the philosophers' dilemma by denying each impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's initial refutation of the philosophers' dilemma: he argues that neither horn is impossible on the philosophers' own principles, and he begins to explain why.

Core Idea
Ghazali denies the impossibility of both horns, stating that temporal knowledge from God or from another is not ruled out.

Having presented the philosophers' dilemma, Ghazali now launches his counter-refutation. He asserts that each horn of the dilemma is not impossible according to the philosophers' own principles. He first addresses the claim that nothing temporal can come from the eternal—arguing that this was refuted in the first problem. Then he turns to the second horn, stating that knowledge proceeding from another is also not impossible. However, the text cuts off mid-sentence, leaving the argument incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's refutation
Read the Arabic and translation where Ghazali responds. He begins by stating that each division is not impossible on the philosophers' principles.
Note the phrase 'كل واحد من القسمين غير محال على أصلكم'.
2
Analyze the first counter-argument
Ghazali says that the claim 'nothing temporal from the eternal' has already been refuted in the first problem. This refers to his earlier arguments against the philosophers' proof of the eternity of the world.
He is not accepting their premise; he is calling it invalid.
3
Consider the second counter-argument
Ghazali then says the second horn (knowledge from another) is also not impossible. However, the text breaks off. What might his argument be? Possibly that the philosophers' own principles allow external influence?
The sentence ends with 'And the emanation of your principles...' leaving the thought unfinished.
